The Red Page
Clnquainanity
Thousands of cinquains, or worse, have
poured in upon this page. It looks so easy
to do ! Five little lines that needn’t even
rime! Yet, what the majority of cinquain-
ists forgot, those five little lines must hold
poetry, or humor, or emotion, or thought.
The cinquain must be packed tight: into
that tiny space so much must be crammed!
The evil influence of the American example
quoted on this page permeates this competi-
tion. Hundreds of cinquains are merely
echoes of that poor and uninspiriting effort.
Yet here and there, as will be seen from the
examples quoted, that commonplace idea of
flowers fading, of leaves falling, of night
coming, is touched with fantasy or expressed
with felicity.
The question of rime has been raised by
several competitors; and a number of cin-
quains possess that added grace. Rime
should certainly be allowed in the cinquain,
though, as will be seen, some of the finest
cinquains submitted do not need the help of
rime. Thomas V. Tierney offers us a varia-
tion of the form, which he terms the cin-
quainette, though the name cinquade would
be more appropriate. It consists of three
cinquains, and contains five rimes, each line
of the second and third verses riming with
the corresponding line of the first verse. His
example:—
THE PARTING.
Dearest,
The time draws nigh
When I must join toy mates;
Beside the dock the transport stands,
Adieu!
Clearest —
Its note on high—
The bugle rings; who waits
When all his help that call demands?
Ah, who?
Nearest
My heart shall lie
Your image, and the Pates
May bring- me safe, from distant lands,
To you.
A number of competitors make a long poem
with each stanza a cinquain. “Bellyband,”
M.B. and “Elizabeth 65” prove that the
five-line stanza has possibilities, though none
quite merits quotation. R.G.S. offers a
pleasant cinquain-twin :
THE MOTHER.
To-night,
The wind outside,
Shrieks through the timorous trees
Of anguished souls, with sobbing breath,
Who die.
But yet,
This firelit room
With loved familiar things,
Speaks to my heart of peace—and you
With God.
Naturally, the cinquain lends itself to humor.
The sting in the tail is so often ironic : the
ballet-dancer, pirouetting, ludicrously falls.
Hebe is an assortment of canned cinquain
humor: —-
Her dress
Came home. Her hub.
Was shocked to see the length
That she had gone. It barely reached
Her knees!
Graham Mar.
THE MOURNER.
He said:
“It makes me sad,
In some sharp, sudden fight,
To see it reckless spilt.”... .He spoke
Of beer!
Peter Faux.
ANY WIPE.
Dry-eyed,
She broods and waits
The homing of her lord ;
Nor doubts that, ere the dawn, he’ll come—
Shicker’d.
Peterkin.
Good beer
In pewters many,
Conflicting views with Mick;
Intrusive Bung and Hop’s coat torn
—One month! „ ,
E. B. A.
THE STRIKER’S SON.
Softly
Crooning baby
Tunes, nurse brings him his son.
Gently he lifts the shawl and finds
Him Black 1
0. P. K.
This page specified that cinquains had to
be in iambic metre, though allowing slight
irregularities when they improved the mean-
ing. But a number of cinquainists deliber-
ately disregarded this advice, and the excel-
lent cinquains of Millie Burrows and L. FoU
are cast out; as also for other technical
faults are those of R.G.S. and Henry Tate.
Though “Eadith Walker” breaks all the
metric rules, the absurdity of her cinquain is
sufficient excuse for quotation : —•
A dark,
Well-kept secret,
Which means loss of temper—
For no one knows where the matches
Are kept.
The war has its cinquains, too: —
ACTION FRONT.
Listen !
Silence that cries,
Night slit with searching stars ;-
Then, as a beast roars, suddenly
The guns! ,
M. H.
THE SURPRISE ATTACK.
Beware!
The still night air
Echoes the littlest sound-;
And softly ’long the line is breathed:
“Don’t sneeze!”
C. V. Jervis.
It comes.
High in the air
We hear the warning note;
Then bolt like Hell and wait the crash.
A dud! _ ' n
Block 10.
The following einquains are selected for
their daintiness, their truth, their power to
evoke emotion, even for their apt and neat
compression of a common-place thought, or
the age-old human touch. A personal pref-
erence is perhaps the chief criterion of this
page’s judgment: the cinquain is so delicate
a thing, an ephemeron poised in its moment
and caught ere it disappears, that it must
make its appeal instantaneously or not at
all. i
Caprice!
How durst thou come
In Love’s disguise and touch
A heart that waits for Love —and then
Depart ?
E. H. Wills.
MALLEE RAIN.
Raindrops
On Mallee leaves
Like polished silver stars
Will cling to mem’ry longer than
The drought.
Sweet word,
Soft spoken, sad.
How oft has love, grown cold,
Re-kindled at the whisper of
“Goo'd-bye!”
Roy R. Rede.
Softly
The scented dusk,
A- velvet curtain falls,
And holds us willing prisoners
To Love.
Gwen Henderson.
FRIENDS.
Silence!
A weird calm !
Then plaintive soothing cries.
And lonely bushmen bless the old
Curlew.
Heather Murray.
’Tis dusk,
And filmy veils
Invest the transient world
That slowly fades to nothingness
And night.
G. B.
DROUGHT.
Storm-black,
The clouds swoop down
Upon a withered earth;
And, treasure-filled, pause tauntingly,
And pass.
May Dexter.
SEPTEMBER.
Shell-white
The petals fall
From sun-kissed orchard trees
Soft as the sighs from hearts that wait....
And wait.
H. W. Stewart.
The sky,
A burnished plain
Of sunset light—-the moan
Of winds —the crescent moon—and I,
Alone.
Mari Marina.
The way
To live, to love —-
Teach me this magic craft,
For, oh, Life flows so fast, and Love
Dies soon.
Mari Marina.
In youth
When pleasures crowd
Each sorrow breaks the heart:
But sorrow breaks monotony
In age.
G. M. Johnston.
HILDER.
Turquoise
And green, and gold;
The moods of sea and sky;
And fleeting Beauty’s soul is here
Entrapped.
Olga M. Parker.
MY PRAYERS.
The sun,
Blue sea and sky,
A laugh, the scent of trees,
Kind hearts —these only, yet I have
Great faith.
M. S. Wallace.
HAPPINESS.
When I
Was young I held
.. The blue bird in my hands.
As I grew old he crept into
Mv heart.
Allan Denny.
Tumult;
About my heart;
Grief drowning speech with tears
Unshed. My eyes are bright, and smile.
Goodbye!
Black Bonnet.-

Freda E. Hamilton. Clifton, Mentone, Mel-
bourne, wins—by a nose.
“Somewhere”
In that long lane
Of crosses crude he lies;
And sleeping here at my full breasts
His son.

Will Donald has written and pictured an
Australian alphabet, chiefly for kiddies, en-
titled Kids’ Cannon, the proceeds for the
War Chest. Ais for the Anzac, and Z for
the Zep. The pictures are better than the
verses—or, if you like to put it that way, the
pictures aren’t as bad as the verses. Price 2s.
The French Revolution
Timely, is the translation of the brilliant
and authoritative National History of
France, written by a group of the greatest
French historians under the general editor-
ship of Fr. Funck-Brentano, and now pub-
lished in six volumes by Heinemann, and
sold, in complete sets only, by Angus and
Robertson, Sydney. Each of these volumes
is about 600 pages in length, and the periods
covered are: the Middle Ages, the Renais-
sance, the Great Century, the 18th Century,
the Revolution and the Consulate and the
Empire. So far, however, only three
volumes hk-e been published. The set costs
£3 out here. _ '
Especially interesting is the volume by
Louis Madelin, upon The French Revolu-
tion, since from this brilliant study of the
greatest drama in history we gain a stand-
point of comparison of the similar drama
now happening in the Republic of Russia.
It is at least probable that the two revolu-
tions will run approximately the same
course; and the student of history may
already, with the help of this book, discern
points at which the later drama plagiarises
the former. Louis XVI., like the Czar
supposed to be a despot, was really the chief
slave of a system which he might dis-
approve of but could not modify. The more
complete the French Revolution became the
more evident were the dissatisfactions it
evoked; and already we see that tendency
in troubled Russia. The Russian soldier’s
revolt against his officer is but a reminiscence
of the destruction of all army discipline in
France in 1790: at Nancy three regi-
ments threw their officers into prison. And
the foolish hesitation of Hindenburg to
attack Russia in her first agonies of revolu-
tion is but a repetition of the craftily wait-
ing attitude of Europe while ITrance worked
out her salvation. The amiable delusion of
France’s enemies was that France was about
to disappear as a factor in the European
problem. Like Russia she was to b§ allowed
to stew in her own juice—and the chief in-
gredient of that juice was named Napoleon!
So far history has not divulged the name
of Russia’s Napoleon—that military genius
who will appear to weld this distracted
anarchy into a conquering power; but
Russia' has a habit of producing military
geniuses. But even a revolutionary army,
in which the t volunteers elected their own
commanding officers, as the French army
did, was able to beat the Prussian. And
surely we are entitled to look for the ulti-
mate advent in Russia of “the superior man,
above all his contemporaries, and perhaps
above his century,” as Katherine 11. of
Russia wrote in 1794, predicting Napoleon.
The time will come in Russia, as it came
in France, when Russia must once again be
governed by a single ruler. “Revolutions
always end with the sword.” Though after
the sword there may be the Rock. And
after the Rock the stable Republic.
&
During the last 20 years an avalanche of
documentary testimony has overwhelmed
the historians of France ; and thus the time
is ready for a re-writing of the drama of
the Revolution. And this Louis Madelin
has brilliantly and dramatically done. The
author considers that the Revolution was
not against despotism but against inequality :
its nature was far more social than political.
The majority of. the nation dreaded revolu-
tion. “Once rid of the feudal rights and the
crushing weight of the taxes, a good three-
quarters of them would have been content.”
The author, however, gives full weight to
the part played by the bandits, the criminal
and I.W.W. element of the Paris of those
days. These battened on revolutions; they
had nothing to lose. “And the whole coun-
try was in the grip of a sort of national
hysteria, which historians will never be able
to explain.” The taking of the Bastille, a
bogey fortress, was due to the mere aimless
longing of the mob for something to destroy.
Madelin calls it a contemptible business.
The storm began to growl; and, swept on
by forces that they could not control, leaders
momentarily emerged, flew for a mad instant
before the wind, and fell. Of these remark-
able, figures the author gives vivid portraits.
Madame Roland “was one of the human ele-
ments in the Revolutionary drama: she in-
spired it, led it, diverted its course, till she
herself. Jay crushed beneath its ruins She
was womanly to her fingertips, fanciful, pas-
sionate, exaggerated. . . . Her view was always
obscured by her affections or her dislikes.”
In the editor’s eyes Danton towered high
above them all. “He was horribly sincere;
everything came from the depths of his
heart—pity, rage, love, ferocity. Rancor
and calculation were alike foreign to him.
He had a great brain and a warm heart.
.... He was a volcano, but above the hor-
rible-flood of lava played the purest flame.”
He was a greater man than Robespierre.
There, too, was Marat, violent to the verge
of insanity, quarrelsome, cynical, cunning,
a Mediterranean half-breed, deliberately sor-
did and evil-smelling, with black locks escap-
ing from the bandana handkerchief soaked
with vinegar, worn to alleviate his almost
unendurable headaches —the People’s Friend.
Robespierre, who sent Danton to the guil-
lotine, and soon followed him, was a “vir-
tuous” man, upright, chaste, moral, dreading
money, and most of all dreading women. “He
was to pursue them always with a special
hatred; he it was who did most to bring
Mme. Roland to the scaffold Even his
own sister Charlotte was coldly thrust out
of his existence. And, hating women as he
did, he despised anyone who loved them.
For the first time in history sentimental and
laughter-loving France was ruled by a sworn
enemy of women and of laughter.” A wow-
ser we would have called him. An elegant
youth, with of course the famous light green
eyes behind blue spectacles, the perfection of
neatness, clean-shaved, his hair curled and
powdered, this sans-culotte thought too much
of his appearance to sacrifice his silk
breeches. He guillotined all the friends of
his childhood and his youth. His three dog-
mas were: the support of virtue by terror,
the existence of the Supreme Being, and the
absolute sanctity of property. The trouble
about cutting off people’s heads in order to
make them good is that you can never cut
off quite enough. As Robespierre’s batches
grew bigger and bigger each day, the number
of those who knew their heads were des-
tined for to-morrow’s basket proportionally
increased. At last there were so many
under suspicion that desperation drove them
to consider the possibility, of “shortening”
Robespierre himself. If they failed the re-
sult would only be the same to themselves.
So, as the author dramatically describes,
they got him.
Another interesting figure is that creature
of beauty and grace, Therezia Cabarrus,
formerly a marquise, then the wife of Citi-
zen Tallien, ultimately to become Princesse
de Chimay. She was beautiful and seduc-
tive beyond all expression. After the reign
of Robespierre the woman hater, it seems to
Madelin right that the new regime should find
its incarnation in this fascinating woman of
easy morals, generally believed to be common
property.
Under the Directory it was Therezia Tal-
lien who set the fashions. “All through four
years of the blackest misery her famous red
coach passed up and down, the only one in
Paris that escaped insult. She bestowed
royal alms upon the populace: she never
shut herself up in the Luxembourg: she
showed herself half-naked in the public places*
of the city.” She was worthy to queen a
society when the women wore hardly any
clothes at all —the celebrated sans-cliemises,
or the sauvage costume, the arms and bosom
bare, with a gauze skirt and flesh-colored
drawers beneath it. It was this society that
passed the law which gave authority to hus-
band or wife to divorce on the ground of
absence for six months, no exception being
made in the case of soldiers absent on active
service. Thus a soldier might come home
from Italy, covered with glory, and find a
new husband installed in his home.
For the greatest personality of all France
was waiting, the artisans soured by false
hopes* the middle-class grown rich who
wanted to be safe, the peasants who wanted
the revolution consolidated: a personal ruler
was required to ensure stability. The coun-
try was weary. Not even victories roused
it now. There was a general indolence, a
general indifference.
J*
So the tremendous drama rushed to its
climax. “To the very last the French Revo-
lution was driven forward by a kind of
Fatum. Bonaparte no more knew what he
was going to do on the 19th, Brumaire than
the men who took the Bastille on the morn-
ing of July 14, the deputies who upset the
throne, or those who set up the revolt.” It
was Lucien Bonaparte who made his brother
supreme—though history must give equal
credit to Napoleon’s pimples. In his frenzy
of impatience he had torn the pimples, with
winch his face was covered, with his finger-
nails. “The blood gave color to the story that
the deputies had attacked and wounded him
‘with daggers’: the troops were infuriated by
the thought that these lawyers had dared to
lift their hands against the victor of Areola.”
Napoleon’s bleeding pimples and Lucien Bona-
part’s melodramatic acting convinced the
soldiers; and the drums began to beat the
march that would lead him to the throne.
•
The Ascanian, published at sea, is another
troopship magazine. After an editorial it
begins well with Church News, and with
humor and verse, and even an Answers to
Correspondents column and an Art Supple-
ment, it no doubt contributed to the amuse-
ment of those on board. Most of the writers,
as usual, are bards —and pretty bad bards.
Another interesting war-publication is
The Harcfield Park Boomerang, issued
monthly by the patients of No. 1 Australian
Army Hospital, Harefield Park, Middlesex,
England, where many wounded and sick
Billjims are keeping up their spirits in exile.
A contributor, writing from a sick-bed, ap-
peals to Bulletin artists to send them
drawings for this magazine. It is a cheery
little rag. genuinely Australian in sentiment
and humor. Its most .popular illustration
is doubtless a photograph of a dairy herd in
distant N. S. Wales. There, is a list or
Anzac graves, too, 29 Anzacs in the old
parish churchyard of Harefield, and a Head-
stone fund, for which the pathetic appeal is
made to provide those lonely dead with
marble memorials.

Write for Illustrated Literature
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spring Sunshine.
I do not want to be “a brain” to-day, ‘‘a
soul,” nor any fine progressive thing.
I only want a warm slope of the earth
To smell the sun-hot herbs and suck their
worth,
To see the midges in their circling play, and
know that there is Summer following
Spring.
I do not want a speech on Woman’s Rights,
the rhetoric of labored feminist,
My duty shown me in a stuffy hall,
With rows of benches gaping at a wall.
I want free blue-air morns and star-white
nights. Not to be reasoned with, but
only kist!
I don’t want economics proved to me, nor
matriarchy and its missioning.
I want to put my mouth against a flower
And drink of folly for a jesting hour
Abubble like the young sap in the tree, in
the compelling, ruthless lust of Spring.
Nature is cruel —and she always was! If I
drink deep to-day, to-morrow’s thirst
Shall send me, parch-mouthed, to another
stream.
To-day I seek no reason in a dream —-
A truer dream than reason my heart has.
Come, Phoebus, from the sky and do
your worst!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Motherhood: The First Phase.
When whirled the shining dust of stars
unborn—
A little spinning eddy ’twixt the palms
Of the least angel of creativeness —
A beauty brooding in the mind of God
Beat like a heart. Pleasure and painfulness
Fluttered together, and a sanctity
Pain-pointed, exquisite and glittering,
Was set about them as a crown of thorns.
And silver were those thorns, and diamond
tears
Were slipp’d amid them, and the drops of
blood
Were rubies, poignant, rich with agony.
A wreath of precious sorrow holding joy
So stirr’d the motherhood that was to be,
Conceived within the tenderness of God.
A pregnant silence held the primal world.
A silence cramm’d with growth and the
uncurl
Of frond, and the out-thrust of petal-lip.
The overwhelming forest, midnight wombed
Thickened with rope on rope of sappy life,
Inlacing, till its dome of blossom broke
Sun hungry to the sum a hundred feet
Above the fecund ferment at the roots.
The tepid river, darkened at midday,
Lisped through the labyrinth of mighty ferns,
And not a gnat dance<J on the water floor.
Only the leaping pour of the cascade.
Born boiling from the lips of uncooled rock,
Where yawning bloody-throated in the steam
Gnat flower-gullets gaped from mottled
leaves,
Made clamor in the dumb, growth-burdened
world.
The pregnant seed-bulb swelled, and pod, and
sac,
And honey-headed stamen did their part,
A ceaseless, silent stir of parenthood.
But unemotioned was the blending act,
And painless the fertility, and buds
Clove mindless to a mindless mother-stem.
u.s.a.
DULCIE DEAMER.
The Tie.
My pulse is cold ; my heart gives not a thrill
To see her as she lies.
The cover, twisted by her loose-flung arm,
Showing her snowy neck, her bosom warm,
Heaving with sleepy sighs.
Her full, red lips just quiver with her
breath ;
Her lashes shade her cheek ;
Rut recently my heart had been a-fire
With the strong passion of my heart’s desire
That now is grown so weak !
To touch her hand ; to, trembling, let my lips
Meet hers that said she loved,
Set blood a-stir with passion wild and warm.
But now the full possession of her charm
Leaves me unstirred, unmoved.
And yet I love her —not as once I did ;
But, in the cold control
Of all my senses, one warm thought holds -
sway ;
She gave herself to me. . . .and so I stay,
For that gift bought my soul.
n.s.w.
HAMER.
To a Great Coquette.
They tell me that you are a flirt.
Ah, cruel woman, thus to hurt
A soul so tender. Why not be
A flirt to me?
They say you laugh at Cupid’s darts,
Make light of breaking loving hearts;
Yet all neglected I must pine—
You don’t break mine !
’Tis whispered that your wondrous eyes
Win men from home and sacred ties.
Make me a victim, sweet, I pray—•
Win me away.
With but a single hair you lead
A Samson? Is it so indeed?
Then leave me not to fret and chafe,
Supremely safe.
They talk of cold men passing by,
Stilettoed by your steel-grey eye.
Ah, callous wretch ! My breast is bared f
Why am I spared?
VICTORIA.
D.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER Id. 1917.
WILHELM.
Wilhelm of Prussia takes the baleful lime-
light of the world's most tragic scene. Any-
body who has nursed the smallest doubt as
to the real authorship of the tempest of
blood that is desolating Europe can now
throw that doubt into the rubbish-tip and
readjust his views in the light of the
most recent revelations. It has, of
course, been particularly hard in Britain
and America, and still harder in countries
like our own, to see Prussian intrigue in
proper focus. The acts of king, president
and governor are in the freer lands really
the acts of the nation ; and we have got so
far away from the idea of personal govern-
ment that it was for long impossible to con-
ceive of the Kaiser as other than the mouth-
piece of a people, or at worst of a powerful
faction. The talk about his personality
was brushed aside. We had heard the >■> ne
sort of talk about Edwaku VII. of England ;
but we knew that the alleged deeds of
Edward were the deeds of his Ministers, and
that the scope and influence of the monarchy
were more limited than even the bare forms
of the British Constitution would suggest.
The thing that was so hard to realise about
Germany was that, while making a big show
in arts and sciences, while electing Socialist
representatives to her Parliaments and ex-
tending the most modern of manufacturing
systems and pushing her commerce in the
most modern way, the country was still gov-
erned by the methods of the Middle Ages
and that a king was still a king for both
good and evil. Despite aeroplanes and
radium and Marx and the universal preva-
lence of the German band, Wilhelm 11. was.
at the outset of the war, as unshackled for
intrigue, aggression and reform as Edward
111. of England; and a heap more so than
Charles 1., whose minor acts of despotism
brought about a revolution. And Wilhelm
used the powers that were his to the full:
and used them in a manner that no publicist
in Europe would have deemed possible half
a century before. It is never a wise thing
to under-estimate an enemy; and Wilhelm
has been seriously under-estimated. The man
who could take up such a part as his and
play his role with such astonishing courage
find skill is a very big man. That he is m
our judgment a very bad man does not take
away from that fact. His prosing and
posing, his painted face, his theatrical pos-
turing and all the rest are things that lie
had in common with a lot of the world-
changers of the past; and if he had been in
the habit of standing on his head to eat
his meals it could not filter tin' simple con-
clusion from the general trend of his policy
that during his whole reign he has been by
far the largest force in European polities,
•and that he made himself that force at a
time when democracy seemed to have gripped
personal monarchy by the throat. Alex-
ander rose with the natural rise of the
Macedonian State at a time when all other
national powers were crumbling; Julius
Cjesar was the outcome of the evolution
find extension of the Roman Republic;
Napoleon was thrown up by the fiery surge
of the French Revolution. Wiliielm alone
of all the chief world-disturbers gained his
grim eminence in defiance of and in opposi-
tion to the whole trend of the times and
the whole thought and feeling of the modern
world. To under-estimate a man like that
is as bad a blunder as to mistake the feel-
ings and intentions of a tiger because he
happens to have strolled into a cabbage-
garden.
But the Kaiser’s bigness in one regard
must not be allowed to interfere with our
view of his pettiness in another. The world’s
mighty disturbers of the past have a place
not only in history but also in the philosophy
of human progress. Alexander, mad with
ambition and plain booze, had yet a purpose
as finely ethical as that of a religious re-
former. He conceived the notion of break-
ing the menace of Asia forever by carrying
into that continent something of what was
finest and most stable in Greek thought and
piactice; and his lightning conquests of 11
years did, in fact, alter the whole shape of
things for the half-dozen centuries that fol-
lowed. Julius (Aesar appears, wdien pro-
perly weighed, to have struck a balance of
iron system and free democracy, which was
the top note in political thinking in his day,
and might, if pursued by his successors, have
done more for the moral uplift of humanity
than all the religious systems. Napoleon,
with vaguer ideals and an occasional lapse
from idealism altogether, had also a purpose
of wide human kindness which by making
France supreme in world-empire would have
also made French thought supreme, to the
raising up of every serf and slave to the
dignity of manhood. In such a succession
Wilhelm looks a poor thing. German civil-
isation had no claims over those of the sur-
rounding nations that could at all correspond
to the thing which Alexander took to Asia.
German system was linked to no dream of
universal citizenship justifying the extension
of power such as Caesar imagined. German
lultur had none of the fine elements of the
French thought of liberty which followed
Napoleon’s banners. Reared in a narrow
dynastic nursery, and thinking of the world
and its States in terms of personal owner-
ship, the Kaiser had no bigger purpose than
to grab something large and rich for himself
and the race over which he directly ruled.
A great ethical conception might conceivably
be worth the outpouring of rivers of blood
and the endless disturbance of lands and
peoples; but the Kaiser’s whole mind was
bent on a vast raid of plunder, which differed
only in magnitude from the wars waged by
the barbarous barons and kinglets of the
Middle Ages. He would, by using any means
and in defiance of every law. smash France
and bring England to her knees, not with
any idea of carrying ultimately something
better and finer to those nations in return
for their immediate suffering, but merely that
Germany might be riclrer and his own power
extended. While he was capable of in some
degree measuring democratic forces and
estimating currents of feeling outside his
own system, he yet thought of Russia as
the private park of his weakling acquaint-
ance •‘Nikky" and even of England as a
disorderly workshop belonging to his relative
George, while France was a mere field run
by co-operative farmers whose title to the
ground was bad ever since the first Revolu-
tion. International law was a doubtful
plaything of Hague Conventions and inter-
national honor a mild sentiment which a
howitzer could blow away. All things were
to the strong; and strength alone was law
at the last. It was exactly the guiding prin-
ciple of brigand gentlemen who rode abroad
dressed like kitchen stoves in the Middle
Ages. Yet it was a strong man who could
take such a view in defiance of all the liberal
thought of the age and with the memories
of kings who had lost their heads at the
hands of democracy making a modern blood-
trail across the page of history. Wilhelm
measured himself against those headless
weaklings and took the chances.
* * ♦ »
But it was the essential clarity of the
Kaiser’s thinking that very nearly scored a
success greater than that of any Alexander
or (Aesar. The building up of force and
the training of the German people to ideal-
ise force was not a particularly difficult busi-
ness in the light of previous Prussian suc-
cesses ; and the building of ships and guns
by private firms fostered the idea among the
manufacturing and industrial classes that
war might be good and profitable business.
But it was not in this direction that Wil-
helm’s real successes were won. It w 7 as in
his estimate of mankind and its advances.
England, France, the TJ.S.A. and a number
of other countries built their ideas of human
progress on the works of their best authors
and thinkers; Wilhelm built his on the
sure knowledge that an enormous chasm
divided the thinker from the great mass of
the people. He knew that to a very large
extent force ruled the world —whether it was
the force in the hip-pocket of an armed
civilian or the boots of the policeman on his
beat. He was sure that the world would
still understand an empire of force better
than anything else. Smaller men with bigger
hearts would have hesitated about allying
themselves with the crude old things which
all men of light and learning condemned.
Wilhelm had no such hesitation. The bed-
rock savage w r as still the dominant factor in
human nature, and fear was still the true
ally of the conqueror. But a show must be
made nf things, and the Prussian desperado
knew' how to keep up that show' better thiiu
most men His pretended intei national
fSdsSps' were only half believed .n : hut
his religious zeal and his encou agemei t ot
apparently progressive movements within
his own empire were taken at face va
Gott was still with him even when he was
scheming with "Nikky” for tke assassina-
tion of England and proposing to walk
roughshod over all the rights of Denmark
The long years of strenuous self-repression
and carefully-rehearsed theatncalism indi-
cate the man of intense purpose. He was
playing for world-empire, and lie brought his
weapons to a fine edge. Those weapons were
the German people and as many people ot
other nations as could be impressed with the
idea that he was something other than lie
was. His spv system and methods of rninoi
intrigue were marvellously successful; ana
the wav in which these were linked up with
commerce and won a smiling recognition
from the importer of every country revea a
genius for detail in the handling of the vast
plot. Other Germans did much; but there
seems no reason to doubt that the controlling
mind was in every instance the Kaiser s.
Force was the master-key and guile the oil
to make it turn in the world’s rusted lock
Then the unexpected occurred. Ihe war
began, and a single hole was knocked in the
vast system that Wilhelm believed to be
perfect. France stood up and fought .as no
nation of men had ever fought in all lustoij.
The world believed in force in its inmost
heart—perhaps, as the Kaiser had all along
assumed, it believed in nothing else. But the
world saw force matched with torce; anti
the smaller-seeming power linked with all of
noble and fair that it had ever dreamed.
Wilhelm reeled back from that first blow
and never wholly recovered. But it was not
the end, nor is the end yet in sight. Through
the cannon smoke and the world-murdei the
man who, of deliberate purpose, elected to be
the arch-traitor of civilisation still looms
tremendous. To under-estimate him now is
as dangerous as it was four years ago. On d-
isation is fighting a Prussian system, ant
more. It is fighting a man who is capable,
as your large man alw T ays is, of snatching
victory from the dregs of defeat. II index-
burg and Michaelis, Tirpitz and Holl-
weg count for something; but the hand
that holds the sword is that of the Kaiser
himself. There is no safety for the nations
and the children of the nations while such a
man lives. The thing that he has done can
be repeated. In its advance the world —that
fine part of the world that counts, but a few
thousands, and does all the sincere thinking
—must leave the boots of a countless host
far behind it; and it must always be open to
a man without half the Kaiser s daring to
follow the example he has set and organise
the laggards for an attack on the rear of the
marching column of mind. There is the
supreme danger for all the future from
which only a hangman’s rope well applied
can save humanity.
CAN LABOR FIND ITS ENEMY?
Years ago workers in England and some
other countries combined as trades unionists
, i i * n
and put up a scattered but determined light
. 1 , m, , h „
for tuppence a day. The tuppence was the
• *.
main consideration, as it stood directly and
unmistakably for the workers’ breakfast-
a minor luxury which had for long been
practically denied to them. When, however,
the fight for better wages and conditions had
continued in a spluttering and uncertain
fashion for some time, theorists got busy
about the meaning of the movement, and it
was understood that Labor was fighting
Capital. Just what Capital was like and
what sort of force he had behind him was a
matter about which nobody was very clear,
The owner of the works or the boss of the
mine was known; but it was understood
that he was merely one of the lieutenants ot
Capital, and the elusive animal itself on
which Labor made war was never dis-
covered. It was evident that the banks
existed by lavor ot Capital and that cash
waUed him about in splendor on every side;
but never even in the thickest of the battle
as . a . tthmpse caught of his countenance.
Statisticians produced endless columns ot
figures professing to explain him ; but the
figures only deepened the mystery, lo the
actual fighter in the Labor ranks Capital
appeared as something in a belltopper from
whom by truculence or guile an infinite mini-
her of tuppences per day could be drawn
And while things remained in this doubtful
condition it was understood that both Capi-
tal and Labor had come to this country and
were continuing their old-world fight across
the new continent. In the full Australian
sunshine and the wide, open spaces it seemed
inevitable that Capital would have to dis-
cover himself and show his face plainly in
the battle-in fact, there was a great and
growing hope that Labor might actually cor-
ner him, get him down and take away all
the tuppences he had in his bulging pockets.
But the fight went on and the central figure
round which it raged was still only a name.
Politicians, intoxicated with their own
wordy outpourings, scanned the horizon
and declared that thev saw Capital
flying away. That the great bird in bell-
topper and frock coat should spread its
wings and depart, leaving Labor without the
desirable tuppence of its latest demand.
seemed to them the last calamity that could
overtake the worker; but the evidences of
actual and visible cash indicated that in
spite of many alleged flights Capital was
with us still, though it contrived to hide its
face and inner purpose as cleverly as ever,
* * * *
The involved and uncertain fight wobbled
on. Labor became a political force; the
people were told to become class-conscious
and get the enemy under their feet. They
became class-conscious: the toilers reduced
the quantity and quality of the thing they
exchanged for their bread-and-butter: Capi-
tal appeared to be getting it in the neck
through countless strikes. So hard was
the fight and so bitter the spirit in which it
was urged that at last the foremost ranks of
Labor broke turough the very centre of the
enemy s formation and came face to face
with the arch-monster himself—Capital,
arrayed not in belltopper and spats, but
wearing plain bowyangs and looking as if
ho needed more dinner! There and
then the old fight should have come
to an end; but the nature of the
warfare was such that most of the com
batants were too blind with anger to pro-
perly understand the position. Those in the
rear ranks did not see and refused to believe
until they had seen; and only a few 0 f
those who were right up against the central
danger itself would admit the truth of their
own vision. So the fight goes on; and
Capital—the thing that yielded and would
continue to yield tuppences—is bein''
hacked to pieces. In other words, Labor
smashing Labor takes away the only chance
ir has to improve its own position. Capital
can only exist while Labor is prepared to
create it; and the worst blow ever struck
at unionism is the one which aims at
destroying the only basis on which unionism
can carry on.
* * * *
The present strike, which has gripped
X. S. Wales by the throat and flopped over
into other States to extend inconvenience
and public suffering to its utmost limits, has
made these things clear. The Railway
workers strike, and another union in a spirit
of loud, brotherly sympathy declares the
food of the first strikers black. Another
union and another rallies to the battle-call,
each holding up out of sheer goodwill some-
thing without which the others cannot live.
The machinery rusts, the organisation of
industry breaks up in a way that renders
its repair difficult, insults are hurled about
and angry declamations fill the air; and the
effect of the whole foolish suffering on the
part of the workers is merely to render their
own chance of making a living in the future
more precarious. Getting back to the
statisticians who tried long ago to explain
the nature of Capital by columns of figures,
the real nature of a general strike is seen
sharply enough defined to warn Labor off a
track like that. The whole Capital of new
countries such as Australia is re-made every
five years. Cut out plain toil from a few
weeks of one of those years and add to that
loss the continued disorganisation which
must follow, and it is pretty evident that
Capital’s power to pay out the additional
tuppence whenever demanded is much les-
sened. The fruit of five years’ toil is all
we possess; and by reducing our joint pos-
session even 5 per cent. Labor’s position
must be seriously shaken. There is only
one way in which Capital can be made to
unload from bulging pockets all that Labor
may desire or demand. That way is by
keeping those pockets full to overflowing
with systematic toil. Rut Labor clings still
to the childish fancy that it can draw wages
from some imaginary treasury where a fat
man has stored up wealth and hidden it out
of sight. It is a destroying belief. Labor
holds Capital in the activity of its own
strong hands; and if the hands cease to be
active all the processions and banner-
wavings in the world won't create wealth
out of which wages can be paid.
CHINESE HABITS AND BRITISH
CUSTOMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The houseboat chained to the canal-bank did
not attract my attention particularly. I sat
eating my pocket-tiffin and joyfully watch-
ing the endless stream of traffic being carried
with wind, tide and oar towards Soochow in
the fond illusion that I had left western
civilisation behind me, when a well-groomed
head of fair hair was thrust out of a port-
hole. and I was greeted in the dear, familiar
accents of Australia. I climbed down, in
response to a pressing invitation. He was a
Victorian, and was very lonely out there.
He welcomed me gladly. He formed the
northern barrier of the Chinese Imperial
Maritime Customs. He had just passed
through a trying time. Waves of revolution
had passed to and fro over his head, and the
rebel and republican leaders had both
stretched out hands for the eashbox he held
in trust; but he had sat tight and refused
to pay out to either party until constitu-
tional authority had been re-established;
and, although the odds had been all against
him. no attempt had been made to force the
till.
Just below him was the likiu station, rep-
resentative of that infamous institution
which hangs like a deadly parasite to the
sturdy growth of Chinese trade. It is the
State Frights of China. It stands on the
borders of every district, and holds a pistol
to the head of the merchant, making him dis-
gorge Customs dues which vary according to
the greed and audacity of the local adminis-
trator. Some idea of the quality of this
peculiarly Chinese institution can be gauged
from revenue figures published some dozen
years ago. Out of 15 million taels collected
as likin in one year only one million reached
Peking. A parcel of tea at the end of u s
vicissitudes has been known to pay in taxes
40 times the legal amount. Of the total
native collection of revenue in the provinces
it is estimated that only 30 per cent, to la
per cent, reaches the Imperial Treasury
The possible revenue from land, customs,
salt and tribute under present conditions has
been placed at 400 millions taels. Twenty -
fivo millions actually reach the capital!
Contrast this with the record of the im-
perial Maritime Customs. Hired in the up
rightness of a single British official lin
time of internal weakness and disorder, when
the Government was unable to collect 1
taxes itself, he refused to allow his con
trymen to follow the example of the trader
of other nations and smuggle their goods 1
with impunity), and carried on b.v the gem •
of Sir Robert Hart, it stands a monumei
to British incorruptibility and an examP
to the Chinese of the material and ,n ° ‘
advantages of honesty and exactitude. •
annual statistics stand in sober contrast ,
the wild and wonderful guesses, estinn ■
and subterfuges of the other departure ■
and. except for the recently re-constiti
salt tax, its returns are the only 1
that do not savor of comic opera m
Budget which is solemnly presented to .
nation every twelvemonth by respons
Ministers. Controlled and manned by
ishers to load British and foreign traa
the interests of the Chinese, it_ fo. rnl V‘
better example of what Western cl^ 11S! i 0 i e
at its best must stand for than the w


fabric of British commercial enterprise and
missionary effort. The present highly-
developed posts and telegraphs were an off-
shoot from the 1.M.C., which, amongst its
multitudinous activities, surveys and lights
the coasts, beacons the rivers and provides
the bulk of the funds the Government ex-
pends internally, besides paying the interest
on loans and indemnities.
The canal led to that great artery, the
Grand Canal, and a constant stream of
vessels passed up and down, swinging in to
the Customs station to present their papers
and clearing out again. A “boat-train”
would go by, a sturdy steam-launch towing a
procession of crammed native houseboats
like a string of railway-carriages behind it.
Tide and wind were working together, and
the great mat sails of the cargo boats glided
by wbth quiet and impressive speed. Small,
elegant houseboats carrying officials or well-
to-do merchants and their wives and concu-
bines flitted past, and huge hayricks piled on
punts drifted on the current. Against the
tide went another .swift procession, towed by
ropes from the bank. The pullers, yoked
into a long painter, tugged and panted, rac-
ing around obstacles and clambering over
bridges, for a pittance of a few cash a day.
Along either bank of the canal went an end-
less troop of wheelbarrows bearing astonish-
ing mounds of merchandise and propelled by
sweating coolies, whose plaintive song min-
gled with the squealing of the wheels to
make a weird land-chanty. Behind them
stretched the fiat green crops, pungently fer-
tilised with the nightsoil from the cities. It
was a wonderful picture of the indomitable-
ness of Chinese industry .which each succeed-
ing year, despite war and brigandage, cor-
rupt officialdom and weak central govern-
ment, persists in providing bigger and bigger
trade returns.
Narrow steps led up from the water’s
edge to the gateway of a small, dilapidated
temple, dedicated to an upright and honest
official who lived in the Golden Age before
this era of corruption and provincial
tyranny. There is a current saying that
there will be found but two honest officials
in the whole of Chinese history. One of
these is already dead, the other yet unborn.
Perhaps this was the former. His image,
repainted, still stood behind a broken shrine
on which incense had been freshly burned.
Possibly the fat little man, arrayed in dingy
foreign dress, who sat in front of the likin
office, guarded by soldiers with rifles, would
in the course of years be deified, too. There
was a school in the temple precincts where
a teacher of the modern type presided over
the education of 30 small shaven scholars.
The teacher had an English vocabulary of
portentous fragments, which floated up in
his speech like bits of ornate wreckage from
a torpedoed liner; but beyond these relics of
a college training he was quite incomprehen-
sible and equally unable to understand us.
His modern methods of teaching had deteri-
orated along with his foreign speech. Ilis
pupils had reverted to the time-honored way
of learning the classics. Each was a class
unto himself, and was bawling at the top
of his voice his own lesson. The school-
master walked amidst the tempest un-
harmed, like a demi-god caught in a terres-
trial storm. They played this pleasant game
together from 10 a.m. to 4 p.m., and seemed
to like it. There was no vestige of discip-
line. Each bawled his individual song, or
slept or gaped as inspiration, which Chinese
physiology places in the abdomen, moved
him. The teacher’s wage was 4sd. a week.
Amongst his bulbous-headed brood, in the
old democratic days of competitive /examina-
tion, there might have been a future Minis-
ter of State or a senior-wrangler of the
Empire. In these Republican days one has
to don spectacles and go to America, to come
back fearsomely arrayed in blob-toed shoes
and a Broadway suit, with a mind stuffed
with Western science and Western thought,
a heart secretly ambitious, and a mouth full
of fine phrases culled from John Stuart
Mill. Henry George and Theodore
Roosevelt. One then either drifts into the
revolutionary clubs of the foreign settlements
and uses one’s knowledge of chemistry to
make bombs or is appointed superintendent
to the local likin station, with a squad of
soldiers and a war-junk with a real brass
cannon aboard to guard one from the fury
of the mob. In the latter event one sits
down to apply to Chinese trade superior
Western methods of squeeze and peculation
vastly more profitable and outrageous than
the old.
Before condemning Chinese venality out-
right. it is as well to remember that Europe
only acquired a conscience of recent years.
It is scarcely a hundred years ago that Pitt
began his campaign against a system ot
bribery and corruption so universal and con-
doned that the House of Commons thought
that the discovery of a culprit amongst
its members was a subject fit only for hilari-
ous laughter: just as they laugh to-day
when the Speaker is inadvertently served
with a summons to do national service. The
British Customs Department in the time of
George 11. was not one whit better than
the Chinese likin is to-day. In one year of
that glorious reign £750,000 was _ collected
on wines and tobaccos, of which only
£160.000 reached the Exchequer! A single
article of import might be subjected to more
than a dozen different duties. Corruption is
not peculiar to any one form of govern-
ment. Republican America can be as venal
as despotic Russia. Britain, having, after
shameful days, attained to a purity of ad-
ministration probably unequalled in the
world, has just uncovered an ugly cesspool
which party politics has long been fostering
on the premises. Corruption is a Devil
hard to exorcise. With the Chinese it is a
national habit: but the magnificent and suc-
cessful effort to throw oft the deadlier habit
of opium-smoking shows what potentialities
for spiritual regeneration China has in her.
In their zeal for reform some of the provin-
cial governors burned their opium-smokers.
Perhaps a little burning of provincial gover-
nors may help in depopularising the other
vice. . . . .
Just as I was abandoning my compatriot
1o his loneliness, a cargo-boat made last
alongside, and the laodah, preparatory to
starting negotiations with the master, passed
up a packet of soap to the receptive cook.
He was not adroit enough ; and I started on
my way with such a torrent of Chinese
\ ituperation mixed with good Australian
oaths ringing in my ears as seemed to take
the wind out of all the junk-sails in the land-
scape.
FENG-SHUI.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Food and Clothing; in Germany.
Dear Bulletin,—An Australian girl from
Berlin dropped into our flat here in London
yesterday like an angel ghost. Twenty-seven
months ago, in spite of my expostulations,
she insisted upon throwing in her lot with
her married sister —she has no other relative
—whose husband was then in the German
Embassy in Constantinople. Out of the fur-
nace. but not unscorched ! Her late mother
was almost aggressively English, and Hell
itself could not shake nationality out of
Nancy Robinson, for she is British to the
core and returns as she left. It is untrue, as
reported to The Bulletin in May. 1916.
that she became a naturalised German. If
she had done that she would not be here now.
Her story is full of interest. The whole
time was spent in Berlin. At first there
was no real squeeze for food. Things were
dearer, much on the same scale as in Lon-
don to-day. Gradually the pinch grew tighter,
until absolute necessaries have become
wholly unprocurable save by the plutocrats.
Miss Robinson speaks little German, but
was game to’ stand for hours with irate
housewives in queues to get the family’s
share, per card, of food. The butter limit
was jib. weekly, but of late unobtainable.
Potatoes, like other articles, often sold out
and the queue must disperse, disconsolate.
Wertheims—the Whiteleys of Berlin —
made a brave show of clothing, even of
fashions, but the prices were monstrous and
quality deceptive. Woollen goods of all
kinds —and this is what should interest us
most —are all but impossible to get. Hence
we may be sure that the Boche Army is also
ill supplied for the fierce winter that must
again be faced. Cotton is scarce and dear.
A dearth of all manufacturing materials is
openly declared. All leather, even scraps,
lias long since been requisitioned. Brass
window bolts, door-handles and stove fittings
have been gathered in. Church-bells, though
not all, have been removed for foundry pur-
poses. The populace, though discontented,
is still well under military control. Attempted
meetings in the Potsdamer Platz were
quickly dispersed, the soldiery enclosing all
converging streets, “according to plan.” and
thus localising any disturbance. But. back
of it all. the Imperial family is increasingly
unpopular, both males and females. The
generals are no longer gods and distrust is
scarcely concealed. There are even said to
be meetings of people of the “better classes”
in the swagger Hotel Adlon to discuss radi-
cal changes. In short, the ferment has be-
gun ; but to anyone who knows the Boche
all ferment will be futile until it includes
the sergeant and the sehiitzmann. They
only can put the kybosh on the'Kaiser.—
Yours, Octavius C. Beale.

The Disease and the Microbe.
Three or four dozen men were drafted into
the police courts of Australia last week and
sentenced to six months’ imprisonment for
being members of the I.W.W. This is what
the Law provides, the I.W.W. being an
"unlawful” association. But it is no use
gaoling these men if the doctrines of the
I.W.W. continue at large. Men have been
gaoled ever since prisons came into fashion,
and before that many had their heads lopped,
on account of holding unpleasant opinions ;
yet few religions or theories have perished
because the persons who held them were
locked up or killed. This paper looks upon
the I.W.W. as an imported disease, and is
more concerned about impounding the
microbe than about segregating a certain
number of patients. The real problem
the Legislature is up against in con-
nection with this organisation is not whether
the corporate existence of the I.W.W. is
destroyed by the vigilance of the police, but
whether its doctrines are absorbed by other
brotherhoods. And it is not much good
pretending that they are escaping absorp-
tion. So long as public money is expended
on the promotion of loafing—and millions
have been so spent in Australia of recent
years—and so long as the administration of
the Arbitration system is conducted in such
flagrant defiance of the spirit and letter of
the law as was revealed, for instance, in
the last big X. S. Wales coal strike, the
theories of the I.W.W. can be depended upon
to spread far and wide, even though every
member on the roll be deported beyond the
equator. The theory that there are no
advantages in going slow, and that system-
atic striking is not a payable proposition,
will never be exterminated by public policy
proving the contrary. It is a rotten theory,
disastrous in the end to individuals and to
the State; but he is a painfully-unobserv-
ant citizen who holds the I.W.W. entirely
responsible 'for its growth and acquits the
more sinister system of party government
that has in this country prostituted princi-
ples and the public cashbox for political
support.
The Fools of the Pit.
“Rope Boy” : Some years ago a British
Labor leader, on his return to England after
a tour, scornfully described the miners of
Australia as “damned fools.” Once more
we are proving how accurate his summing-up
was. With better conditions of work, shorter
hours and higher pay than ever coalminers
had before, we have dropped tools and are
idling about while our families are going
short, and the tradespeople are warning us
that no more goods can be supplied without
the money. Why are we idle, and at whose
behest? I’m d —if I know, nor have I met
a fellow-worker who can tell me. We are
told that we gave authority to a committee
to call us out when the proper time arrived,
and that this committee will also order us
back to work when its members think fit.
In the meantime w T e must tighten our belts
and ask no foolish questions. But who is
on this committee that we should give it
greater powers than even Kaiser Bill pos-
sessed? If we could only find out, it’s a
thousand to one that some of these gentle-
men were in the Old Dart when that re-
turned leader gave his candid opinion of the
Australian workers. Yes, he was right! As
long as a man can get on a platform and
blab about “fighting the capitalistic class”—
whatever that may. mean —we are satisfied.
He’s the man for Galway! We are not even
loyal to our own, for, when the opportunity
comes, we turn out good, honest battlers and
proved workers from union positions and
put in the lately-arrived gentleman with the
oily tongue. To any loyal mate who pro-
tests, we say: “Why, he has had a Univer-
sity training!” Of course, that is only one
of the glib stranger’s many lies, but we are
a credulous lot. Another question : Why are
coalminers, of all people, supporting a lot of
loafers in their opposition to a card system?
Aren’t we working under a similar system
ourselves? And what a howl we would raise
if it was proposed to abolish it! Every skip
of coal raised to the surface carries on it the
token of the man who has hewed and filled
it. At the end of the fortnight his pay is
computed on the quantity of coal sent to the
surface. Then why object to the card system
for others? It would be better for the
majority of us if we objected in a convincing
way to the three-card trick now being played
on us.
The Imperial Trade Tout.
Willy’s wire to Nikky, October 27, 1904 :
You ought not to forget to order some new ships,
which will be excellent persuaders during the peace
negotiations. Our private firms will be most glad to
receive contracts.
There you have the monarch in commerce
touting for trade for his country with all the
enthusiasm of a traveller for a new whisky.
The private firm, being made up of private
individuals, abhors war, in which it might
get hurt, and in which it is sure to be
robbed. But war possibilities, which mean
contracts, which in turn mean dividends, are
a totally different matter. To spread the
war interests as widely as possible in the
days of peace is the job of kings and politi-
cians who intend sooner or later to fight.
The more private firms there are to handle
the machinery of stoush. and the more share-
holders there are, the easier it is to get votes
for armaments; and the armaments once
ordered and paid for out of the public purse
the easier it is to get up an agitation to use
them, and so get something, or appear to get
something, for your money. No better argu-
ment than the Kaiser’s telegram just pub-
lished has ever been advanced against per-
mitting private interest to have even a finger
in the pie of war preparation. Even after
the present commotion some sort of big mili-
tary arrangements may still be necessary;
but there should be a very hard line drawn
against private profiteering in the machinery
of death.
—*-
Enterprise in German New Guinea.
Ernest Osborne’s observations :
On the spoken word or implied understanding that
investors and capital were cordially welcome to
develop the territories, a few Australian companies
acquired land in German New Guinea before the
outbreak of the war. Land was offered cheaply on
conditions which could not well be easier. The
authorities were benevolent, and no perceptible dis-
tinction in treatment was made between Australian
and German planters. The Government was eager
for development; labor was plentiful and cheap.
Thus favored, planters—individuals and companies—
pushed ahead. The result to-day is a picture of
growing wealth displayed on an equally imposing
scale in no other part of the world.
Beginning well down the straits separating New
Britain and New Ireland (formerly N'eu Pommern
and Neu Mecklenburg), the Gazelle Peninsula is
covered with plantations. Cape Gazelle to its very
point is being cleared and planted. Thence for many
miles into Blanche Bay, far as eye can see, steadily
rising from the beach in serried, perfectlv-straight
rows, the cocoanut palms run inland in graceful
upward sweeps. Herbertshohe (now Kokopo) stands
among immense wealth-producing plantations in the
heart of this marvellous district, almost facing the
Mother and Daughter volcanoes across the bay.
Spread over a one-time mangrove-swamp, beautiful
and unhealthy Rabaul, with its imposing wooden
buildings, graces Simpsonhafen, overshadowed by the
brooding volcano.
The principal development in New Britain, New
Ireland, Bougainville, and on the rich agricultural
flats around Madang on the New Guinea mainland
is in copra production, though some cocoa and
rubber are also grown. The Neu Guinea Oompagnie,
most aristocratic of German colonial concerns;
“Forsayth’s,” otherwise Die Hamburgisches Sudsee
Actien Gesellschaft, successors to “Queen Emma”
Fersa.vth’s magnificent plantations—these and “Herrn-
sheims” are making immense profit with copra at
present prices; 200 per cent, on the capital is a con-
servative estimate.
Of the immense profits but a small proportion
benefits white men locally. White men do not
generally live there long enough to reap any consider-
able benefit from their enterprise. “Forsaytn’s” and
a few others excepted, the' most magnificent estates
on the main as well as on the outlying islands,
including the Mortlocks, with lovely homes and great
incomes, are owned by Samoan ladies, wives of the
white men who gave their lives for the development
of the territories. These women, many of them
capable in business above average men, are not so
readily affected by the inimical climate as the
majority of whites. From the other plantations the
lion’s share of the wealth goes to shareholders in
temperate climates.
A matter closely affecting Australian enterprise
in these territories should receive attention. Some
companies hold options over land actually under
development. The German laws are equitably admin-
istered, to the satisfaction of all concerned, by the
Australian Administration, and it might be possible
to adjust matters so that the determinations would
be binding in the final war-settlement. Thus the
doubt about the future now entertained by Australian
companies operating in the N.-W. Pacific would be
set at rest. This would also make possible to Aus-
tralians the acquisition of land available in regions
whose natural development seems destined to pro-
gress under Australian control. Labor is still plenti-
ful and cheap (four to five shillings per month with
the simplest of food, trifling perquisites and
quarters), but the labor recruiter’s price has risen
from £5 to £7 10s. per recruit. For plantations
where the labor is known to be poorly fed, defrauded
of its perquisites, overdriven, neglected when sick,
and where laborers die, the boys refuse to sign on.
The old-established plantations and plantations known
for fair dealing have little difficulty in securing labor.
And that gives a hint to the white official. Tt is
of the utmost importance that Australians who may
go to Australian-owned plantations in the N.-W.
Pacific, giving health and life itself for shareholders’
benefit, should be adequately protected in regard
to accommodation, fares, salaries and medical atten-
tion on the plantation, by Australian legislation.
A beginning should be made by scrutinising the
agreements which employees are required to sign by
Australian companies. The arrival of low-salaried men
from the Commonwealth in high-salaried foreign
territories is the least desirable kind of advertise-
ment for Australia.
So far John Fell, who has resurrected the
Commonwealth Oil Corporation’s business at
Newnes (N.S.W.), lias received little
encouragement from either the Government
or the workmen ; and if it had not been for
the Protection afforded by Kaiser Bill’s sub-
marines. the shutters would have gone up
long ago. When a duty on oil was asked
for it was turned down because it would
increase the cost of lighting to the workers:
yet. through having- to depend on importa-
The Frenchman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
White, bled white! I am bleeding still!
Over my shapeless fields
The stalks are stripped, and the full ears
thin
And the sheaves to the binding.are gathered
in,
In the harvest that battle yields.
Terrible, tireless, moon by moon
Waits for his guerdon the Reaper grim ;
Out of my dwindling garner, soon
What shall I offer him?
The soil gapes black in the furrows bare,
And the naked clods in the stubble show,
Save for a patch where, here and there,
A few fair wheat-ears grow.
My eyes are keen by the pasture-wall
And my arms are strong by the pasture-
gates ;
Shall I render you. though the ears be few,
The fields where the Reaper waits?
Spilling and spreading, as runs the stain
Of new-shed blood on snow,
The hordes of the East swept up from Spain
To Gascony. Quercy and old Touraine.
In the red days long ago.
Hard by Tours and the river-marge
There stands a field that is known to you,
Where the Saracen broke to my white-hot
charge.
When Martel hammered through :
And though on the slope of Roneevaux
My Roland lay on his broken sword.
I strove to the winning, as all men know,
Mighty before the Lord !
Shall vou covet the streams that mv sires
held.
Or thresh in the barns where my fathers
flailed ?
Shall your flag rank where the green flag
sank
And Abderahmed failed?
Since, long since when my coast lay stark
To pillage and torch and shame.
Scoffer and spoiler of creeds and kings.
With the salt wind wet on his helmet-wings,
The long-haired Northman came.
The grey seas foamed to his galley-sweeps;
From Texel down to the ruined South.
His scaling-towers lay by the keeps
His camps at the river-mouth ;
His long keels swung on the harbor-bars,
And Paris flamed by the reddened Seine,
Yet, from the scorn of my threefold scars,
Stricken, I rose again !
Shall new lies level the olden hope
Or the moments mar what the ages make?
Can you break the pride, and the strength
beside,
That Rollo could not break?
Comradely over the English hand
My own hand rests and grips;
Flank to my flank for your overthrow
This England stands, whom my fields know
As the old seas knew her ships.
Doggedly (such is the English lot)
Her footmen harried my choicest spears.
Her gaunt kings rode and her bowmen shot
For the half of a thousand years !
Arrogant (such is the English way),
Tried in the fire and tested true,
Once at Trafalgar and Vigo Bay,
Cressy and Waterloo!
Shall swine be stalled where the lion lay.
Or truth in the mart stand stripped and
sold ?
Can yon fetter fast, can you hold at last
What England could not hold?
I have known the fulness of those that feast.
The leanness of those that lack ;
I have bedded with hunger and drunk with
guile,
Province and march have I lost awhile;
But my arm shall win them back.
Careless and chivalrous, fierce and free,
Heedless of cavil or blame or praise.
I have walked in the gloom of the Jacquerie
And the gleam of the Hundred Days.
I have felt the favor of fortune wide.
The veering of change and of fickle chance,
Yet ever, through passing of time and tide.
I have loved her, this my France!
By the tale of your thousand thousand dead.
By this my blood on the sodden grass.
It is thus and so ye may read and know
My will — you cannot pass!
White, bled white! lam bleeding yet!
But while one pulse shall thrill.
While one, but one, red drop shall start
Warm from the valor within my heart.
That heart shall mock you still!
Silently (such is France’s way.
The new. strange way of her later birth)
I guard, till the dawm of the newer day.
My fields that are sacred earth.
See where the Reaper, passing through.
Left us but litter and stubble-rows.
And standing ears that are fair, though few ;
A sweet, strange flower grows.
Seed of my labor and blood and breath
Here in the soil that is holy ground.
This red bloom springs from the wreck of
things—
The soul that France has found!
victoria.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.

The Migrations of the Bear, I ncle Sam
and a Word about Billjim.
-KussiA looks more like a stranded whale
every day. Last week the German threw
himself at Riga, whereupon what was left
of the local army melted iufi> the vastness
beyond, and when next heard from was
being assisted by the enemy’s cavalry as
pacemaker. The result of this is to make
the invaders masters of practical!j the
whole of Courland, and the next two ques-
tions relate to (1) the Russian naval force
in the Baltic and 12) Petrograd. There is
the usual story about the navy being ready
for eventualities. Also, as usual, it is
stated that the enemy will encounter great
difficulties in reaching the capital. Aone
of these allegations are worth the Russian
equivalent of 14d. Six months ago Russia
awoke and arose, for what purpose and in
obedience to what influence has never been
satisfactorily explained. Three months later
the army of the Revolution advanced undei
Brussiloff. Since then it has been
steadily going astern. At no stage did it
make even a decent excuse "for its existence,
and if the condition of the alleged "Front ’
be about the most awful into which such a
place can fall, the position at the rear seems
equally dreadful. With the shadow of
famine over the country in one direction and
demoralised armies on the run in another,
and no one in the length and breadth of the
land with even a figment of authority. no
great imagination is required to fill in the
blanks. Speculation on what Von llin pen-
burg intends to do next quite futile.
Apparently he can please himself. Any-
way, whether he takes a trip to go skating
on "the Neva is much more likely to depend
on his personal predilections than on any-
thing the Russian may do. If he has the
men to spare, the things that may happen to
Western and Southern Russia before General
Frost resumes command are not pleasant to
think about. That way the outlook is as
hilarious as the spectacle of a shipwreck
with 75 politicians struggling for the last
remaining lifebelt. Over at the other side
of the disturbance the fact that the Ameri-
can is under no delusions about either the
size of the job or the consequences of mak-
ing a dead-heat of it is the most satisfac-
tory aspect of the proceedings. 1 ncle
Sam is going about the business with his
mind made up that the individual with a
loud• whoop relating to an early decisive
victorv is merely a. noisy liar. This atti-
tude of mind calls for continual encourage-
ment, for of all the pestiferous nuisances
the Allies have had to overcome, the indi-
vidual and the newspaper and the
book written in 24 hours proclaiming
Germany to be at the last gasp have
been the most sinister. During the summer
now passing away the German armies on
the Western Front have stood up to the
heaviest punishment ever inflicted in battle,
and have hit back hard and often. _ There is
no getting away from this, nor is it any use
pretending that a broken Germany can
be detected. The Allies have a long way to
go yet. And they will get some bumps on
the journey. It is more than possible that
some of those bumps are waiting for our
own men and their mates in Palestine, that
theatre-ought to have more attention from
the “heads” than it is getting. _ In particular
the supply and quality of allied aeroplanes
in use on that Front is worth attention. It
isn’t pleasant to hear of Australian and
English hospitals being repeatedly bombed
from the sky. It suggests that things are
not being made unpleasant enough for the
bombers.

“Julius Caesar,” as played at Melbourne
King's last Saturday, is worth seeing by all
friends of Sliakspeare, if only for the admir-
able first act, which here extends into the
third division of the classic tragedy, closing
with the removal of the corpse of the notori-
ously dead Dictator from the Senators
Chamber. Not that the interest ends there.
The present version is still a fine play
throughout the second act (containing only
the Forum scene, with Cmsar’s body placed
on a funeral pyre below the pulpit where
Brutus and Antony deliver their orations)
and right to the end of the Tent scene in the
final section. True, the idea of burning the
late lamented in the Forum, instead of tak-
ing him off to the sacred crematorium, is a
melodramatic anachronism ; yet it serves its
lurid purpose, and would be a Quite success-
ful scenic interpolation if the pyre were
rather more imposing, and the cries of the
crowd leading up to the conflagration in the
streets beyond were clearly distinguishable.
The action of the mob is most hurried when
it should he most explanatory, owing to the
fact that there isn't a large enough mob in
the first place, and the spokesmen are chosen
from the principals of the co., who, having
no business in the hurly-burly, are trying to
disguise their voices. The opening of the
last act in the tent of Brutus carries the
narrative on without a break. But when
the warfare begins the air of grows
very murkv. Any production of “Julius
Caesar” is ’bound to wilt at the battle end.
The same weakness attaches to “Richard
III.” The action becomes scrappy and con-
fusing. The various scenes that the Bard
places under the stereotyped heading of
“another part of the field” are here conhned
to only one part of the field, the monotony
being broken by a tableau which is alleged
to indicate the triumph of Antony’s side.
And this one scene, littered with stage rocks,
is supposed to be the Plains of Philippi,
where the respective leaders of the opposing
forces stand and call one another names.
The spectacle of Brutus and Cassius, on the
one hand, standing on a rocky elevation to
exchange incivilities with Marc and Octa-
vius on the other side of the nuobled land-
scape, is not imposing. And the effect of
warriors going away for three minutes in
order to return to the same spot for dying
purposes becomes somewhat tiring. It is
unavoidable, but it is not war. Evidently
the scrap can’t be getting any for’arder it
all the chief contestants are wandering
around the same little hit of broken country
all the time. It might as well be trench
warfare in Flanders. The author made a
convincing conclusion to the pl&y practically
impossible. And if he hadn't it couldn't he
done at the price, or within an ordinary time
limit. The King’s production is done mighty
well at the price, and it lasts till past 11 p.m.
* * * *
Among the performers Greenaway comes
an easy first with his smooth, refined imper-
sonation of Cassius, the observant, scholarly,
smileloss man who objects to anyone being
greater than himself, and lias a keen eye for
the perquisites of office. Greenaway gets
inside the somewhat lagoic character. More-
over, lie looks it outside, and his elocution
is a pleasure. The lines of C ass i us . could
hardly be more effectively spoken in the
opening scene, where they are all important,
and in the Quarrel scene, where he appears
to be the injured party. George Bryant’s
rumbling diction is more at home in the Tent
scene than in previous parts of the play, for
he is always a good conversational actor.
He makes the honesty of Brutus terribly
rugged, with his tongue and his legs, too. and
it is only in the rough kindliness of his
manner that he seems to be the noblest
Roman of them all. His speech from the
Forum pulpit leaves one in doubt as to
whether the artful Antony wasn’t gammon-
ing when lie spoke of Brutus as an orator in
the florid sense of the word. lan Maclaren’s
Antony is not more oratorical than the pre-
vious speaker. lie makes the points of his
speech to the multitude in a barristerial
manner, like a counsel addressing tlm jury.
He doesn’t give the impression of a politician
stirring up the feelings of the mob. If the
scene were so placed that the bulk of the
mob could only be heard roaring and groan-
ing in the wings, thus creating an atmo
sphei-e Of crowd/ and if Marc someUmes
shouted to make himself heard afai o ,
CO d accent him as a possible composer ot
the most famous public speech on refold.

Miriam, who flaunted her red Jjat in ‘‘Out-

stantly dissipates the prejudice her head
r excites by revealing all the little tneK

firs? man who has the mental eminence to
pitv her position. Rescued from the streets
hv'a voung financier, who is steadily doping
a system already overcharged with disap-

her hunger with half a banana,

the freedom of his time and income, and

generation, both vastly improving by e
experience and generally creating a beautiful

turns to her hubby leaving Miriam to resume
the old alliance on the same terms as before.
* * * *
Miss Maedonell plays Miriam principally
in the lower register. Even in her. angriest
passages there is no noticeable heightening
of tone, but rather a hoarseness and explosive
breathlessness which seem to blow her sen-
tences to pieces and hardly allow her to
retrieve a solid word from tlie massacre. It
is somewhat showy work in a. passe style
of acting, but it is less impressive than her
sure, nonchalant touches in the first act.
The part of her boy, Geoffrey, is effectively
millinered by Waldron et Oie. There never
was such an exquisite exponent of free love.
He wears his feelings like decorations and
puts his dialogue into gaiters, so to. speak.
I? all Nuts resembled Charles Waldron,
flappers wouldn’t consider marriage worth
while. Louis Kimball and Lewis Willoughby
correctly express the chivalry of the well-
bred man in irregular situations, and
Plumpton Wilson is a perfect servant. .Miss
Beryl Bryant is Geoffrey’s first love. Valan-
tine. She has a warm voice that goes well
with brown velvet and her brunette coloring,
but she keeps her reserves of soul for her
kisses. Apart from her canoodly moments
with Waldron, she appears to regard life as
a bit of a bother.
“You’re In Love.” the now musical play
by the manufacturers of “High Jinks,” made
its strike-delayed start at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, last week-end, with Maude Fane
leading with confidence and charm in a
Dresden-doll part that hadn’t previously
been worn by Dorothy Brunton. It involves
her in seven songs and gives her no chance
to show how amusing she can be in a frump-
ish hat and a macintosh. Still that can be
held in reserve while she sings and looks like
something off a wedding cake. The brightly-
mounted trifle, with a hotel sun-parlor for
the first, act and for the second an elaborate
steamer deck “set” with a swinging boom
that supports Maude Fane over the heads
of the front stalls, has Californian sunshine
in it and not much ragtime. The first trio
(a daintily-scored number) is almost
stately, and No. 2 on the musical list is
the first plain trace of “rag.” The plot was
probably written in full on the back of an
envelope while the librettist waited for a
car or a cocktail. An incredibly nervous
and croaky young man ("William Greene)
allows himself to be married by dry legal
contract to his piuk-and-fluffy girl (Maude
Fane), who lives in stage dread of her curi-
ously old-maidish aunt —a grass widow three
times removed from her husbands. This bleak
well-brushed tyrant (Connie Ediss) hates all
men so much that she loads the marriage
contract with clauses which keep the newly-
weds on mere nodding terms till the end of
the second act. It is the fretful Connie’s
monotonous job to plod through the enter-
tainment like a female referee commandin'*
the two clinging principals to part and box
on. Her No. d husband (Alfred Frith) is
a scared, ostrich-like African missionary
with redeeming traces of Sin, who has some-
how got the job of guardian or keeper to
the contract-hobbled bridegroom. Talleur
Andrews and Cecil Bradley hover round as a
newly-wed pair with no author-made troubles
who can only express their unfathomable
content in polite songandance. Field Fisher
makes a few welcome intrusions as a
pathetic, fatuous, sun-boiled bo’s’n who in-
dulges in dismal deep-sea roarings and pip-

ing S) and receives with simple gratitude from

The co. which finds “Turn to the Right”
almost enough to travel on permanently has
given Adelaide a look at a fresh play, the
New Henrietta.” At least, it is fresh in this
country. The word “New” in the title,
though having no meaning at all in connec-
tion with the story, is understood to indicate
that an old play by one Victor Mapes, lhe
Henrietta,” has been brought up to date d\
Winehell Smith. The Winehellising process
is manifest at every turn. Old Nick, mil-
lionaire financier of N’York. is disappoints
in his idle sou Bertie, and entrusts all his
affairs to Turner, a son-in-law, who thus
stands to get the whole fortune barring one
beggarly million with which Bertie has been
sent adrift. Yet this Turner-fool plans to
ruin Nick within one week. Of course
