Sydney Tivoli turns over a new and (pro-
bably) brighter leaf of the eternal pro-
gramme on Saturday, when Ruth Budd
(“The Girl with the Smile”) begins a
season supported by a co. of new artists, in-
cluding the Camille Trio, comedy acrobats.
Meanwhile an epidemic of last nights is
raging, and the managerial ad. is very excited
about it.
As Taronga Park is closed to the public
arrangements have been made for a grand
parade of animals at Sydney Lyceum this
week. Up go the canvas tents, and the story
of the “Sawdust Ring,” with Bessie Love as
the queen of beauty, is unfolded. “Never
judge a Crown Prince by his coat of arms”
is the moral of the Vitagrapli film at the
same flicker-house. Those who are inter-
ested in the machinations of princes, the
cunning of secret-service agents and dollars
by the million, all flavored by lovely woman,
are offered the ingredients for the usual
silver coin. Rider Haggard’s “Dawn” is
demonstrating at Hoyt’s that' a packet of
compromising love-letters are but flotsam on
the irresistible current of young love. Alice
Brady gets entangled at the Crystal Palace
in matrimonial meshes, first with a married
man, second with a forger, and for the third
and lucky last with a bona-fide millionaire.
“The Flame of the Yukon” is throwing up
an alluring beacon at the Lyric; and The
Empress is giving Cup punters a tip as to
the inner workings of the turf in “The
Whip.”

The Vic. Artists' Spring show: —
The faithful are again called to Eastern Hill,
where Bernard Hall is once more the showiest per-
former, with the customary frozen nude whom a
comparatively warm color-scheme is trying to
thaw. He has another study of a red-lipped lady
with a violet mask tilting a carelessly-drawn wine-
glass in an alleged spirit of carnival. Both pic-
tures advertise Hall’s method of reducing Woman
to the neuter gender; and he fondly uses his fried-
egg effects as if he found most pleasure in vistas
of ruined breakfasts. The Yellow Peril has also
infected the art of Miss Mary Cecil Allen, who
obtains emphasis by posing a damsel in canary
trimmings. Harry B. Harrison has dodged tin;
labor of big endeavors and exercised his brush in
the limited area of “studies.” The most notable
is a well-drawn head of an old lady. In his sub-
stantial view of a female in a blue kimono, O.
Bowman Fletcher reveals an eye for the obvious
that never misses a leaf of the vegetation on the
gown. In “Missing,” a presentment of a familiar
grief, ,J. Ward Symons catalogues the immaterial
with a leaden hand. C. Povne’s “Black Hat,” on
the contrary, attempts to achieve ait by stealthy
suppression of details. Miss Roxburgh handles a
nude, “The Outcast,’’ with a feminine affectation
of masculine vigor. Delafield Cook's landscapes
are beginning to deserve a second look. He is
showing an agreeable artistic reticence and is keep-
ing his palette cool. In “A Summer Storm” C.
Douglas Richardson seems to have prepared a tree
for its portrait by frizzing out the foliage with
curling-tongs. Tom Carter solemnises the alleged
poverty of a cocky in a severe diagram of a gaunt
head. Miss Cumbrae Stewart continues her
implacable energy as a pastellist, and Miss Dora
Wilson robs a well-known visage of a good deal of
the interest it possesses in life. Miss Rose Walker
water-colors two interiors with good effect, but
G. H. Cassidy’s grey studies are eloquent of starch.
The drawing of F. C. Reynolds appeals to one from
several frames, but his outlook is blue with pessi-
mism. Miss D. M. Ellerman’s “Merry Meg” is ar.
elaborate pictorial treatise on an old dame and
some vegetables, with the ancient dutifully playing
super to the vegetables. Arnold J. Shore's Heidel-
berg sketch has considerable value among so much
pretentious amateurishness. The works of W.
Nicholls Anderson are also worthy of favorable men-
tion, along with the miniatures of Miss Golding and
the aquatints and etchings of F. A. Campbell and
P. W. Kernot.
* * * ;!*
Muriel Ostriche and Douglas Fairbanks
are the principal sights at the Melba
Theatre, Melbourne, this week. Muriel is
the popular fancy in “Moral Courage,”
whilst Fairbanks is a Bowery chucker-out
in his well-known disturbance, “Reggie
Mixes In.” At the Britannia Charlie
Chaplin is in “Easy Street” and Kitty
Gordon shows some more backbone in “The
Beloved Adventuress.” Also there is “Love’s
Law,” in which Stuart Holmes and Joan
Sawyer can be dimly discerned after the
dust of an erotic tussle has settled down.-
Matheson Lang has changed his clothes
and his century to go Shylocking melo-
dramatically for a week on Hoyt’s screens
in Melbourne. The old Jew could hardly be
so bad as he is here painted. Miss Hutin
Britton (Mrs. Lang) has a comfortable
style for the part of Portia, and it might
be noted incidentally that George Morgan,
formerly known in Melbourne as a seaside
entertainer, capers effectively as Launcelot
Gobbo. Also in motion at Hoyt’s are the
Haunted Pyjamas, which turn men into their
grandmothers and do other startling things.
Adelaide shows are getting busy. Marie
Tempest is coming in at the Royal, the
King’s reopens with an Italian Opera season
—a novelty for this little house—and, while
the Taits put up Cappelli and other favorites
there, Balboni opens at the Queen’s. Other
good music was given by Winsloe Hall’s
orchestra at the Exhibition. Prince’s
Players are still at the Majestic, which also
has the 3 Sailors in an absurd but funny
turn, and the Anderson Co. at the Tivoli
has switched off to bushranging drama. The
scene being American, they call it a “hold
up.” West’s make a great feature of Charles
Ray, a film artist who dances at least as
well as he acts ; Wondergrapli shows Pauline
Frederick as dramatic and Fatty Arbuckle
as foolish ; and the Pav., where a new big
Orchestral machine “plays” all day. gives
one more of those Warning to Girls dramas
which the authors claim to be moral lessons.
Simon Fraser, -who was one of the fossil
members of the first Federal Senate by the
grace of his old friend Syme, had an 85th
birthday recently. Time was when the old
man worried himself terribly about Rome,
but he has outlived that and is preparing
his soul for a heaven where both sects are
on the free list and even the Pope may have
a chance.

“0.1 v.” : Bailey and Grant’s venture with
“Julius Csesar” is the first staging # of the
tragedy in Melbourne since George Miln
produced it at the old Opera House a quar-
ter of a century ago. Oscar Asche did it
on the Town Hall platform, with a back-
ground of dark hangings, but that was only
a one-matin6e show. Miln’s production was
very respectable. The chief put a forcible
personality into the part of Marc Antony,
though it wasn’t a young and graceful per-
sonality. The most picturesque feature was
the actor in the name part —Collett Dobson,
lie had a high Roman nose and looked re-
markably like the medallion portraits of the
original J. Caesar. Kay Souper has the
height and bearing for a Julius, but his first
appearance is hardly as startlingly impres-
sive as that of the young Australian actor
of long ago. By the way. Greenaway, who
now plays Cassius, is said to have played
Caesar admirably in a N’York production,
though he is on the small side for such a
commanding billet. He comes much nearer
to the popular conception of a lean and
hungry Cassius.
“Up Stage”: “Julius Caesar” was last
played by Rignold at Her Majesty’s. Sydney,
in 1880. George R. headed a fine cast as
Marc Antony, in the bill being Cathcart
(Brutus'). Jewett (Cassius), Scott Tnglis,
Jack Sweeney and Joe Tolano. The piece
ran 10 weeks. Oscar Asche. with aspira-
tions to play Antony, seriously considered
staging “Caesar” during his first visit here,
but was content with a recital at Melbourne
Town Hall.
“Baldhead” : The third act of Tom Rob-
ertson’s “Caste,” put on in Sydney for the
benefit of the Actors’ Benevolent, saw a
departure from tradition. Herbert Ranson,
who played Capt. the Hon. George D’Alroy,
instead of presenting himself :'n mufti to his
heart-broken Esther, sported a uniform that
won a great cheer from the close-packed
goddesses. It was a blend of Indian Mutiny
cum Commonwealth periods, the captain’s
three stars on the shoulder-strap bringing it
right up to date. C’est magnifique —but not
what Tom Robertson intended. Dorothy
Cumming’s soul-stirring Esther atoned for a
little thing like that, and Frank Elliott’s
Major Ilawtree was reminiscent of Squire
Bancroft. If the half-promised revival of
“Caste” follows on “Grumpy’s” heels., the
management must provide D’Alroy with a
real live baby. The rag contraption he
clutched on this occasion set mammas snig-
gering where they should have been sobbing.
“Norbell”: Hear, hear! to “75,” who
observes that the perfect make-up for the
stage is “the sort of which the spectators
are unconscious” (B. 30/8/’l7). I’ve often
seen Nellie Stewart, who knows all about it,
come out on the brilliantly-lit stage before
the curtain went up in order to put the
finishing touches to her complexion under
active-service conditions. But some theat-
rical managers won’t have it so. I remem-
ber another good Australian mummer, Percy
Walshe, a pastmaster at make-up, playing ui
an Andersonian explosion at Melbourne
King's. As usual his preparation was about
perfect; but when the curtain fell on Act 1
the boss, who knew his public, charged round
from the front and complained in scandalised
accents, “You’ve got no make-up on
Walshe invited inspection. “Well, anyhow, ’
grumbled the aggrieved Bill, “you can t
notice it from the d.c.”

The Australian Actors’ Assoc, now has

“Masetto”: Balboni, at Melbourne Audi-
torium, has my praise for not truckling to
the encore fiend. He sings his programmed
song and makes it apparent that he does
not want to give two or three more items
and walk on the platform and bow between
each. I don't think Melbourne has had the
encore madness so badly for 20 years. At
the Scotney-White concerts it got so fierce
that the male vocalist pleaded fatigue, cold,
gout and sea-sickness for not responding
further to the outrageous demands of the audi-
ences. But the lady never wearied of walk-
ing on, bowing in several fashions and sing-
ing something else. Dr. Price, Melbourne
city organist, allows no encores at the Muni-
cipal Concerts. A few weeks ago, _ pro-
ceedings were held up for fully 10 minutes
by the clamoring audience in its wild endea-
vor to force a repeat of one item ; but the
management sat tight and won.

F. J. Riley. a prominent Trades Hall
official with a raucous voice and Parliamen-
tary aspirations, addressed a crowd of pan-
kers after one of their recent attempts to
moh Parliament House, and naively urged
that the strikers “ought to meet every day
and have it explained to them just what it
was they were striking for and what it. all
meant.” Nobody laughed. The crowd just
yelled wildly and kept on striking for—for—
well, just kept on striking.
In its new Tramways Bill the Peacock
Government proposes that the tramways
shall pay to the Vie. Railways £50,000 a
year to recoup them for losses due to tram-
way competition. This means that citizens
guilty of travelling by tram will have to pay
fines, in the form of higher fares than would
otherwise be necessary, aggregating £lOOO
weekly. Probably if the Melbourne cab-
drivers make representations to Peacock he
will be prepared to increase the amount of
the fine inflicted upon the public and pay
compensation also to Jehu.
Westralia’s ex-Premier Jack Scaddan is
going to try to oust Johnson from Guildford
at the coming election. His chances are
poor. The fact is John is a discredited
politician—as a keener man would have
seen when he lost his old seat, Ivanhoe-
Brown Hill, a strong conscriptionist centre,
to Lutey, Avho is a pronounced Anti. But
Scaddan is one of the too-numerous. politi-
cians who understand a hint only when it
resembles a paving-stone.
October 7 has been set aside for “national
prayers” in Maoriland. All other days.-
judging by the food-profiteering that is going-
on. are reserved for national preyers.
Two veterans died on the same day in
Adelaide last week. One of them, J. M.
Wendt, was a standing instance of the folly
of judging a man by his name. He left
Denmark in 1854, to get away from German
rule, became naturalised in S.A. at once,
and at 87 was more bitter_tlian most Aus-
tralian-born against the Kaiser. Several
grandsons are at the Front; one of them
will never come back. The other veteran,
H. J. Gliddon, had a more varied career.
Grandson of Leigh Hunt, he was educated
in France; joined an uncle, an Adelaide
banker, in 3558; enlisted in Melbourne and
saw a lot of Maori fighting; served all over
the world, including Gibraltar and Jamaica ;
got back to Adelaide finally as a railway
man in 1885. He lived to be 79. and had
the military funeral which S.A. gives to all
such old warriors.

case when Richard is concerned.

“Fax”: According to a decision given by
Judge Winneke in a Ballarat appeal case, the
public has for years been fooled by the term
“flannel” as applied to the material which
confiding citizens are advised to wear next
the skin. Dictionaries define it as “woollen
cloth,” but the dictionaries have not kept
pace with the tricks of the trade. Flannel
used to be a woollen material, but the fabric
has become more and more cottonised, until
at last, says John Dodgshun, warehouseman.
“1 per cent, of flannel would technically con-
stitute a flannel.” A piece of softgoods is
not what it is, but what the men who sell it
say it is. Said his Honor: —
Appellants had called a mass of evidence as to
the meaning of the term between buyer and seller,
which was of far greater value than a dictionary
definition.
And the public revenue is a party to this
imposition. Once upon a time the Con-
troller of Customs issued an order that
nothing less than 90 per cent, of pure wool
entitled a material to be called flannel. The
Trade appears to have over-ruled it with a
general disorder. Any feeble cotton fabric is
flannel that pays duty as woollen. The
Trade owes no duty to the public, as long as
JUST IN TIME.
HE : “ Er — y' know — er — I’d do anything on
this earth — er —for you.”
SHE: “Oh, thank you so much. I’m just
wanting another name for my Roll of
Honor.”
it pays duty to the Customs Department.
When Judge Winneke spoke of the "loose-
ness of the term flannel” he was too mealy-
mouthed. His Honor should have described
the term as horribly immoral and indecent.
It is one thing to be a bit loose, and another
thing to be grossly prostituted to the trade's
base purposes. Next time a gent, one is up
for misappropriation let him plead a trick
of the trade and see what happens. Presum-
ably nothing will happen if he quotes this
new precedent.
A cry from the Front: —
Keep on writing to us, you home people! You
all seem to flag if a man only stays away long
enough. The late reinforcements get the bulk of
every mail. Speak to any old hand and he will
tell you that he hardly ever gets any letters now.
It is going to he an effort to maintain interest in a
place with which there is no connecting link, and
with many the effort will not be made. It is not to
be wondered at that men who never hear from home
should form other friendships in a country where
the beauty of the women takes the breath away.
The Australian girls will have nobody but them-
selves to blame if Billjim goes home with a
smiling stranger’s photograph in his tunic pocket—
if he goes home at all.
• * * *
Billjim again. Four of him on convales-
cent leave at Boulogne borrowed a sailing-
boat from the w 7 ater-front of that once gay
spot, and set out across the Dover Straits
for Blighty, quite unmindful of any U-boats
that might be prowling around. They arrived
at Folkestone, to be received by the coast-
guard with open arms —and promptly handed
over to martial care. But the Heads were
lenient that day ; Billjim’s penalty was only
four days’ pay, and he reckoned the excur-
sion was worth the money.
A sympathetic brother moved at a recent
Melbourne meeting of the Tobacco Workers
Union that the concern’s total surplus ot
£250 should be devoted to the use of the
Strike Committee —and the motion was car-
ried. It seems a noble act, or a fatuous
one, according to the leanings of the ob-
server. But the £250 will certainly do nothing
in the way of making two mutton-chops grow
where there was but one before. It will merely
take the chops from one spot a M place them
in another, leaving nothing but a blank.
Considering all the funds that such bodies
have at their disposal from time to time, it
is a pity that some great union leader
doesn’t arise and suggest the formation of,
say. a Toilers’ Barracouting Society. V ith
£250 and a few 7 willing out-on-strikers,
enough cheap fish could be hauled out of the
bay to supply protein for hundreds of
workers’ families. As it is, the 500 ten-
bob coupons by means of which the £250
will be circulated —if the money ever gets
that far from the Trades Hall —will do
nothing whatever to add to the general food-
supply.
“Kassi Kassi” : Professor Preuss,
managing director of the German
New Guinea Co., recently men-
tioned in The Bulletin, was,
before our occupation, one of the
people who ran the show 7 in Kaiser
Wilhelm’s Land. I was on that
coast a few months before the
war, and it was Preuss, apparently,
who had to decide whether I w r as
to be admitted to the capital,
whether I could be allowed to take
photographs, and whether the
“Club” at Herbertshohe might be
permitted to take me in and do
for me at a high figure. He made
up his mind to grant all these con-
cessions : but I am bound to re-
mark that my camera fell sick with
some mysterious illness and any
photographs I took turned out
blank; also, I w 7 as not allow’ed to
sleep in Rabaul. and was carefully
misinformed about all the roads in
the neighborhood. A managing
director of a company is something
anywhere, no doubt, but not quite
all that Preuss appeared to be. I
fancy Berlin could have pinned a
different label on him had it chosen.
Incidentally, Stead’s publications
were lately, and I believe still are,
the only English magazines obtain-
able in Rabaul from German
stores; they are enthusiastically
spoken of by their sellers. As I
found them, the Germans right
through are firmly convinced that
the occupation is only for the
time. They laugh if you suggest
that anything else is possible.
“Pte. Enterprise”: There is a shanghaied
Billjim in Victoria and it happened like this.
A transport returning with sick and wounded
called at Fremantle (W.A.). Those proceed-
ing to the other States were given a few
hours’ leave ashore, and some of them were
detained by beer. Just before sailing time
a picket was sent down with orders to
gather the strays. After collecting a num-
ber the officer in charge saw a Billjim lying
on a green. As he could neither talk nor
walk, they carried him on board on a
stretcher. Next morning, well out at sea,
Bill woke up. stared at the waves and mut-
tered “ ‘Struth !” He belonged to Blackboy
Camp and had never been out of W.A. in his
life before.

When Billjim came down from Nineveh
When operations eased off in Mesopotamia all who
had been 12 months in the country were given
leave. We had a rotten trip down the Tigris, 200
of us aboard the little river boat, and 400 natives
on each barge. Every man was allotted 2ft. of
space on fhe deck, and we had the bad luck to be
placed right between the two funnels. Talk about
hot—it was a Turkish bath, 51 days of it, and all
on tinned tack !
The river from Baghdad to Kut-el-Amara is un-
interesting, and there is nothing much to see at
Kut, except the monument the Turks erected to
commemorate Townsend’s downfall. But the British
have made marvellous improvements along the river
and at the base, and there’s not the slightest doubt
about our intention to hold the place. After that,
three days at our former camp at Margil, the same
old dust, the same old Hades. Then from Basra to
Bombay, with lifebelt drill morning, noon and
night.
We had to report at Poona and take our month's
leave from there, being allowed to go to any hill
station. Most of us chose Darjeeling—from Cal-
cutta by a little toy train that you stoop to get
into. Then up and up 8000 ft., and, with any luck,
wc shall see the sun rise over Mt. Everest one of
these mornings.
Death rides some very close finishes.
A lad at Richmond (Vic.) enlisted early in
the war and served in Gallipoli and France.
His brother joined the A.I.F. last November
and embarked in January following. All
efforts to get in touch with one another
failed, until one day the soldier acci-
dentally discovered his veteran brother
in a neighboring camp behind the lines. They
had barely exchanged greetings when a visit-
ing enemy ‘plane dropped a bomb, killing the
Gallipoli campaigner outright and slightly
injuring the brother. Still the boys won by
a handclasp.

BATTLE PASSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A quaint old gabled cottage sleeps between the
raving hills.
To right and left are livid strife, but on the deep,
wide sills
The purple pot-flowers swell and glow, and o’er the
walls and eaves
Prinked creeper steals caressing hands, the poplar
drips its leaves.
Within the garden hot and sweet
Fair form and woven color meet,
While down the clear, cool stones, ’tween banks with
branch and blossom gay,
A little, bridged, blind rivulet goes touching out its
way.
Peace lingers hidden from the knife, the tearing
blinding shell,
Where falls the spattered sunlight on a lichen-
covered well.
No voice is here, no fall of feet, no smoke lifts cool
and grey,
But on the granite stoop a cat blinks vaguely at the
day.
From hill to hill across the vale
Storms man’s terrific iron gale;
The cot roof on a brooding dove recks not the distant
gun.
A brown hen scolds her chickens chasing midges in
the sun.
Now down the eastward slope they come.
No call of fife, no beat of drum,
But stealthily, and in the green,
Low hid, with rifle and machine,
Spit hate and death ; and red blood flows
To shame the whiteness of the rose.
Crash follows crash ; the bestial roar
Of ghastly and insensate war
Breaks on the cot. A rending stroke,
The red roof springs, and in the smoke
And spume of shells the riven walL
Pile where the splintered elm-tree sprawls.
From westward, streaming down the hill,
Shot-ravaged, thinned, but urgent still,
The brown, fierce, blooded Anzacs sweep,
And Hell leaps up. The lilies weep
Strange crimson tears. Tight-lipped and mute,
The grim, gaunt soldiers stab and shoot.
It passes. Frantic, fleeing death,
Wild-eyed, foam-flecked and every breath
A labored agony, like deer
That feel the hounds’ keen teeth, appear
The Prussian men, and, wild to slay,
The hunters press upon their prey.
Cries fade and fitful shots die down. The tumbled
ruin now
Smokes faintly in the summer light, and lifts the
trodden bough.
A sigh stirs in the trampled green, and held and
tainted red
The rill creeps o’er a dead man’s face and steals
along its bed.
One deep among the lilacs thrown
Shock all the stillness with a moan.
Peace like the snowflake lights again where utter
silence lies,
And softly with white finger-tips she seals a
soldier’s eyes.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

When the eight militant unions held their monstev
meeting in Sydney Town Hall an orating boilermaker
said that “the tools of thousands of men are rusting
in the shops.” And the tools of scores are rusting
on the serapheap where they were thrown after the
strikers had deliberately smashed them before leaving
work.
From a Billjim officer in France-
The Hutl, after ceaseless work on his line, has had
to abandon it—one of the firstfruits of the Battle of
the Somme, Before evacuating the country he
methodically carried out the most awful acts of
brigandage and destruction. The whole countryside has
been laid Waste —-villages burned to the ground and
every tree cut to the roots. It is heart-breaking to see
fruit-trees —-whole orchards of them—lying on the
ground. Nothing was sacred. Churches are burnt
or blown up: even graveyards have been desecrated
for the purpose of getting the metal from the coffins.
In large towns, too, incalculable damage has been
done. I was in one two days after the evacuation.
The inhabitants’ homes and belongings had been
either looted or burnt, and they themselves collected
in one quarter. They had not tasted meat for six
months and would have starved had not the Ameri-
cans supplied them with foodstuffs. Yet the spirit
of these old people was magnificent. French flags,
long hidden, sprang out as if by magic and waved
over homes and ruins alike.
My only hope is that we shall have a free hand
among German villages before long. And I fiope
the French will take over the operations.

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Apropos the
Pankhurst row outside Melbourne Parlia-
ment House. On August 9, 1860, the Nichol-
son Ministry resigned, and O’Shanassy,
Ebden and Heales were successively sent for.
The Land Bill was the cause of the crisis.
On August 22 Parliament met after an ad-
journment. Heales and Nicholson then
made explanations: the former said he had
required a promise from the Governor to
dissolve Parliament, and had been infused.
There was a great outcry about this, and
on the 28th a serious riot took place at
Parliament House. The row started at a
meeting in the Eastern Market. It was “a
vile attempt,” says one alleged historian,
“made by the Eastern Market mob to over-
awe Parliament. The Houses declined to
adjourn, though the building was rushed, and
many of the police were seriously injured.”
My own recollection is that the “mob” did
not enter the precincts at all. The Mayor, Dr.
Richard Eades, mounted the fence which
stood at the old gates, and while clinging to
a lamp-post read the Riot Act. Next day
the Parliament passed a Bill through all its
stages to prevent such meetings within a
• quarter of a mile of the House. This cov-
ered the Eastern Markets and ended the
•campaign there.

“F.N.T.” : The Melbourne Public Library,
with its generous breathing facilities, has
set a high standard in sanitation; but the
case is terribly different in some of the sub-
urban free libraries. The committees of
these concerns seem to take but a perfunc-
tory interest in their duties. At Prahran,
for instance, there is the biggest free-library
out of the city, yet its value is seriously
impaired by the encouragement it offers to
influenza germs and Bouquet de Bookworm.
This and various other suburban reading-
) rooms of the kind were established in the
days when the average municipal councillor
was but slightly informed, if informed at
all, as to the habits of the more popular
cocci, with the result that the air-circulating
arrangements were of a very free-and-diseasy
nature; and even nowadays the authorities
don’t seem to have the heart to upset the
dear old lack-of-system of grandma’s time.
“Sturt Street.” : One Clayton, living within
10 miles of Ballarat, was waited upon a
while ago by a gentle Chinaman. The soft-
spoken, sweet-smiling Asiatic wanted the job
of converting Clayton’s backyard into a
vegetable garden. Never did a man want a
job so badly. The price Sin Cat came down
to eventually was so absurdly low
that Clayton concluded it would be
flying in the face of Providence to
deny himself a superb kitchen garden.
So Sin Cat was set going. He rooted
out some weeds, and mapped a number
of beds, then went to work in a small
hole in the north-east corner of the
allotment. “Wha’for?” asked Clayton.
“Get plenty soil fill ’em up, makee
level,” said Sin Cat. Then suddenly
Sin Cat jacked it. Clayton noticed
that he was carrying away a far
larger bundle than he brought, and,
filled with suspicion, he insisted on an
inspection. He had to use force. Sin
Cat’s bundle was mainly bones —
human bones! A whole skeleton was
there. “My cousin,” said Sin Cat in
explanation. “Me takee home China.”
Clayton is positive that the bones must
have been buried for quite ' 40 years.
Now, who kept a tab on them? How had
Sin Cat contrived to just hit the spot?

“Copabella”: Conan Doyle never strung
together a better necklace of coincidences than
the Victorian C.I.D. did in a case recently
before a suburban court. Last Aprilan old shop-
keeper in Footscray was stuck-up by armed
robbers in his shop doorway. There was a
bit of gun-play by the local police, and one
of the robbers, a youth of 19, was cornered
and captured. Madden, C.J., let him off
with three months and a recommendation
that he should be kept from contamination.
A strong bid was made by the police to get
the name of the youth’s accomplice, but the
lad was “oyster,”* and did his term without
squeaking. About the end of August a Mel-
bourne electrician named How'ey opened by
mistake a letter addressed to one Davey, an
employee. To the similarity of name was
added the fact that the post-office date-
stamping machine had run a blurry line
across the “Davey,” disfiguring it. The
letter made Howey think. It invited the
recipient to get a few of the firm’s billheads
with its receipt stamp upon them, so that
the writer might fake a receipt for a motor.
It also asked for one or two 200-volt electric
lamps to help the writer on his way. The*
requests were made as a means of repaying,
the good offices of the writer in keeping
Davey out of something! Now, a key had
been broken in the lock of the firm’s base-
ment door, and as the letter referred to the
writer’s intention to use that door as a
means of getting the lamps should Davey
fail to procure them, the letter was handed
on to the police. The C.I.D. photographed
it and let it finish its journey. It
told its recipient to “destroy this and be
careful.” Davey did as he was asked—to be
confronted next morning with a 'tec. and
the photograph. The mysteries of the “third
degree” probably form the next chapter of
the story, but the succeeding one opened at
a house* in Footscray, where a quantity of
electric wire and fittings were found and the
just-released 19-year-old was roped in for
unlawful possession. The charge was failing
badly when the police played their ace in
the form of the photographed letter. A
rather staggered lawyer demanded the origi-
nal. known to be burnt. The police coun-
tered with the corpus and evidence of Davey.
now up as No. 2 in the armed-robbery
charge. Costs did his best with a tale of a
youth chastened and repentant after three
months in gaol, but Sherlock Holmes’s photo-
graphed letter was too circumstantially
damning, and the more-or-less chastened
went up for another sixer.

“Globe” : You’ll be glad to know that Bill-
jim no longer has to pay lid. per word for
wireless messages from hospital ships off the
Australian coast. He has been returning to
Australia, sick and wounded, for over two
years now. and at last it has been decided to
give him the rate charged to travelling
civilians, viz., 6d. Now, having persuaded
the Government to that much, can't we in-
duce it to let homecoming Billjim. sick or
wounded from the wars, send just one brief
message to his eagerly waiting people without
paying tribute to the Commonwealth? That
would mean he would pay 2d. a word, the
ship’s charge. The other 4d. goes now to the
Commonwealth. In the circumstances, can’t
we forego that?

A RHYME OF STRIKES AND SPRING,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, Spring is here, and the wattle blooms, and
everything’s really gay.
And the magpie welcomes the early morn in quite a
delightful way.
The sun is warm on the greening grass and Nature is
big with hope
out of the darkness, creepy-crawl, a myriad buzz-
things grope,
Presently in the scheme of thing-
To do their whirl on extended wings.
Spring—and a question beyond it all that tinges
the soul with grey
As I sniff at the tonicity, fragrant air: Will the
baker be here to-day f
Spring t —and the thrusting of tender shoots, and
the, bursting of buds and all.;
The butterflies and the bees that wing to the top of
the gum-tree tall,
Giddy and gladsome and wildly full of the beautiful
wine of life,
Everything doing its destined job with never a hint
of strife;
Purple and golden and yellow and red,
Tossing its glad heels Over its head—
Spring! Yet I stand at the window, glum, awaiting
what may befall,
And plaintively gaze at the “Tradesmen's Gate”—
When will the butcher callt
Oh, Spring is here and all sorts of things that lately
were drab or brown
Are green or tawny or boldly pink; and, clad in a
gay, new gown,
Dame Nature sings at her work all day. Forever the
ether throbs
With a restless murmur of stirring Spring and the
Spring-poet’s shaking sobs;
But never above these sounds I hear
The noise of groceries drawing Hear.
Spring—on short rations! I watch and wait with a
worried and anxious frown
And a quaking soul. What shall vie do if the grocer-
lad turns us downt

Australia has produced another extra-
ordinarily young colonel —well-set-up Aubrev
Wiltshire, aged 25. Wiltshire left a job
in the Bank of Australasia to enlist, and
was a lootenant under Colonel R. A. Crouch,
whose place as C.O. he now fills. The young
man has the Military Cross and the Croix
de Guerre.
Groperland scores another Y.C. through
Private John Carroll, of Ivurrawang. His
was the crashing, smashing kind of valor.
He rushed a trench when the barrage lifted,
bayoneted four Germans, rescued a pal,
killed another Hun, pushed on, captured a
machine gun single-handed and made corpses
of the three handiest gunners. Then, being
slightly wounded, he took a spell. Before
enlisting he was a guard on the Kurrawang-
Kalgoorlie woodline.

who were wounded in the Eureka fight. May
be; but nobody named Monty Miller figures
in Peter Lalor’s list of killed and wounded.
E. A. Huber, Clerk of Records in the House
of Reps., has cut his faint con-
nection with Germany by angli-
cising his name to Hubert.
There never was any doubt
about his character and loy-
alty, but his patronymic has
been a butt for super-patriots,
who have also made it the
basis for the most reckless sus-
picions. Hubert is the Aus-
tralian-born offspring of a
father who left Germany over
50 years ago and died in this
country about 10 years after-
wards. Hubert himself has
never been outside Yic. and has
the warm blood of an Irish
mother to sweeten any sauer-
krautish fluid in his veins. So
far as his job is concerned, he
is considered the safest refuge for flurried
Clerks of the House who have been blown
off their course by some storm that calls for
a wide knowledge of May.
James Millhinch Bickett has gone pros-
pecting in the undiscovered country, aged
92. Born in Scotland, he reached Vic. in
1848, and was among the first diggers at
Golden Point. After the Stockade revolt
he was associated in the Local Court, a
tribunal of the time, with Carboni Rafaello,
an insurgent leader acquitted of high treason
at the trial of the Eureka men in Melbourne.
Bickett was the State’s oldest J.P., a legal
manager, and one of the founders of nearly
every public institution in Ballarat.
From senior-constable to lieut.-colonel is
the record of plain Bill Smith, who, before
the Kaiser broke loose, bought remounts for
the Vic. police. On October 22, 1914, he
went away as Director of Remounts on the
staff of the late General Bridges, and was
promoted captain and provost-marshal in
Egypt. He was slightly wounded in Gal-
lipoli, is now with “Birdy” in France, and
has secured more than one “mention.” His
son, Lieut. W. Smith, who is being invalided
home, has also some mentions to his credit.
Smith senior, on a recent visit to London,
yarned with George R. about a previous
acquaintance, the remount officer having been
in charge of the royal nags during the Aus-
tralian visit of 1901.
Dead in Adelaide, at 79, George Wilcox,
a S. Australian for the last 60 years.
Wool and hides were his strong point. He
has been for over 20 years out of the busi-
ness which he founded, but his sons, with
Glen Legoe, carry it on in more than one
State.
One hot-footer of Richmond (Vic.), a
single man, enlisted away back in Feb., ’l5.
His married brother, going to see him off,
was so affected that he enlisted too. He
got into the same battalion after a while and
in the same company, and a few months ago
the same Hun shell caught them both. Last
week they returned by the same boat, but
both left mementoes in France, the single
one several fingers, the other a leg.
A. E. Hamilton, lately put at the hea 1
of the Military Pay-office in Adelaide, is
about as unlike a majah as a m jor can be.
But he is an experienced accountant, and
brings effective methods into a department
which was a jest until a commission did for
it what the hopeless Pearce should have
done years ago. Hamilton’s level Scotch
head has room for sporting ideas, too, and
he owns one or two useful racers.
Died last month, aged 90, Mrs. Margaret
Jane Caldecott, relict of Alfred Millwater
Caldecott, who was brought out by the first
Provincial Government of South Aus. as
shorthand writer. He afterwards transferred
to the Immigration Department of the first
Vic. Government. With others he joined in the
Fiji cotton boom, which brought financial
disaster upon several Melbourne investors.
Then he moved to Mauritius, and died dur-
ing a voyage from that colony. Mrs.
Caldecott at 17 married a shipowner, Capt.
Young, who was lost at sea a month later.
This was about the time of the Gold Rush of
the ’fifties, and the young widow, with a
companion, Miss Blake, decided to try her
luck in Victoria. The two girls made the
journey from Quebec to Melbourne, where
Mrs. Young got employment under the Gov-
ernment, and thus met her second fate. Miss
Blake, now married and 80 years of age, still
resides at Benalla.

HE MIGHT CHANCE IT.
SHE : “If you were allowed only one wish
in the world what would it be?”
HE: “Haw! Oh, I sa-a-ay, if I— aw—only
dared to tell you!”
SHE: Oh, hurry up! Get a move on!
What do you suppose I brought up the
wishing subject for?”
The election of P. M. McGirr to the
N-S.W. Assembly adds to the list of Ma
State families with two or more members in
the same legislature. The first case on
record is the most remarkable. In the first
Peg. Council under responsible Government
(1856), 31 members were summoned to serve
for five years. Three of those called, Robert
Pitt Jenkins, William Pitt Faithfull and
James Robert Wilshire, were first cousins
the sons of three sisters (Esther, Sussana
and Jemima Pitt), who with their mother
and only brother. Thomas Matcham Pitt
emigrated hither in 1800. The Pitts were
cousins of Horatio Nelson, and all received
large grants of land for that reason. The
litts, Jenkinses, Faithfulls and Wilshires
took a useful and prominent part in the
p .arly development of the Ma State, and their
descendants still stick to the job.
A Ballarat pioneer who died the other
week was the father of the general sec. of
the Vic. P.L.C. Stewart, deceased, was 88
when he passed out. Although he was a
young miner on the. Ballarat field at the
time of the Eureka riot, he never persuaded
lnmself in later years that he had taken an
active part in it. He was strangely unim-
aginative on this point.
The book of Mrs. Hannah Page, of Wil-
liamstown (Vic.), was closely lately, and
local gossips noted that the old lady, who
arrived from Manchester in 1563, and mar-
ried a fisherman born in a whale-boat on a
voyage from Tasmania, had lived in one
street for 54 years.

A SONG OF UNNECESSARY SULPHUR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“It is not to be expected that machine-made men
•of the Teutonic pattern should 'possess souls.”
'Weary were we of the feast and song,
Of jest and tale and wine and revel.
The days were blue and the nights were long,
And a cheerless wight was the old, grey Devil.
King of Evil and Lord of Shame,
He limped through the dark, by all men slighted;
His sceptre of blood and his crown of flame
Were pawned for a wrong that a swift hour
righted.
Often at break of the night he sighed,
M ith the world’s thin sins of a weary gleaning,
And longed again for the full red tide
That surged when Hell and himself had meaning.
For the clash of spears and the murder-cry
And the padded steps of the traitor's creeping—•
But the fields of earth were parched and dry,
\\ ith a dull sneak-thief for his best day’s reaping.
The world was merry and clean and fair,
And a sweetness moved where the lands held revel,
Til] a sudden war-blast smote the air
And a ripple of gladness touched the Devil.
•Guns to the front and men to bleed ;
Ai d the cold world held its breath in wonder
At the lust to slay and the murder deed
That reddened the earth in the battle thunder.
Hell with its flames gaped hot and wide
lor the souls condemned at their fateful dying;
And the sulphur roiled like a flowing tide
1\ itli a scud of hate on its hot crest flying.
Bui. the harvest failed. And the Devil stood
With an idle pitchfork to aid the burning:
For out of the fields of slaughter rude
There were none that took the Ilellward turning.
The Devil pulled at his blistered horn.
“Great Scott,” he cried, “with the whole pit
heated,
A cold-foot creeps to me night or morn,
But of proper guests I am robbed and cheated.
“Briton and Frenchman and Russian fall,
And the men of every Allied nation;
But they answer Gabriel’s skyward call
ho die for a world’s regeneration.
“Damn my hoof!” cried the Lord of 111,
“The world is loud with the noise and stenches!
Where are the comrades my partner Bill
Had promised to send from the German trenches?”
Gun to gun made its bellowing call,
And bomb to bomb in the fierce red revel;
But Germany hadn’t a soul at all
For the lonely Hell and the waiting Devil.
“Damn my hoof and my horns and tail;
And damn the King that I helped and trusted!”
And the Devil looked blue and thin and pale
As he sat on a cindered star disgusted.

In 1906 the capital was £190,000 and the
reserves £100,422; total £290,422. Since
then £333,093 has been added to the reserves,
notwithstanding that a 10 p.c. dividend has
been maintained. There is nothing sordid
about that. Meantime, certainly, £310.000
of new capital lias been got in; but as
that also has had its little 10 p.c., it
doesn’t make the performance look any the
less fine from the shareholders’ point of
view. It hasn’t been done, however, without
incurring considerable liabilities. This table
shows the changes in the past 15 years:—
1902. 1912. 1916. 1917.
Deposits, etc. . £565,851 £1,764,141 £1,779,287 £1,671,702
Other liabilities 111,244 264,439 123,733 129,077
Premises, plant 47,942 144,10 l 137,105 154,921
Govt, stock and
cash .. .. 207,559 421,498 905,515 1,148,805
Advances, etc... 550,516 1,898,750 1,667,250 1,436,119
Other assets .. 115,697 218,166 46,664 79,863
Still, the increase in the liabilities doesn’t
mean anything like it looks in the first
line; for though they are altogether
£1,123,684 more, note that Government
stocks and cash are also £941,246 jnore.
Advances, etc. —this includes merchandise
stock and shares in other cos.—have in-
creased by £886,000; but practically the
whole of it has been provided out of new
capital and undivided profits. That, of
course, doesn't make the advances any the
less liable to depreciation. But the pastoral
finance co.’s commonest danger is not so
much depreciation of securities as having to
realise in a time of drought or low prices in
order to pay oft' a howling pack of anxious
depositors and other creditors. Thanks
largely, no doubt, to the war prices of the
past two years the co. was never so strong
financially as it is to-day ; for not only has
this exceptional market put money into the
pocket of the co., but it has put very many
of its clients in the happy position of not
wanting an uncle. Nine times out of 10
when pastoralists were making such profits
there would be a more or less wild rush to
buy runs at prices to correspond; and cos.
such as this would do most of the financing.
This is the 10th time; for even if there was
a disposition to plunge, which there isn’t,
banks and mortgage cos. would turn the cold
eyfe upon the enthusiast. So, though profits
are not going to continue on the present
scale, it seems to be certain that the co.. for
once in a way, is not increasing its risks
with its profits. They come, in short, not
from the finance side of the business, but
from the commission side —including agen-
cies, of which this co. has some very well
worth having.
The Federal Mutual Insurance Co. of
Australia (Sydney), which began with
£4039 of its capital eaten up by flotation
expenses:—•
Towards
June, Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.

1913.. £3425 Nil £3425 614
Further preliminary exes., £247 861
Reserves.
1914.. 1898 Nil 1898 1037
1915.. 4188 Nil 4188 5225
1916.. 6904 8 p.c. =£3076 3828 9053
1917.. 3965 8 „ „ 3099 866 9919
This co. was promoted by a man who knew
the business and had climbed high enough
in it to be N. 8. Wales manager of one of
the big English fire offices. But as a good
Australian lie was not content to see profits
going out of a country that badly needs all
the capital it can retain; so he threw up
his job and founded this co., and did it on
sound lines. Most young insurance cos.
make the mistake of buying big business
figures at a very heavy price, and, instead
of reckoning the cost as working exes.,
charge more or less of it against capital and
include this among their assets, calling if
“Business extension account" or something
of the sort. There is no such visionary
entry in this co.’s balance sheet. On the
contrary, even the preliminary exes, were
paid off out of profit as soon as that profit
was made. The result, is a remarkably
“clean” balance sheet and one of which any
young office might \ r ery well be proud.
Naturally in these circumstances the ex-
pense rate is rather high, yet not so high as
not to leave generally a fair margin of
underwriting profit:—
Net Expense Lost
Premiums. Expenses. Losses. Rate. Rate.
1915. . £25,520 £13,303 £9,636 52.1 p.o. 37.8 p.c.
1916.. 32,303 14,981 12,292 46.4 p.c. 38.1 p.c.
1917.. 35,356 17,942 15,508 50.7 p.c. 43.9 p.c.
For the past year the margin is down to
5.4 p.c. of the net premiums; but it was a
bad year from the insurance point of view
because of the heavy losses on hailstorm
risks in the N. S. Wales wheat-cocky dis-
tricts and the large number of losses through
fire-bugs in Sydney. Even so, with £2012
earned by investments, there was £866 to go
to unearned premium reserve after paying
the 8 p.c. dividend. It isn't as much to put
by as this paper would like to see; never-
theless the financial position remains quite
satisfactory. The net premiums for the 12
months amounted to £35,356. It is reckoned
that 40 p.c. of the year’s premiums should
cover an office’s unexpired risks, so that say
£14,100 should cover the Federal’s losses
—and, as against that the co. has £48,056 in
capital and reserves. The other factor is
the nature of the investments. Public secu-
rities and cash run to £17,206; and probably
there is some cash also in “sundry debtors
and agency balances, £8083.” Mortgages
account for £23,056. The other items are
negligible. And behind that, for the further
protection of policy-holders, there is £116,212
for which shareholders have made themselves
liable. One cannot say positively, of course,
what the future of any insurance co. will
be; but once a concern of the kind has
finished teething, there are few businesses
that show better returns. The heavy mor-
tality is in the cos.’ early years, and that
is mostly due to causes which this office has
studiously avoided. On all present appear-
ances it looks as though it has come to stay
and prosper.

This co. now does almost exclusively a com-
mission business: advances and all other
book debts on June 30 were only £47,717, and
even that is more than they were at either
of the previous two balances. One result
is that, notwithstanding a large investment
in recent years in a city freehold —freeholds
now stand at £125,874 —and notwithstanding
the co. has in cash and public securities
£42,632. the total of its liabilities is only
£33,856. It is very rarely indeed that a co.
handling pastoral products is able to show
such unimpeachable security for its capital.
Or. put in another way. it isn’t often that a
co. in this line has been able to build up and
hold so fine a business without drifting
much deeper into dubious pastoral-finance
transactions. No doubt conditions have
helped of recent years. Even so this record
of profit from a business conducted on these
lines is very admirable. To a great extent
it is due to the co.’s big hold on the fat-stock-
market. During the past two years the co.’s
yardings have been :
1915-6. 1916-7.
Cattle . . 148,864 149,457
Sheep .. 2,327,633 1,703,663
Though the turnover in sheep has been less,
prices (and consequently commissions) have
been higher; and there has been a nice bit of
extra commission, too, in the increase in the
average price of wool from £l5 2s. 4d. to
£l9 18s. Id. per bale. Whether any and
if so what provision has been made for such
contingencies as a retrospective war tax is
not visible from the published accounts.
“Rates and taxes, depreciation and all other
charges” are included in the one item of ex-
penses. which have increased from £38.058 in
the year ended June 30, 1914, to £43.670 in
the past year.

This co. has been getting it both ways—out
of the military trade and out of the civilian
trade through the Protection which the U
boats have been giving the overseas manu-
facturer. Out of the extra profit the State
has been taking its bite. Rates and taxes
were formerly lumped with “interest, dis-
count, salaries and general exes.,” and in
the year to the end of July, 1914, the lot
amounted to £10,144. In the past year in-
terest, discount, salaries and general exes,
were £6306. Assuming that they were £5OOO
in 1914, the increase in taxation has been
from £5144 to £12,886. This is a case in
which the assessment of war profits is exceed-
ingly difficult. How much of the extra profit
which the co. has unquestionably made has
been due to the increase in trade through
the substitution of Maoriland for foreign
goods? Suppose Mcßrown previously bought
a foreign tweed and this co. made 10s. profit
by supplying him last year with good home-
grown, home-made material, is there any
sense in taxing this co. on the extra 10s.
that it made? Haven’t we been wanting the
local manufacturer to get Mcßrown’s cus-
tom all along? But if, because of the war
and the U boats, this or any other co. added
30 or 20 p.c. to its prices and that much was
extra profit, then it is a fair thing that the
co. should disgorge the greater part of it.
The proposition seems to be one that cannot
be tackled in detail by an Act of Parliament.
The most that an Act can do equitably is to
lay down a few general rules and leave the
rest to taxation commissioners. That enor-
mous profits have been made through the
war is. certain —by shipowners and graziers
and miners. And it is a monstrous thing
that that war profit should escape taxation
because of the difficulty of the problem.

£115,350 since the war started ; nevertheless
it has been able to spend about £12.500 on
plant, etc., pay its 13 p.c. dividend and put
by over £38,000: —

shipping will be scarce for some time after-
wards, whereas the demand for civilian
clothes will be pretty extensive when Bill-
jim pomes home —especially if his deferred
pay is handed out promptly.

It isn’t much use trying to find out what
profit is actually made in any one half-year
For instance, this co., like all the other bi"
ones at Broken Hill bar the Sulphide Corp S
sells its concentrates to the Associated
Smelters, in which it is a partner. The
smelter co. isn’t a speculator in metals or
extractions; so it fixes what it regards as
safe prices for metals and makes an advance
cf 90 p.c. During the last couple of half-
years lead has been valued at £25 and silver
at 2s. So the North’s revenue last half was
made up of (1) 90 p.c. of the value of the
deliveries of silver and lead, silver being
taken at 2s. and lead at £25; (2) the sur-
pluses realised on the previous half-year’s
deliveries; (3) proceeds of sales of zinc
tailings to De Bavay’s. Big surpluses must
have been received on the previous half-vear’s
deliveries. Actually lead started at £3O and
has stood there or thereabouts ever since,
while silver (standard) lias steadily pushed
up from 3s. 04d. (average for January) to
4s. 14d. (present value). The current half-
year will be cut into by the strike, the
Broken Hill and some of the Port Pirie men
having decided that it will help the Rand-
wick and Eveleigh workshop employees to re-
sist the purely supposititious perils of a card
system by depriving Billjim of munitions in
France and Palestine. But the longer these
men knock off. work the higher the prices
will go and the bigger the surpluses which
the cos. will realise: so the cos. won’t be
punished to any serious extent.
There is another way of finding out
approximately what profit a mining co.
which has got into its stride is making. Look
at these figures:—

AUSTRALIAN ELECTRIC STEEL LTD.,“it
100,000 shares paid to 2s. Only 12 years ago
the surplus of assets apart from the mine
was under £5OOO. No new capital has been
got in since then, though there have been
plenty of waterings. Five'years ago, when
the Bailliens had got more say, the capital
was increased from £200,000 to £OOO,OOO by
the simple process of writing up assets.
Since then the assets have so far justified
the writing up that they have paid £840,000
in dividends; and probably a couple of hun-
dred thousands have been made and not
divided.
As to the future? The war, of course,
has given the co. some extra profits. Still,
we haven’t been able to do much
with our zincs, and the half-year immedi-
ately prior to the war showed a larger profit
than any subsequent period. Certainly
1.16.020 tons ore was handled then as
against 111.290 tons last half-year. Taking
the average at 130,000 tons per half-year
there is enough ore proved in the mine to
last something over 10 years. The divided
profit has of recent years averaged about
18s. per ton. But it would be dangerous to
value the 2,750,000 tons of proved ore on
that basis. What basis is safe this paper
doesn’t know. Over a long run of years
13s. 4d. has been realised; and there seems
no reason why the future should do w 7 orse.
That means £1,830,000 for the ore above the
1400 ft. level ; and during the past half the
main shaft has been sunk to 1539 ft., with a
view to presently finding out what is at
1550 ft. Add to the proved ore in the mine
the possibilities and the half-million of
liquid assets; and then reckon that the profit
from the proved ore won’t all be in hand for
10 years or so. There are 600,000 shares.
Silver (standard) has passed 4s. again : it
is at a higher point than at any period since
the great discoveries of the Hand and West-
ralia poured so much gold into the world
that the bimetallists’ old dream of making
silver legal tender along with gold, at a fixed
ratio, seemed to have vanished for all time.
But the world hasn’t been the same old
world during the past three years. There
has in the past been a vague notion, with
nothing much to support it, that £lOO of new
gold could sustain from £5OO to £7OO of new
credit. But with little more than
£100,000.000 of new gold coming into the
world per annum, credits have been growing
at the rate of at least £10,000.000,000;
and the voice of the silver-man is
heard again saying that his metal
must come to the rescue. To some
extent it is coming to the rescue: silver
is being poured out of the American mints,
and the Australian mints are not idle, either.
Meantime, the silver-using countries of Asia
—Japan in particular—are flourishing as
they never flourished before; and they are
taking large quantities of the white metal.
Hence the rise of nearly a shilling during the
past year. That something of the sort was
coming this column made more or less of a
pot-shot at predicting many months ago.
♦ * * *
From the other Pacific Coast: —
Ocean Oil Co., of Brisbane, has had its annual
report published in London and Los Angeles
(U.S.A.), though the Melbourne and Sydney papers
do not seem to have received a copy. Because of the
seerecv observed by Tom Whaley, the Californian
driller who bored for the Queensland co. near King-
ston (S.A.), very little has ever leaked out in Aus-
tralia about his work. It is now alleged that he
made some remarkable discoveries before the capital
of the co. became exhausted. Whaley’s report states
that every indication encourages boring to a greater
depth. The discovery of traces of oil and paraffin-
wax in the oil sands passed through proved to him
that it was only necessary to go deeper to get oil.
At 730 ft. the first bed of oil sands was encountered,
and it showed traces of heavy mineral oil. The
amount of paraffin-wax was equal to 5.61 b. to the
ton. At 1008 ft. more sand was traversed which
showed a residue of one-third grain of wax in 1750
grains of sand. At 1100 ft. two samples of a con-
glomerate' of blue shale and sandstone gave lOoz.
and 80oz. of pure wax to the ton. It is a pity that
the Ocean Oil Co. has not published the log of its
bore, and it is clearly up to the Minister of Mines
in South Aus. to insist upon this valuable informa-
tion being made public.

“Jaeko”: The inspired growl about the
bad treatment of the local molybdenite pro-
ducer, and the accompanying threat that
“with nothing more than the advance re-
cently offered (16 p.c.) the low-grade pro-
positions must remain unworked and tne
metals so necessary at the present time will
not bo procured from that soui’ce,” are in
pretty contrast to the joyous language of
the company-promoter. Recent prospectuses
yelled of the fortunes under a few feet of
dirt, gainable even if the recent quotations
fell, and marvelled at the indifference of the
public. In view of these conflicting state-
ments, the public may presently begin to
suspect that co. floaters are optimists!
* * . * *
The copper gougers at Phillips River
(W.A.) lately got a statement of the State
smelters’ third campaign—in the earlier
part of last year. They don’t seem to be
much the wiser for it. The works are rented
by the Government, which pays the owner
2s. per ton on all ore treated. The state-
ment seems to have got the buyers tangled
because special charges made against par-
ticular sellers are included with the out-
goings, as of course they should be, presum-
ing that they have been deducted from the
payments made to these particular ore-
suppliers. That should be made clearer.
The only other point which needs clearing
up is with regard to a very small item —
“Charges on parcels under 5 tons, £ll 11s.”
As The Bulletin understands it, any cus-
tomer who can’t send in five tons or more is
fined £1 Is.; anu how that comes to figure
among the outgoings this oaper can’t under-
stand. For the rest the statement seems
straightforward enough. The works treated
3385 tons ore, containing 216 tons copper,
20280 z. gold and 131 oz. silver. The gougers
netted £17.083. Or put it this way: they
netted £4 per oz. for their gold and £4l 10s.
per ton for their copper. The gross pro-
ceeds of the sales of metal were £39,694.
This was absorbed as follows: Smelting and
realisation charges, £18,415 (= £B5 ss. per
ton copper) ; concentrating and other
charges, including freight, interest and rent
(£323), £4199; balance available for sup-
pliers, £17,080, after writing off £668 of
preliminary exes. The whole concern is
under the management of Richard Shepherd,
who has had as much experience of smelting
—first at Broken Hill and afterwards at
Mt. Morgan and elsewhere —as almost any
man in Australia.
Someone lias roused Tom Duff and co.’s
ire by alleging that the new shoot in the
Edna May Deeps is going into the Central.
The drive on the reef is described in official
reports as north-east, and unless this infor-
mation is designed to hoodwink and mislead,
the workings must, it is declared, be in the
direction of the Central. An equally interest-
ing point is whether it will continue high
grade or not. This is of importance to both the
Central and Deeps: to the Central because,
if high values continue across the boundary,
a boom in share values is assured, and to the
Deeps because the longer and stronger the
shoot is the worse it will be for the bears,
judging by the way the Edna May shoot
has kept parallel with the Central boundary
in the upper levels, there is no reason for
believing that it is going to pass out of
Deeps at the present bottom level; but the
ore is so close to the boundary at that point,
and the shoot is so strong and rich, that
there doesn’t seem to be any reason why it
shouldn’t continue well into the Central. It
is not a case of the Central wanting to pick
the Deeps’ pocket, as some of the latter’s
barrackers imagine. It is only hopeful of
being able to line its own before long.

Mount Rankin is a Southern Cross
(W.A.) show picked out by Alec Mac-
George in the days of the Edna May boom
as a low-ferade proposition that needed
£lO,OOO to give it a start. The syndicate
didn’t then fructify, so the mine went to
one McGuinness, who by the*use of elbow-
grease and a deft tongue afterwards got
permission to float an £lB,OOO co. in 10s.
shares. After six months’ work under J.
H. Franks, 12,000 tons of ore are blocked
out on four sides between Nos. 1 and 2
shafts. The grade is from 21s. to 255. per
ton. Better stone was got in No. 3 shaft
till buck quartz knocked values out. Franks
is driving here, but has still to connect up
with his other two shafts. That done, he
works out that he will have 60,000 tons for
the battery whenever cash is available for
plant. His idea is to shove No. 2 shaft
down to get water and to see if anything
better lies underfoot. It is a good pro-
gramme. . Any other would most likely mean
that the concern would be kept going indefi-
nitely, with the help of occasional calls, for
the benefit only of the wages man and the
machinery merchant.
David Currie, of Lake and Currie, who
took over the Briseis for John Bull from
Bill Allen and A. E. Langford, has been in-
vited into the Board room. That’s better.
With Lindesay Clark at the mine it was ex-
travagance to pay a consulting engineer fees
in London ; albeit Currie has been useful in
the hunt after new properties. Briseis profit
in 1916 was £32,517 against £20,007 in
1915, all made with tin at about £l7O. This
current year the co. started with a reserve
of £50,000 intact and cash assets of £26,829
to set against sundry creditors £5441. Since
January tin has gone up from £lB6 to £246,
so that profits have accumulated in the face
of the bill for switching the Ringarooma
River to safety behind the Krushka fiat open-
cut. Once the big Ringarooma Hill is gone
Lindesay Clark will have six million yards
of drift to draw on, with estimated tin con-
tents of 3845 tons oxide. There are worse
mining possibilities when the world wants
tin.

Wallaroo and Moonta wound up on June
30 with surplus assets £248,341, after paying
three divs. of 2s. 6d. each, the lot amount-
ing to £60,000. This looks poor against the
surplus of £345,346 on .Tune 30, 1916, but
the comparison is better than appears. Thus,
after paying the divs., total assets were
£445,628 against £383,017, made up on these
lines: —•
June, 1916. June, 1917.
Cash items and war loan .. £70,441 £157,480
Copper in stock or transit . . 160,431 132,894
Surpluses in London . . 34,710 22,580
Stores, fuel, etc. .. .. 99,058 130,365
Sundry debtors .. .. 18,377 2,309
Liabilities a year ago were £37,671. On the
same basis they would be £41,287 this year;
but co. drags into the liabilities £156,000 as
a provision for contingencies. In other
words the co. put by that much as a fit and
proper contribution to war taxation because
of the w 7 ar price for copper. Bigjohn For-
rest’s Bill may, however, prevent the co.
doing its duty. In that case either the State
will start smelling around or the fund will
be made available for general purposes.
Underground better ore has made at 2910 ft.
off Taylor’s shaft at the Wallaroo. In the
Harnley mine, Moonta, the zone of low-grade
stuff has been left behind. Otherwise the
stuff hauled is of the old 3 p.c. grade.
w * * *
Clans McLeod and McKenzie, supported
by other kiltless Celts in Madden and
Meehan, are the largest producers of wol-
fram in Tasmania. As the owners of the
Story's Creek property they started off the
mark to develop with the war to help them.
A year ago their 100-ton-a-day mill began
on ore from Nos. 1 and 2 lodes, with a width
varying from 2ft. to 6ft. Running one shift
7300 tons have been treated for 133 tons
concentrate, roughly half wolframite and
half cassiterite. When chief McLeod went
north and south to gather bawbees to help
buy “The Creek,” Tassy’s mining folks were
full-handed; but the neighboring clan
McKenzie came to the rescue with the neces-
sary reinforcements, and noo a’ the profits
remain in the claelian. The mine, which is
away up on Ben Lomond, is said to have
several years’ ore proved.
* * * *
General-manager Sticht assures Mount
Lyell that Read-Rosebery ore is adapted for
treatment by the electrolytic process. That,
of course, is from the technical side ; it will
still be for the market value of zinc and lead
—zinc especially—to determine whether the
complex proposition is to hand out divi-
dends. The future of zinc is very uncertain.
It is all a question of the world’s powers of
absorption. Without a doubt a tremendously
increased supply will be available. While
the U.S.A. has almost doubled its plants,
Japan is making very rapid strides, and
Burma is becoming known as a zinc coun-
try. And Broken Hill, with its enormous
output of zinc tailings, is a competitor with
Read-Rosebery.
* * ■* *
Westralian Mines Department is now look-
ing for someone to take the broken-down
Santa Claus mine and plant off its hands.
Years ago a Perth co. tried, and failed, to
make monev out of it, and later another co.
also died in debt. This, however, did not
prevent an ill-informed Mines Department
from advancing a Kalgoorlie syndicate a
thousand or two to work it, nor the Water
Supply Department from spending a bigger
sum of money on a water-supply which no
one appears to have ever used.
* * « *
Westralian Chamber of Mines, acting on
behalf of Kalgoorlie and other mineowners,
has induced the British authorities to allow
the shipment and use of more powerful
explosives. Some time ago general dissatis-
faction was expressed at the poor class of
fracteur that was being sent here, and a
request was made by the Chamber that the
composition be 80 p.c. gelignite containing
50 p.c. nitro glychrine, and 20 p.c. standard
gelignite containing 61 p,e. of nitro glycer-
ine. The Chamber’s request was granted
except that gelatine dynamite is being em-
ployed instead of standard gelignite. The
former is really a more powerful explosive
than the mineowners in the West require.

GAS IN THE STOMACH IS
DANGEROUS.
PHYSICIANS RECOMMEND THE USE
OF MAGNESIA.
Sufferers from indigestion or dyspepsia
should remember that the presence of gas or
wind in the stomach invariably indicates that
the stomach is troubled by excessive acidity.
This acid causes the food to ferment and
the fermenting food in turn gives rise to
noxious gases which distend the stomach,
hamper the normal functions of vital inter-
nal organs, cause acute headaches, interfere
with the action of the heart, and charge the
blood stream with deadly poisons, which in
time must. ruin the health. Physicians say
that to quickly dispel a dangerous accumula-
tion of wind in the stomach and to stop the
food fermentation which creates the gas, the
acid in the stomach must be neutralised, and
that for this purpose there is nothing quite
so good as half a teaspoonful of bi&urated
magnesia taken in a little water immediately
after meals. This instantly neutralises the
acid, thus stopping fermentation and the for-
mation of gas, and enables the inflamed, dis-
tended stomach to proceed with its work
under natural conditions. Bisurated mag-
nesia is obtainable in powder or tablet form
Irom any chemist; but as there are many
different forms of magnesia it is important
that the bisurated which the physicians
prescribe should be distinctly asked for. —
