A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 10, 1917.
My Dear Arini, —
There’s a Michigan Uni. Professor of
Oratory resting here for a few weeks. With
his wife he’s on a year’s tour. Truebloou’s
his Cromwellian name, and he is large and
impressive, with a Quaker like softness of
manner. He moves with the dignity of a
continent and brings a wad of letters of
introduction. One is from ex-President Taft.
Of Michigan Uni. he says: “There are
SOOO students. Of that number about 1200
are women. It’s the first State-subsidised
University, and a youngster—it started in
1837—compared with Yale and Harvard,
which date back about 300 years.” Uncle
Sam puts sb much stress on oratory and its
near relatives, extempore debate and logic,
that Michigan devotes a chair to the sub-
ject. “Every seventh or Sabbatical year my
leave comes along. Last time I took it
early, and in 1912 was in Germany, amongst
other places. To Americans it was extra-
ordinary to see so many old men, women and
children gathering the harvest. When one
asked, “Where are all your young and
middle-aged men?” they replied: “Most busy
■ —drilling!” There’s honeyed sweetness in
the Michigan man’s postscript. “Well— we
are drilling, too.”
The big prof, wants to do something. As
he moves through Australia and Maoriland
he is eager to give Shakspearean recitals for
the Red Cross.
Killara fete day came in with a crash of
thunder and blew out in a wild westerly
gale. Yet thei'e’ll be a good deal over £2OOO
for the War Chest. A strike-time train ser-
vice made the trip a fine sample of Rugby
scrum.
Arrived at Killara the goffer goffed, with
Appy ’Ampstead roped off the sacred greens.
The westerly blew down a tent here and
there, and tore the feminine hat up by the
roots. Otherwise the damage was slight,
except to the patience of thirsty souls, who
formed long queues outside quite inadequate
tea-tents. Mrs. Maschwitz, in mourning for
a. son killed in Prance, stuck to her post,
with Mrs. J. E. Donohue at the ’baccy stall.
The Killara produce dames—Mrs. Budden
and Mrs. Perdriau to the fore—had their
roof blown off, but the day was cool and the
trussed fowl and pound o’ chops kept condi-
tion. Warrawee tent had Mrs. Percy Hardy
and her mates to sell groceries and look after
kitchenware. Roseville modestly sold towels
1 Mrs. Archbold in charge. Chatswood got
into the hand-me-down business—Mrs. Read
and friends here advanced for orders. Pym-
ble tried the miracle of loaves and tea-
leaves—Mrs. Buck and Mrs. Crossing as the
chief conspirators—on the multitude who
charged the vittles with the hunger born of
an adventurous journey. In the evening
John Brownlow was hon. concert manager
of a good musical show in the Golf House.
In a letter to a Sydney friend Mary
Strickland chats of ancient Malta, w’here the
delicate mother is now well enough to be
wheeled about in the garden. “Lord and
Lady Methuen lent us their wing in the
Palace, and moved out. Our house, Villa
, is just over the way, and we can go
over and watch the workmen complete the
alterations before it is ready for us We
can. hardly realise we are so far from Aus-
tralia and long to hear all the latest news.”
Goldie-locks is trying to add Maltese to her
stock of foreign tongues; but as its ety-
mology goes back to the days of the Phoeni-
cians it is hard to grip. “We had an
annual agricultural show,’ very tiny after
Sydney’s Easter carnival. They tether the
animals under trees in a little wood, and
there were _ some splendid plough-horses....
It's so quaint to watch the treading out of
the corn—an ox and a donkey or a horse
and a mule yoked together. We have also
been to a race-meeting! The animals run
uphill {that's the test in Malta), and the
riders sit bareback. Lady Methuen gave
away the prizes—great banners, which the
country people carry off with much pride,
and usually give to their village church. It
was all very picturesque, and there must
have been about 30,000 spectators.”
The Holman family on their way from
Here to There crossed the blue Medi-
terranean in a fast battle-cruiser, which
landed them in Italy, where they progressed
overland through France to Britain and
dinings-out.
Heber McMahon, grandson of the late
Macquarie Bond McMahon, who joined an
Irish regiment ’way back in the Big Tur-
moil’s early stages, has now a third star on
his cuff —he’s chemical adviser to an army
corps and boss of high explosives. A brother
is a captain in the Irish Guards. The
mother of the two boys stands by on English
soil.
Melba slipped through Sydney so quietly
that only a few pressmen and a little group
who hung round the wharf to see her off
had speech with her. John Lemmone oiled
the wheels. Our Sovereign Voice, with
her youthful mate, Lady Susan Fitz-
clarence, rumbled over tiie border in a
reserved carriage. They found, what many
less pampered travellers discovered long
since, that it doesn’t mean sudden death to
have no sleeper on the Melbourne express.
A “stretch out” on a seat isn’t a bad substi-
tute.
“Wenty” Bucknell—-officially Lieut. Wil-
liam Wentworth Bucknell. of the Field
Artillery—was killed in France the other
week. He was the eldest son of the well-
known Quambone station owner, and a fine
sample of young Australian manhood.
There’s a nice girl—one of the Ivatt family
beyond Bathurst—in tears.
After a blizzard of presentations, includ-
ing a diamond boomerang, Dorothy Brunton
is now really and truly packing up for the
U.S.A.
Now a bomb has fallen out of the Post
Office among the Comforts dames. Even
before the Christmas mail starts to pour in,
900 tons of mail matter has banked up in
Australia, and the Post Office doesn’t see a
hope of getting it away for months and
months. Such things are so severely cen-
sored, otherwise the public wouldn’t be sur-
prised to know that practically the only mail
route open now is via America; and that is
almost impossible for parcels. P.M.G. Web-
ster can’t see any other way out —wherever
possible, send money to England and have
the goods forwarded from there. One thing,
however, we ouvht to send, no matter what
else is crowded out. Socks! There’ll be
dreadful suffering and loss from trench feet
if we can’t keep that service going through
the winter.
Young Fitzhardinge, a schoolboy who died
a few days ago, following on a side-
car accident, was a grandson of Dr. Clubbe,
the children’s specialist. But no skill could
save the little sufferer, who lingered only a
few hours.
Next week the
Royal Art Society
will hold its an-
nual show. If in
its 38 years it has
often been too
kind and given
space when it
might have shown
the way to the
door, here is the
man who doesn’t
mind shouldering
some of the blame.
For 20 years presi-
dent of the Society,
W. Lister Lister is
an incurable opti-
mist who can see
a glad to-morrow
for even wooden-
heads in an art class. Over his studio
should be written, “Abound in hope all ye
who enter here.” Most genial of mentors,
he tempers the bleak wind of criticism year
in, year out, to the lambs.
Born in Sydney, a son of John Armitage
Lister, of Brunteliff, Yorkshire, he went to
England as a youngster when the family
packed up for the other side. Educated at
the Bedford Schools and at Pont St. Max-
ence (France), W. L. Lister was an exhibi-
tor at the Royal Scottish Academy at 17
and at Manchester and other English gal-
leries before he bought his first razor. But
Australia called, and he humped his color-
box back to his native land before rapping
at the door of the Royal Academy. And
here he has taken root. Addicted to expanses
of creamy, sand, with meditative seagull
in attendance, and big bush canvases with
giant gums glinting in a gorgeous sunset,
his. work hangs in our National Gallery, of
which he is now one of the trustees, and
in the galleries of Adelaide, Maoriland and
Geelong. Winner of the prize offered for
the best picture of the Federal Capital site,
and a prolific painter (his annual bill for
frames must be a heavy tax on his modest
profits), Lister is one of this city’s most
persistent helpers of lame pups over the art-
stile.
Mrs. George Black, after putting up a
brave fight, has been carried home from a
private hospital. Yet she’s still concerned
about the finishing of some War Chest work
before the Call comes.
One of the Makinson boys, a lieut., was
wed in London lately. He gave his arm to a
Sydney girl, Mary Gibbes, who has been
pegging away at war work on the other side.
Ciceley Hughes, younger daughter of
the late John, M.L.C., has accepted a dia-
mond keepsake from Major Curtin, of the
Army Medical Corps.
The spiders will soon get a move on _at
deserted Cranbrook. The roomy mansion
which languishes under the forlorn title, “ex-
Government House for State Governors,” is
to be lent for a double-banked party on Sep-
tember 21. The afternoon will be for juven-
iles to frolic as they list in the sunny gar-
den. At nightfall the flapper will dance
with whom she can —grandpa is not excluded
—and the chaperons will play bridge up-
stairs. Funds to the War Chest.
The R.A.S. presi-
dent is Joyful over
Muir Auld’s progress
in oils. “He’s found
a market in Tiie
Bulletin for black-
so it’s
been au revoir to
pot-boiling. Hence his
best record at this
1917 exhibition.” The
son of a Scotchby-
terian parson who
used to expound the
W ord at Ashfield,
young Auld studied
awhile with J. S.
Watkins. Then to
London, where be-
tween drinking in the
best in the famous
art galleries he contributed sketches to the
Windsor, London Opinion and other mags.
The Adelaide gallery and our own National
temple hang some of his efforts in oils.
It was a very quiet knot-tying at Hamil-
ton when Myra Shelley was handed iby
her father to Ernest Philip Collins, of Can-
delo. Mrs. Trefle gave niece Myra her
foamy bridal gown of lily-white drapery
and gossamer lace. One of the Shelley boys
was lately killed at the Front, so there was
only a small gathering in the parental nest
after the ceremony.
The Symphony Orchestra—conductors Joe
Bradley and Slapoffski—shook the Conserva-
torium rafters the other afternoon. After a
Debussy Prelude, Ella Caspers appeared in
a pink frock with Hugo’s ballad, “La Fiancee
du Timbalier,” set to music by Saint-Saens.
To the Wars,” warbled Ella, who got some
sweet peas and daffodils for her pains—“to
the murd’rous combat appalling.” An inter-
val of ten minutes gave the Highbrows time
to get into a suitable frame of mind for the
big symphony in C Minor. Glazounoff was
the composer, if that's any comfort to you.
The horrible cruelties of this war! Soon
after it started “Tim” Henderson, a Manly
boy, was married to Vi Robey, of the family
that has been associated with the Village
nearly all its days. Then Tim went off to
do his bit; and his little wife left for the
other side to be as near as she could get. A
few weeks ago Tim was sent back to Aus-
tralia with an eye and a limb missing. His
wife had to stay behind—she couldn’t get a
passport, and she couldn’t have travelled
then even if the leave was given. Now-
trouble heaped upon trouble —word comes
that the baby is dead.
Perth sassiety, with Mrs. D. G. Gawler
and Mrs. H. Hill in the van, runs an every-
Wednesday luncheon and tea-room, which
nets about £BO a month for trench comforts.
Nicholson and Co. lend a gorgeous show-
room, and Mrs. Frank Davis provides a
musical programme to help down the cold
beef and salad and afternoon-tea. All the
prettiest girls, dressed a la Hollandaise, float
round with trays and things.
Cyril Monk’s home at Mosman has vocal
music to rival daddy’s fiddle, for a very new
lifts up her voice at morn and eve.
(P-k- —I have no information about noon.)
Artist Mary Edwards and her mother are
oft to Tahiti for some months. They are
going to camp under the cocoanut to give
Mary who is young and primed with South
bea romance—a chance to paint Miss
h uzzy \\ uz in her native state.
Having decided to raise £lOO for the War
Chest. Sydney Sketch Club is setting about
the task in an artistic manner. It is com-
piling a of original sketches—pen
pencil crayon and water-color—which will
be raffled among a hundred guinea sub-
scribers. The lucky winner will receive
an imposing volume, bound in a hand-
embossed leather cover, and containing work
by most of the leading Australian artists

The Actors’ Benevolent Fund matinee
ought to bring a nice plate of refreshment
to a bare cupboard. Her Majesty’s was
packed. “Caste” fell like a wet blanket on
the opening of an otherwise brisk show. Is
it the fault of the comedy, or does Cyril
Maude’s drawing-room version of Esther
Eccles’s hilarious old dad drown the mirth’
Stephen Scanlan’s Sam Gerridge and Frank
Elliott’s good work as Hawtree saved this

melancholy of a wet day in strike time
Marie Tempest and the nimble Graham

little lot and a Tivoli contingent backed up

The men of Yass and Canowindra and
Young and Forbes and Wagga who are down
here saying the country were bidden out of
the habitat of the elephant and the hornbill
on Saturday evening to a melodious srnoke-
oh at the Mosman Bowling Club. Mrs
Alec Thomson and Miss Creagh had snared
the musical talent of Mosman for the pro-
gramme and a smiling cluster of V.A.D.’s
for the commissariat. Colonel Spain shep-
herded his flock, and the president of the
club and member for the district Percy
Colquhoun rounded off the angles and made
the large congregation quite at home.
Stella Miles Franklin, of “My Brilliant
Career” and other writings, is toiling in a
new sphere. After several years in Chicago
as an ardent suffragist, and a year or more
of she is now working with the
Scottish Women’s Hospital somewhere in
Macedonia, and her immediate O.C. is Dr.
Agnes Bennett, of this town. Several other
Sydney women are attached to the same unit,
including Dr. Helen Sexton, who was deco-
rated the other day for work done among
the Servian sick and wounded.
The kiddies’ matinee ball, organised by
Rose Lyons for France’s Day, brought in
£lOO. The French Consul took delivery.

HIS Animated Advertising Machine, shown in the right-hand
top-corner, is the smartest advertising money-maker yet
offered to live advertising people. It is an American inven-
tion, and has been remarkably successful in America and
Canada. It is automatic in its working, and is specially
adapted for advertising in shop-windows and first-storey windows,
showing slides 14 inches by inches in length, the figures moving
like human beings. For first-storey window ads. the pictures can be
screened 7ft. by Bft. in length, giving an actual motion-picture of the
advertised article. The machine is specially fitted to be attached to
ordinary electric connections. Bach machine carries 30 slides or
advertisements, and each slide is thrown on the screen for
15secs., which means that each ad. is displayed 30 times on a run of four hours. No attendant
is necessary, and the cost of current is under 2d. per hour. The agent securing the machine
can offer space to advertisers at a ridiculously low rate, and give 100 per cent, efficiency adver-
tising, while big profits may be made for himself. While in America a few weeks back, Mr.
Parker secured the Australasian rights, and now offers them to agents in the different States of
the Commonwealth and New Zealand.
Big Picture-Theatre Proprietors should see the machine, as it is particularly good for
large-screen advertising. The big picture-theatre people in America all have machines.
Set in a handsome shadow-box, the finely-colored pictures shown by this Animated
Advertising Machine make the shop-window brilliantly attractive in the daytime, night-time,
and all the time.

Melbourne, September 10, 3917.
My Dear Yarrie.
The Melba departure from Spencer-street
was a Great Spectacle. Nellie stood like a
rock in a billowing sea of “Con.” girls,
friends, florists’ messengers, telegraph boys
and frenzied outsiders. She looked posi-
tively girlish, and that takes some doing on
a north-wind day in Melbourne. Much
befurred, with her arms full of Spring prim-
roses and boronia, she bade good-bye to pals,
admonished her pupils to be diligent, gave
final instructions to secretary Freda Stern-
berg, and murmured thanks for gifts thrust
upon her. Lady Susan Fitz Clarence dimpled
her good-byes prettily, saying she was “really
sorry to leave Melbourne,” while staunch
supporters in the persons of John Sander-
son. George Allan, Italian Consul Eles, and
Tommy Cochrane stood behind heaps of bag-
gage wearing photographic smiles. Mrs.
Hughes and Lady Doris Blackwood buzzed
about the farewell riot. Pianist St. Leger
took affectionate farewells of stage-manager
Bigo, who stays behind as first gentleman-
in-waiting to the Coombe Cottage fowls and
dogs. Melba is ready for her entree in the
musical world of America, and can step on
the stage of the Metropolitan Opera House
garbed as Marguerite or Mimi at a moment’s
notice.
The Munro Ferguson pair invited 40
guests to a dinner frivol on Thursday night
to amuse niece Doris. After the coffee and
cigarettes the young people two-stepped in
the entrance hall.
Senator Pearce and his wife gave a dance
on Friday night. It was a young people’s
farewell to the Maribyrnong camp and Fly-
ing School boys, and the young Pearce
daughters had pretty new dresses. Marjorie
wore heliotrope and Dorothy silver net and
lace. Mrs. Pearce got into black silk net
fixings. The Forrest dame, Airs. Billy
Hughes and Mrs. Sands went as social over-
seers, and Muriel North attended to enjoy
herself like the two Baillieu girls and
Marion Bridges.
St. Vincent’s Ball was one of the big
events of the season. It was labelled
'Plain, Picture and Poster,” but thez-e was
nothing plain about it except the crow-like
garb of some of the men. The girls who
were not in fancy dress had lovely evening
gowns they w’anted to show. Airs. St. Leger
and Airs. Merson Cooper spent half the day
getting things ready at the St. Kilda Town
Hall, and then appeared at hostess-time
fresher than paint. Airs. St. Leger's gown
was a delicate lilac color and Airs. Alerson
Cooper wore nigger-brown and cream in
stripes with a cross-section of white tulle.
The 21-year-old daughter wore her birthday
frock. The Forrest dame, in black, had her
irrepressible niece Aluriel dressed as Hail
Columbia. Airs. Billy Hughes wore black
and white and her roundest spectacles.
The Alerson Coopers turned their house
upside-down for the coming-of-age of their
only daughter. There were 150 guests, in-
cluding the Simon Frasers, the Cleggs, the
Aladdens, the Fenners and the Alortons. A
little batch of French naval officers were the
spice, and many of the available home-ser-
vice military and naval chiefs rolled along in
uniform. A platoon of adoring young
soldiers helped with the dancing. It was a
party for which most of the women got new
dresses—a notable event these times. The
young hostess was enveloped in a dream of
rainbow pinks and silver, and got loads of
presents, including a new runabout from
dad. (The young woman drives her own
cai’.) The guests danced and played cards
and champagned in the intervals.
The popular Stevenson sisters are mourn-
ing one of their number, dark-eyed Bhoda,
who died very suddenly last week. They are
daughters of the late Geoi’ge Stevenson, of
the Lane.
A pretty little well-dressed American
lady blew into Alelbourne lately in the per-
son of Airs. S. A. Bansora. Hubby is—-or
was—Public Health Officer at Shanghai for
the U.S., and is going to France via N’York.
She is setting sail shortly in hopes of seeing
him before he goes off to look after some of
Uncle’s “Sammies.”
Dr. Carty Salmon is seriously ill. He
overdid it in the early stages of the war.
The Horsfall widow is back from her Syd-
ney trip and perched at the Oriental preen-
ing new feathers, which include fascinating
ivory and putty-colored cloth suits. These
are billed as “half-mourning,” and will go
with Spring hats that are not sable.
There’s a warm welcome waiting for one
padre, Chaplain (Colonel) J. Crookston, of
St. Andrew’s, Bendigo, whose health com-
pels him to come home after 18 months’
active service. He has two sons still at it —
Sergeant Keith, with the Light Horse ; and
flying-man (late Gunner) Bruce.
A belated mail brings news that Captain
(acting-Major) Percy Bobert Murdoch
Collins, D. 5.0., one of the two soldier sons
(both decorated) of 11. M., was killed in
France on June 25. Born in 1891, his Eng-
lish public school education was topped off
with a course at the Boyal Military
Academy. He got his commission in the
Boyal Garrison Artillery in 1910, and in
that year was one of the team which de-
feated Sandhurst at football under the eyes
of Lord Boberts. Three years’ service at
Hongkong and Canton was the prelude to
his two years in France with a heavy siege
battery, of which he was in command when
the end came. Within a fortnight of his
going to the Front in 1915 he married
“Dodie,” youngest daughter of tlie late
Bichard Grice.
Months and months ago I told you of a
life-saving drill and a boat in which Miss
Strickland (matron) and another nurse were
seated, and of a shark waiting in the water
below—and then of the canting of the boat
through defective tackle, and two minutes
of breathless excitement. Miss Strickland
is matron of the hospital ship Kanowna. and
the chief-officer assures me it couldn’t have
happened on that ship because its tackle is
never faulty:—
I am proud and glad to say that in all our many
drills not the slightest hitch has occurred. The
tackles on our boats are systematically overhauled
every trip and every possible care is taken.
Then, for the further comfort of the friends
of sick and wounded Billjim, the chief tells
more of the practice of the hospital ship :
The plan followed is to let nurses and badly-
wounded or sick patients into the boats at the
promenade deck. The rest get into the boat when
she's in the water. To enable them to do this
ropes and rope-ladders are kept ready for use at a
moment’s notice. Thus, as little stress as possible is
put on the boat-falls, hooks, etc., and the danger of
accident accordingly minimised.
Lieut. Frank Lascelles, son of the Geelong
family, has been killed in action. His father
died last year, and an elder sister was lost
with the Waratah.
Sister Adelaide testifies: —
Delegates from the Children’s Welfare Associa-
tion of Vic. are with us for three weeks to swap
opinions on infant life protection. Our State
Children’s Council, of which we are proud, has
nearly 1900 embryo citizens under its direct control
and as many more living with accredited foster-
mothers.
Tlie governors of St. Peter’s College (C. of E.)
are going to provide hursaries for sons of old
scholars who lose their lives in- the war. Inci-
dentally, forgetting old rivalries, a party of Peters
and Alfreds, 160 strong, foregathered for dinner at
Amiens early in July. Ambulances took the old-
hoys of our two big schools from their billets to the
blow-out, which was “excellent and exquisitely
served.”
A tablet has been unveiled at the University to
the memory of the late Dr. Barlow, for 19 years vice-
chancellor. The donor was his sister, Jane Barlow,
of Ireland.
This yea r Unley is to be denied its usual dissi-
pation of a mayoral reception, and the money that
would otherwise be wasted will honorably go to
swell the Bed Cross Fund.
H. M. Muirhead, Registrar of the Industrial Court,
has followed his three brothers into khaki. Wilfrid,
the second of the F. 11. Snow sons, has got his
majority in the Royal Flying Corps and has been
given command of a squadron in France. Another
South Australian to go up a step is A. Iv, Wendt —-
captain.
The Anglican Synod has set a time-limit for
speeches —lo minutes for the proposer of a motion
and five minutes for his bottle-holder. Now, if the
scheme ,were applied to the pulpit, wouldn’t that be
a dream!
Port Pirie produced some imposing wharf-laborers
at the beginning of the week, when Capt. Davis
(who went to Antarctica with the Aurora), the
Mayor (Sir. J. S. Geddes) and the Rev. H. F. Severn
lent a hand in getting 400 tons of cargo off the
Bulga. The parson is a nephew of the Ivennions,
now at Bath and Wells (Eng.), and at Bishopcourt
here before the Harmers’ day.
Another Rhodes Scholar, Howard Rayner, son of
the Rev. G. C. Rayner of Glcnelg and formerly of
Homebush (Sydney), is in an officers’ school in Eng-
land. Ilis brother, who for months was dumb from
shell-shock, has got back his speech.
What Lady Galway described as “a preliminary
canter to a bigger undertaking later on” was held
at the Alexander McCulloehs’ house in North Adel-
aide on Thursday—a garden fete to raise funds for
Navy Day in October.
Another A.I.F. lost to ns! Lieut. B. J.
Wells, of our Murchison, now on service
abroad, is to marry Marjorie Hall, of I' resli-
fiold Ilall, Brighton, England.
Joyce, only daughter of the Arthur
Cohens, of St. Kilda-road, has accepted a
diamond solitaire from an English officer,
Lieutenant Gerald Kaye, of the 1i.1.A. The
wedding will probably be in England.
Mrs. Fred Fairbairn is crazy to get to
England for daughter Jess’s marriage, but
she cannot scare up another passport. This
is a horrible deprivation for such a con-
firmed world-tripper. Jess —the sporty
youngster with the bright complexion——is to
marry Lieut. Bussell, son of the sheepy
Philip Bussells, of Victoria. A cable says
the wedding will be soon.
Jack Fosbery, of Dalgety’s shipping
braifch, is busy buying lavender gloves and
top hat for his wedding-day. Dorothy Garnet
is to be Mrs. Jack.
There is a terrible fuss about the marriage
of the little Kent Hughes girl. Papa, of
course, has been in Egypt through the whole
hurried course of engagement and marriage.
Captain B. S. Finn, D. 5.0., of Maori-
land, won his distinction on Gallipoli in
August, 1915. There were two Captain
Finns then attached to the Australians, but
B.S. was the one who obtained the
D.S.O. He has since been in France, and
is now in England. Dolly Castles, now in
New York, is engaged to a Captain Finn,
who is also, she says, a D. 5.0.; but Dolly’s
fiance is not the Maori la no officer. The
Alaorilander is tired of being congratulated.
Make a note of it.
Lady Helen took the chair at a meeting
of the Council of the Australian Bed Cross
at Government House last week. The Coun-
cil adopted the request of the over-sea Com-
missioners for an additional £25,900 reserve
fund and an increase in the monthly remit-
tance to £12,000. Colonel Murdoch had
begged, from London, for increased supplies
of foodstuffs for prisoners of war. The
Cairo Commissioner begged for more* fruit,
milk, cocoa, books, pyjamas and old linen
for Egypt. A new and very large Australian
stationary hospital is now planted in the
desert, and it needs vast supplies. Also, the
number of field ambulances in Egypt are
double those of last year.
Miss B. Kaighin, of Melbourne, and Miss
Park Taylor, of Brisbane, are on their way
to Egypt armed with official appointments
as Bed Cross voluntary cooks for the kitchen
attached to the Australian Base Hospital at
Cairo.
Amy Matchett, author of the “Coo-ee Aus-
tralia Souvenir Booklet”—a collection of
patriotic album-verse full of kindly senti-
ment —reminds Billjim’s friends and rela-
tives that the literary trifle can be obtained
at Norman Bros.’, Mullen’s and Cole’s. If
paper, printing and binding cost nothing, the
lady would be delighted to have the whole of
the A.I.F. reading free copies. The proceeds
of the local sale are wholly devoted to the
supply of the booklet to Billjim.
Etcher John Shirlow has been invited to
become an associate of the Boyal Society of
Painter-Etchers, London. Characteristically,
his native Victoria does nothing officially to
recognise him. The fine black-and-white col-
lection in the Melbourne Gallery contains no
print with his signature. Lately he has
been essaying portrait studies, one of his
sitters being poet Bernard O’Dowd ; another
B. H. Croll, of Hawthorn. Next month a
volume of reproductions of some of John’s
best work will be issued by Angus and
Bobertson.
Artist Hughie Paterson is seriously ill in
a private hospital. The hearty, energetic
man went down with a paralytic stroke some
weeks ago.
The Lindsay show is just open at the
Fine Art Society’s Galleries. Alfrcd-place,
off Collins-street. Brothers Norman, Lionel
and Percy have an exhibition of water-color
paintings and etchings at that address, and
art lovers are flocking in to pay their
respects.
The late Franklin Peterson’s collection of
books, music and pictures is now on view at
the Fine Art Society’s Booms. The collec-
tion includes first editions, beautifully
engraved, of Bach, Beethoven, Llandel and
other composers; autographed letters from
Spohr, Berlioz, Bulow. Pinsuti, Saint-Saens
and Mendelssohn; and a large number of
Covent Garden programmes, dated about the
beginning of hast century, including those of
the first English performances of Mozart’s
operas. The musical soul of the period
apparently insisted upon good value, for one
programme offered “Fidelio,” followed by an
Auber opera, “the whole entertainment to
conclude with a well-known farce.”

The Grand is having a little strike all on
its own. Eight of the waitresses downed
trays because they objected to buy shorter
aprons, at 2s. each, to match their new uni-
forms. If the girls will only solve the
trouble by taking a loaf out of the Book of
Genesis, business will boom. (You remem-
ber the earliest fashion-note. If you don’t
you can ask the curate next time he calls.)

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
“Bunyip”: “E.S.S.” (B. l(i/8/’r7) asks
for our 12 best songsters. Well, here goes: Magpie,
butcher-bird, drop-lark, reed-warbler, white-throated
flycatcher, golden wren, blue wren, blue thrush,
mountain-thrush, bellbird, brown cuckoo, ground-
lark. P.S.—-I am 6ft. 3in., weigh 13 stone and have
a reach of 76in.
“Pandamis”: “H.V.E.” (B. 10/5/TT) is
certainly wrong when he says that the death-adder
uses its tail as an anchor when about to attack,
unless the Westralian specimen differs from that
located in North Queensland. I have killed a great
many in the neighborhood of Cape York, and have
experimented with most of them before killing to
see how they bit. Touch one on the middle of the
back with a stick and like a flash the head and tail
strike at the same moment.
“H.W.F.”: Nothing said alters the fact
that I saw a white-ants’ nest used as an oven in
1878 at a railway-survey camp between Cooktown
and the Palmer (Q.)- The plan I knew was to
select an ant-mound the size needed, cut a square
doorway near the bottom and burn out the inside.
Then clean out the ashes and use either ants’-nest
casing or flat stones for the hearth. Put in fire of
dry wood and let it burn out, clean away the ashes,
put in the dough and affix the door to the opening.
There are several of these ovens in use (or were) in
Gippsland Mountains. My present fireplace is lined
for 6ft. up with ant-hill covering, and scores of
other fireplaces are similarly improved to my know-
ledge.
“Tarltou-Il.”: “Dominie Sampson” (B.
16/8/’17) is not the only one bushed about the ti-
tree. Some highly decorated botanists are no
better off. James F. W. Johnston, M.A., mentions
the ti-trees—he calls them tea-trees ( Leptospermum
scoparium and L. thea)— as two of the many sub-
stitutes for the china tea-plant ( Thea viridis). The
Australian vegetable belongs to the Myrtacece, and it
is worth mentioning that two other members of this
order butt into the tea business — Gtaphyria nitida
in Sumatra ; and in Chili Myrtus ugni is used as a
substitute for Paraguay mate. With what one might
call pot-luck the Paraguayonions selected the leaves
of the native holly ( Ilex Paraguayensis) to brew into
tea, and now chemists tell us that the theine of this
plant is identical with that of Souchong, Twankay
and other China crops.

“E.S.S.”: “Dominie Sampson” says (B.
E 6/8/’ 17) that “tea-tree” should be spelt “ti-
tree.” He is wrong. The ti-tree is distinct, and is
a native of Maoriland. About 16 years ago, on the
suggestion of the late H. J. McCooey, the Secretary
for Lands in N. S. Wales issued a circular to sur-
veyors and draughtsmen, instructing .them that the
word which they had been in the habit of writing
on plans as “ti-tree” should in future be written
“tea-tree.” It was then established that Captain
Cook and his men had made tea out of the dried leaves
of the Australian vegetable, and had named it tea-
tree. As the shrub is not related to the tea-tree of
commerce (Bohea), and is quite different from the
ti-tree, and also because a Queensland aboriginal
name of the white-flowered species is teedoo, I spell
it “tee-tree” as a compromise.
* * * *
“Kookaburra”: The East-West line
threatens to add “sparrow” to the alliterative “sin,
sand, sorrow and sore eyes” that is supposed to sum
up the West. Months ago the advance-guards were
reported to be spying out the land. To check the
coming invasion headquarters here in Westralia
decided to ignore international law and poison the
food and drink supplies along the line of attack.
Latest report is that a lusty young cock-sparrow
was observed at the end of the S.A. section heading
straight for us. I happened across a possible solu-
tion in Perth. Passing a chicken dealer’s I heard a
lady gurgle, “The dear!” I turned and saw “the
dear” in a cage waiting for young Westward-Ho to
come and claim his little brown bride.
“59 : My word, there was trouble last
time mum went to town 1 She was steering down
Collins-street keeping a wary eye for Fiji uncles and
white-slavers, when a clerical-looking party breasted
up and handed her a card, on which was printed,
"Behold, I am with you always.” It took three
policemen to divorce mum from Ebenezer’s throat.
“Eloc”: Nine out of 10 sheep that have
their eyes pecked out by crows die of blood-poison-
ing. There’s an easy and almost sure cure in a
bottle of Condy’s Fluid. Pour a little in the eye-
hole. An essence-of-lemon bottle will hold enough
for about a dozen. But have the mixture strong.
* * * *
“Leo Lear”: If a vote was taken to dis-
cover Australia’s favorite bird the winner would
probably be a wren, and there wouldn’t be many of
the family ahead of the blue branch (Malurus
cyaneus ) of S.E. Australia. This wee, frail mite,
with perky tail and legs thinner than violet stalks,
would hold his position for a combination of beauty!
usefulness and song, as well as for gentle nature and
friendliness. The blue wren belongs to a large
genus distributed throughout the continent, and in
all cases, while the males are bedecked finely
generally blue heads and black backs—the ladies are
clothed in sombre brown. A cousin of the superb
warbler is a Bananaland beauty. This is the orange-
backed wren with a jet black head and body and
brilliant scarlet back. He has my vote for beauty,
but loses points otherwise. This genus are falseiy
charged with being Mormons. The fact is this. The
males only attain full plumage in old age, and many
of the dowdy clan of hangers-on always seen in the
bright-colored male’s company are immature gents.
Other Australian wrens belong to different genus,
and dress with a quiet, Puritanical touch. They are
all useful citizens, abhorring won’t-work tactics,
snakes and cats. Best known is the streaked-brown
field-wren, cursed by quail shooters when a dog
sets ’em, and blessed by all who love a sweet
song. Then there are the scrub-wrens, about a
dozen of them, all timid and hard to locate in the
undergrowth. The rare grass-wrens of the interior
I’ve not been introduced to, but to the last wren of
all, the emu-wren, I lifts me lid.
KNOWLEDGE IS POWER.
THE REINFORCEMENT : “Say, Digger, wot
ther ’ell do you, usually do during a bom-
bardment like this ’ere?”
THE ANZAC : “Oh, play cards.”
“Norseman”: Coming up from Wilcannia
to Wanaaring- (N.S.W.) I dropped in at a woolshed
dance which was to Win the War —at least, such
was its stated object. All was ready, floor greased,
women ditto, but no musician till I saw a chap
being led in with a concertina. He was a Darling
abo. who can play guitar, lyre, mandolin, mouth-
organ, violin, harp and other instruments. He was
a shy black and never once raised his face to look
at the hoppers, and at the finish of each dance he
bashfully retired, though many a white with much
less musical skill would have remained to put on
dingo.

BIDING SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All the men were up and doing long before the break
of day,
And we saddled up and mounted when the east was
turning gray;
Then we ambled forth in column and we knew that
. we were free
As we rode from Tel-el-Farali to the sandhills by
the sea.
It was pleasant, easy going,
And the stars were faintly showing
On the road from Tel-el-Farah to the sea.
Oh, the thought of take-it-easy caused the light of
joy to leap
Into eyes worn red and weary by long nights of
broken sleep,
For the heavy hand of duty was compelled to let us
be
When we rode from Tel-el-Farah to the rest-camp by
the sea.
Oh, the beach was wet and hazy,
And the waves broke loud and crazy
Down the road from Tel-el-Farah to the sea !
Every eager horse responded to the welcome of the
surf,
And they raced across the sandhills as they’d
crossed the level turf.
And the horsemen soon dismounted in disorder, mixed
with glee,
Twenty miles from Tel-el-Farah, at the horselines by
the sea.
Horsemen took their mounts a-swimming.
And the cup of joy was brimming
Twenty miles from Tel-el-Farah, by the sea.
Now the languid days are over and the call is stern
and plain ;
We must saddle up and ration for the danger zone
again;
We must get our things together and go hard and
hurriedly
Up the road to Tel-el-Farah from the sandhills by
the sea.
For the battle-fires are burningi-
But we’ll shortly be returning
Down the road from Tel-el-Farah to the sea.
PALESTINE.
GERARDY.
“Bill Bowyang”: Binghi is a pathetic
figure when anything goes wrong with his health,
but lie’s not in the same paddock as the Gippo
laborers employed by the military here in Egypt and
Palestine. A sick Gippo is about the most dispirit-
ing panorama that one could behold. His com-
plexion is usually changed from a good., healthy
chocolate to a washed-out yellow. There is a look
in his eye like that of a lizard that has just lost
its tail. He is shrouded in a blanket, which is
wound closely round his head and allowed to hang-
down over his shoulders. His arms and legs are
bare, and he shivers though the day may be hot
enough to fry Sheol. The weight of his affliction
presses too closely on him for mere speech. He
moans a little, groans a little, presses his hand to
his stomach, and looks unutterable anguish. There
is always a big sick patade after a Taube has
dropped a few bombs on a Gippos’ camp. The crash
of the shell seems to remind them of the number of
complaints they are capable of suffering from. The
best remedy is the sight of one of our own machines
soaring heavenwards like a protecting angel.

“A.J.R.”: Of tlie many safeguards against
cbaffcutter accidents that have been devised all have
the defect that their operation depends on the man
in trouble. They mostly consist of placing the revers-
ing lever in as handy and prominent position as
possible; but generally a victim whose arm is being
ground through rollers and eut into lengths loses his
head. The proper time to prevent accident is before
it happens, and to this end the best idea I have seen
was on Harry Jamieson's machine in Berrigan
(N.S.M.). Tlie guard consisted of a piece of gal-
vanised-iron reaching back from the rollers about a
foot, thus compelling a margin of safety of at least
nine inches. Nine in ten chaffcutter accidents are
the result of working needlessly close to the feed-
rollers. Even in feeding thrashed straw I always
found it possible to maintain a six-inch clearance,
while with even tlie poorest hay there is no need to
approach tlie rollers closer than nine inches or a
foot. The introduction of the Jamieson idea would,
in my opinion, effectively safeguard all hands, includ-
ing the' inexperienced and the damfool.
“tickle’ - : Out here in western N.S.W.
we were all admiring the display of the Aurora
Australis on the night of the 9th. I pointed out a par-
ticularly bright beam of light shooting up far into
the sky to a bush chap standing near. “Cripes!’’ he
said. “Isn’t it a bonzer? Look, it goes right across
the sky 1” He was going into ecstasies over the
Milky May, which he had only just discovered, after
being about 50 years on earth !
"Lantana”: Dunno whether they grow
longer elsewhere, but this is the record death-adder
on the N. S. Males North Coast. It was massacred
at Eden Creek and taped 27 inches.

Eddyson : Coyle was beset with a pro-
blem—how might a man keep his barmaids? The
discovery of payable quartz at Tinkettle had brought
a number of eligible and affectionate young bachelors
into the district catered for by Coyle’s Teamsters’
Inn, and there was an emphatic demand for female
society in the bar. In the absence of a barmaid
Coyle’s trade slumped with a suddenness that left
no room for doubt as to its cause. Coyle brought a
barmaid from Melbourne. She served five weeks, and
then Craddock married her. Coyle wired an agent
for a second Hebe. The lady was forthcoming, and
she married Dunstan nine weeks later. Coyle pro-
duced a dark barmaid, finding blondes in too strong
demand. She was married to M’illiams within two
months in defiance of the covenant. Then Coyle
despatched a message for No. 1, and Ilortense arrived,
llortense was tall, dark, attractive and of a confiding
and kindly disposition. Jude and Harrington began
a hot competition for her exclusive regard, Coyle
watching progress with pathetic concern. Jude and
Harrington were large, fierce men. and served a use-
ful purpose in holding off all other suitors. It was as
much as an ordinary man’s life was worth to come
between them. “If you get her,” said Harrington to
Jude in friendly admonition, “I will cave your head
in with a sled. Jude was equally communicative.
“It she marries you,” he told Harrington pleasantly,
“she will be a widow within the week. The matter
will have my personal attention.” The situation re-
mained strained for close upon five months, and then
doubts and speculation were abolished by the simul-
taneous disappearance of Harrington and Hortensc.
Two days later there was reliable information to the
effect that the pair had been seen on the Adelaide
This isn't a guessing competition. To settle the bet before ft
there are 2226 pairs in this load of rabbits. The other week THE BULLETIN
introduced Australia's-perhaps the world's-first rabbit-trapping magor,
Thos. W. Draper, of Broidwoud (N.S.W.). For seven years he was manager
of the Braidwood Refrigerating Co. Then he reckoned that there was more
money in organising the business of trapping bunny for export; and this
is part of the supply which he handles daily at his Ballaba freezing
express, going west. Coyle was terribly distressed.
The defection of former maids had put him out;
this abandonment almost broke his heart. “But you
know what they are, man,” Clever said in his
capacity of counsel and comforter. “Have another
try. Fix the next one with a strong agreement.”
Coyle put up his hands in protest. “Strong agree-
ment!” he wailed. “Where's the agreement that
will hold them ? Why, dammit, man, 1 married
Hortense!”

May D.”: Doubters that padymelou
will live in captivity should see the one that a
friend of mine at Randwick, near Sydney, has had
in his back yard for the past nine years. Its diet
consists principally of lettuce leaves and bread and
milk. On the other hand, while Sydney Zoo has had
no difficulty in keeping kangaroos, wallabies and ’roo-
lats alive, padymelon, with similar food and con-
ditions, has invariably expired.
* * * *
‘ Leo Lear” : Any drover know why sheep
when drinking from troughs always make to the
tap end ? It isn’t for a cool swill, as they do it
irrespective of the water’s temperature. Even if
your trough is 50ft. long the jumbucks crowd and
push round the inflow.
Another little peculiarity
of sheep is their contempt
for camping cover. They’ll
leave good shelter on a
wild night and camp on
the most exposed hilltop
in the paddock. This may
be an inherited instinct to
see the approach of
enemies. In whatever low
class you place the woolly
animal’s brain otherwise,
I put him number one as
an engineer. He’ll set out
a grade on a hillside with
any 8.C.E., put in curves
switches and zig-zags, and
reach the top without
straining his boiler. I’ve
only once seen a mob of
sheep take a hill in the
face. It was after the
break of the 1902 drought.
We were mustering off the
ranges at the back of
Alexandra (Vic.), and the
orders were to truck the
weak ones and drove the
balance of the mob. There
were no yards, so all
hands and dogs got be-
hind the jumbucks, and
them hell-for-germany up a steep pinch. The
strong beasts were soon over the top. but the others
chucked it. I enter this as the quickest and most
effective draft in the Commonwealth.

... 7i rr „ ikin ” : What the sandflies of
nestralm? It is on record that two bushmen died
m the Onslow Hospital from the effects of bites. Of
course a lot depends on the state of the blood, and
the amount of alcohol in the system. I flatter mv-
self my blood is f.a.q.. and that I don’t stop more
than the next man, but in AVestralia, owing to
lumps, blisters and sores caused by sandfly bites, I
couldn't put a razor on my face for a month.
“Hay”: Here’s my selection of 32 best
songsters. First, the top-knot pigeon of Centralia,
also known as the Australian nightingale. Next, the
grey thrush, for liquid melody. Then, for his gay,
rollicking songs, the butcher-bird. After him that
happy-go-lucky vagabond the cuckoo. The mag-
pie for his carol next, followed by the merry, mock-
ing bush-lawyer (also called wattle-bird and
leatherhead), the bell-bird (whose chimes ring
through Gippsland fern gullies), willy-wagtail, the
wood-dove, the Australian goldfinch, the native
canary and the Australian skylark.

“Warrego”: Has the recent Abo. bar-
lackei for the hairlike substance of ripe maize as
a fine substitute for tobacco ever tried the dry tassel
of maize? I’ve known outbackers to smoke bushels
of this tack and prefer it to the real thing.
* * * *
Momokani” : Miss Grimshaw writes about
living on native produce in Papua. This is my view
of the facts. Agriculturists don’t exist in Papua,
no one having any time for anything except cocoa-
nuts and rubber, which are very poor fare. Only
one plantation in the whole of the country has its
own killing cattle. As for the wild pig, the flesh,
when obtainable, is about as tough, stringy and
nasty_ as any I know. Wallaby, so lamb-like, is
conspicuous by its absence where I am. Eatable
birds, when obtained by the £l-a-month shooting-
>oy, often require 24 hours* boiling and a sledge-
hammering to make them edible. Besides, how many
can afford to keep a boy for nothing else but shoot-
mg. . A prolonged diet of sweet potatoes, sweet
only in name, is liable to bring on all kinds of
ailments, and are as sloppy and tasteless as pith.
Maize I have seen in only one or two places myself—
just small patches grown to feed a horse ' Miss
Grimshaw refers to tropical fruit as if it was
ambrosia Can she honestly compare any tropical
trint with the fruit grown in sub-tropical climes?
1 ask Miss Grimshaw to name half a dozen places
in Papua where temperate vegetables are grown 1
know she grew none on her “Coral Island.” Fowls
may increase like flies in the bush, but their lives
must be as short, for no one can find them. Sago
may be all it is described, but when a man lives
on native food only he is generally considered to
have gone native.” Miss Grimshaw’s “Coral
sland may have rivers teeming with fish, but most
of the rivers in this part of the country are nothing
else but raging torrents. One of the 'largest rivers
in Papua is near here, and there is not a fish in it
Rice is grown at one place only, the Catholic
Mission on Tule Island, for local consumption. It
about time the real truth was written about the
country and its administration, or rather lack of it
tion o a i VeT S i° nd °i eXposin ” the maladministra-
tion of the Northern Territory, but leaves this nos
hT",htr„,. Th ' re «™>* iin.
~ u ”, cst Peaces as at Darwin, and, I think if
the veal truth was written, the local “cocked hat”
would vanish into the limbo of the past.

“V.” : The large livid-blue bottle-tick com-
mon in coastal scrubs sometimes obtains its blood
supply from unexpected sources. I have even found
one clinging to the forehead of a lyre-bird chick
(By the way, has any Abo. handled a well-grown
lyre-bird chick? From my experience, the operation
usually 1 esults in torn and bleeding hands for the
youngster never fails to use its powerful tow, armed
with sharp horny points. Meanwhile, the young
fowl emits a series of piercing calls, which can be
heard half a mile away, and which bring papa and
mamma lyre-bird to the rescue.) Young foals run-
ning in scrubby coastal country also furnish the
bottle-tick with nutriment. However, unlike his
lethal brother, the bush tick, he instils no poison
into his victims. The bush tick, apart from the
great mortality it causes among dogs, is often fatal
also to foxes and sometimes to cats. Strange that no
seropathist has yet taken to this insidious insect. A
cure for its wound or protective inoculation would be
worth thousands.
* » * ,
“5.0. H.”: Chicory and coffee as a substi-
tute for tobacco are not bad, but I know something
better. On the plains country, Westralia, grows a
small scrub bush commonly called sand-plains wattle.
It has a thick brown leaf, which, when cut and
dried for a few days, makes excellent pipe-fuel. By
inhaling the smoke of this plant I have known eases
of chronic asthma to be cured. I got the knowledge
from a Chow on Hampton Downs, who was at the
time using the weed as a substitute for the for-
bidden drug.

My form with here and there a pad

That all girls pad is widely known ”

My teeth are mostly not home-grown.”

He’s found I'm eking out with curls ' hair
And switch and front.” “Why should l’, e earev
That's done by all the city girls."

\ Real Triumph Beyond
Doubt for
Witt s phis
Kidney & Bladder
Where is there a woman who has not heard it said that if a
man had to endure half the ailments, pains and anxieties that a
woman suffers from time to time he would die under the strain.
i’his very probably is true. Women, owing to the delicacy of their
organic construction, do suffer acutely, in some cases at intervals,
in other cases for the greater part of their lives.
While a good deal of this suffering in women is difficult to
account for, still a very great deal of it is simply and solely due to
weakened kidneys and bladder, both of which organs are subject
to greater strain and more liable to derangement in a woman than
in a man. Women, further, suffer considerably because, from an
altogether disproportionate amount of modesty, they prefer to
suffer in silence rather than to make their condition known to
others, even of their own sex, on such matters.
Sometimes their suffering becomes so acute that they are
driven to search diligently for something which will bring even
temporary relief. Fortunately many women use sound common-
sense and their own judgment in regard to the treatment of their
peculiar ailmehts.
“I suffered terribly with kidney and bladder weakness and
rheumatic pains for over ten years. The sufferings I went through
were a trial. I became very depressed and absolutely unfit for
anything, even my household duties. Knifelike pains seemed to
catch me in the back; at times they would come on so severe as to
make me quite helpless. I was really in a very bad state. I tried
anything and everything I heard of, different remedies and treat-
ments of all kinds, but nothing seemed to do me any good.
“I had heard a good deal about De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder
Pills and what a wonderful remedy they are for suffering woman-
kind, so I decided to get a box and give them a trial. The results
were most astonishing, gravel and sediment were brought away
after the first two doses, and I got wonderful relief. I persevered
with the pills and I gradually got brighter and better day by day.
They completely cleared away the bladder pain and kidney, trouble,
and the rheumatism that I had completely left me. This is now a
year and a half ago and I have not had the slightest twinge of
pain since. De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills, in my case, have
proved a veritable godsend, for they have absolutely cured me.
Knowing how much good they have done me I have been able to
speak most highly of them, and am very pleased to be able to
recommend them to other women who suffer as I did.”
Mrs. Holt, the lady in question, who lives at 20 Windsor
Street, Nuneaton, was cured about Christmas, 1912, now over
three years ago. On April 18, 1916, she said, “I can gladly
confirm all that 1 have said previously about the wonderful
efficacy of De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills. Not only did I
derive such prompt relief from them, but through letting other
like sufferers know, I have been the means of bringing relief to a
great many women who suffered as I did. They worked a splendid
cure in my case and I shall always gladly recommend them
wherever I go. My health continues to be very good.”
Another lady living quite near to Mrs. Holt was similarly
afflicted. At No, 23, Victoria Street, Nuneaton, lives Mrs. Wykes,
who was suffering in a similar way to Mrs. Holt. “For over
eleven years,” says Mrs. Wykes, “I suffered with terrible kidney
trouble and most acute backache pains. At various times through

Business, Robbery, etc
“M.P.A.” : A word about Prime Minister
Hughes’s order that Australian flour for
Java must be consigned to the British Con-
sul. If the little gentleman with the hot-air
balloon had troubled to ask the advice of a
few of our commercial travellers who have
struck Batavia and tried to get a word with
the said Consul, he would have appointed a
home-made agent for the Commonwealth. It
is easier for an Australian to get an inter-
view with Rockefeller on a mail day than
with the British Consul-General at Batavia.
The market for Australian flour, ham, butter,
bacon, fruit, etc., is much greater than is
supposed, and if we had sufficient business
capacity we should show some signs of it
before the Yanks and the Japs collar the lot.
* k * *
“Kopai”: Returning home from a late
assignment the other night I met my friend
the senior-constable at the gate of the public
gardens, and we strolled on together. We
were passing through an old pine avenue
when an appalling stench struck our nos-
trils. “Wind must be blowing from South
Kensington,’’ I said. “Don’t look up!” he
answered irrelevantly but with intense feel-
ing. “Don’t look up!’’ “What’s on?” I
asked, mystified. “Don’t look up!” he
answered again, still peering low. “Eyes
down” we made our way towards tlffi other
entrance. At the young Constable
Green was awaiting his chief. “Noticed a
bit of a smell in there, Green,” said the
senior, casually. “Just have a look round,
will you?” “Look here, senior,” I said, as
Green disappeared into the gloom. “Let me
into the know !” “The last time I smelt that
smell, my boy,” the sub-officer retorted, “I
had the job o’ cutting down a man who’d
hanged himself in those pines.” He patted
the silver chevron on his coat-sleeve. “You
get that for experience, my boy!” he added.
“No. 87”: Well, he deserves success for
being ready to seize his opportunities. To
overcome the difficulty arising from the stop-
page of the supply of German dyes a project
was set on foot in England to start dye fac-
tories on a large scale. Nippon at once
skipped across to England to watch the ex-
periments, and is now out in the world’s
markets with the goods, while in England
the industry merely potters along for want
of labor.
“Duct”: Re the modern miracle of heal-
ing with glands (B. 5/7/T7), Redbank (Q.)
works last year saved the thyroid glands and
exported them. The A.M.E. works, which
is looked on as a branch of Armour’s, also
tried saving these organs; but labor costs
were too high. By the way, the Chinese
make a medicine out of the suprarenal
glands. Anyone know anything about it?
* * * *
“Tunic”: I disagree with “Enzedder” (B.
16/8/T7) as to the clothing of Billjim. I
have seen the uniforms of the soldiers of all
the nations fighting at the present time, and
declare that the Australian is the
best-clothed of the lot. The Maorilander’s
tunic is well worthy of mention; but the
tunic is not the outfit. As to underclothing,
I have chatted with Billjim—hundreds of
him —at Salisbury during the coldest winter
England has had for 60 years, and he made
special mention of his warm clothing. Apart
altogether from the splendid slouch hats, the
Tommies envy the Kangaroos their West
End quality materials and finish. I never
saw Tommy walking round one of “Enzed-
der’s” Maorilanders.

“F.N.T.”: Victoria is making a move to
grow its own paper. Since so far the pulp-
ing of local timbers has not led to satis-
factory business, 3000 acres on the Dargo
high plains are to be planted with Norwegian
spruce, and if the trees promise well the
spade will get busy.

NUPTIAL KINEMA.
To Engaged Persons.
Do you wish to preserve a vivid, living recollec-
tion of the happieit dav of your life? Have a film
taken of the ceremony of your wedding, and in after
years you will be able to see yourself on the screen,
young, loving, full of hope in the future. Prospectus
on demand.
Walter Bayles, brother of Norman,
M.L.A., is doing his bit for the returned sol-
dier bv supplying stock from his poultry-ranch
at Hexham (Vie.). With a skilled manager
Bayles has been doing the thing properly
for years, harboring only the best birds,
guaranteed to lay eggs in the depth of
winter if properly housed and groomed and
fed ; and if the Coomete gifts go to men who
only need them as the finishing touch to an
80-acre farm, all should be well.
“Petifi”: The shipping shortage threatens
to hit the Australian wine industry close to
the neck. No juice has been shipped for
many months, and vignerons—especially the
small ones —are wondering what is going to
happen. The custom has been for the little
growers to sell to the big firms, which had
facilities for storing, etc. But there is a
limit to the present holding space, and unless
some of the stocks are shifted soon, a few
of the big buyers won’t have room for any
small producer’s output. Which means
that the s.p. will either have to haul
in liis bush until the weather fines or sell
to any speculator who cuts into the game.
There is money in that, probably. Most
things don’t improve with age; but wine
does, so the capital put into juice to wait
until the war is over wouldn’t be capital
lying idle by any means.

“X.Y.”: “M.G.’i (B. 26/7/Y7) wants to
know “Why not making cooking utensils
out of concrete?” Because, apart from the
difficulty and expense of constructing con-
crete pots, they would be very wasteful of
fuel, as the ingredients are wretched con-
ductors of heat.
Still another vote of thanks to the other
William. For 40 years the fine slate quarries
at Bangor, on the Tamar (Tas.), have been
idle, people finding it less bother to pav for
imported galvanised-iron with in lOU.' But
now that the submarine has got basy iron
is off, so a Sydney syndicate is resurrecting
the quarries, and in a few months should
be shipping house-tiles to the mainland.
* * * #
“Te Whare”: Ginseng, John Chinaman's
boss medicine-plant, if it succeeds in Aus-
tralia, should turn in fair dividends. Ameri-
can ginseng (Panax quinquefolium), in the
shape of dry root, is worth, at latest quota-
tion available, from 10s. to 20s. per lb., in
bulk, according to quality, which varies a
good deal. But the ginseng grown in Korea
(Panax schinseng ) is said to fetch its weight
in gold at times, and at present is worth
about £2O per catty of one and a quarter lb„
or about 20s. per oz.—in bulk, not by retail
The best American root brings only about
£3 per lb. retail, less than one-fifth of the
bulk value of the imperial ginseng of Korea.
The American species was raised in Adelaide
Botanic Gardens over 30 years ago, but the
plant was allowed to die out, and difficulties
have since arisen in regard to the germina-
tion of seed. Nevertheless, as the cost would
amount to only a few shillings, an experi-
mental ginseng plot might be wrnrth while.

“0.5. H.”: Pearl buttons (B. 9/8/17)?
Well, last month 11 Jap schooners dropped
dowm to the islands off the Barrier and
secured from four to 10 tons each of pearl-
shell, which in Nippon is worth about
a ton to button-makers. So, you see. Aus-
tralia will not be left stranded for pyjama
fasteners, thank God !

“Pick”: Following the example set by
their neighbors the N.S.W. South Coast
dairymen, some of the Southern Monaro
sheepmen have lately commenced experiment-
ing w’ith drought-resisting grasses. At
Merambego, near the Vic. border, a paddock
40 yards by 20 yards, sown v T ith blue-grass,
fed *4O hoggets and three cows throughout
last w’inter, when, owing to dry conditions,
flocks on their customary areas (sheep and
a half to the acre) were dying of starvation.
What herds could be raised in Australia
were the chapce-the-luck systems of dealing
with the soil set aside for up-to-date
methods!
THE ETERNAL CHANTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, a fine old ship was my last packet,
She could run before the wind or tack it
An’ sail through hell an’ stand the racket;
Blow, boys, blow,
An' raise it from below !
She was handsome rigged from truck to keel,
With a jaunty poise an’ a graceful heel,
An’ a wheel-house over her steerin’ wheel.
Blow, boys, bully boys, blow!
From London Docks to the River Plate,
From cold Cape Horn to the Golden Gate,
With a kind Old Man an ’a soft-voiced mate;
Blow, boys, blow,
An’ let her flimsies show.
From Puget Sound to the Lonsdale Rip,
From Akyab to the Mississip,
There was never a ship could lick that ship—
Blow, boys, bully boys, blow!
Her cook he came from the P. and 0.
As black as a coon in a minstrel show,
An’ he baked the duff an’ the cakes just so:
Blow, boys, blow,
For a cook from the P. and, 0.
There was ham an’ eggs for the loomin' meal,
An’ bread as wasn’t stone nor steel,
An’ a tot o’ grog as you left the wheel.
Blow, boys, bully boys, blow!
No soogy-mooge when the days were chilly,
Nor kicks nor cuffs to drive you dilly,
Nor sweatin’ at night on the handy-billy—
Blow, boys, blow,
Aloft the yard must go!
It was rum, hot rum, red, rare and old,
As a batterin’ ram agen’ the cold,
An’ a nice soft job in the after-hold—
Blow, boys, bully boys, blow!
In the swelt’rin’ heat o’ the Tropic days
It was knock off work an’ smoke an’ laze,
An’ yarn o’ the good old hooker’s ways.
Blow, boys, bloic,
An’ let the hooker go!
Lollin’ in the cool o’ the croonin’ Trade
As the porpoises leaped an’ the fishes played,
Dreamin’ o’ the love o’ your latest maid —
Blow, boys, bully boys, blow!
There was real gold quids on reachin’ port,
An’ liberty days an’ lots o’ sport
Alovin’ o’ the wives we didn’t ought;
Blow, boys, blow,
For Sailor Town, yah ho !
Oh, the nights o’ noise an’ song an’ cheer,
An’ old salt’s tales right good to hear
(Old Sailor Town is seldom drear!) —
Blow, boys, bully boys, blow!
But there’s none o’ that in this yer clipper
With her Cain-brand mate an’ flint-heart skipper,
An’ the first port touched here’s one who’ll slip ’er—
Blow, boys, away,
For the shores of Sydney Bay!
For the darn dead-horse neighs overhead,
An’ it’s tough salt-junk an’ granite bread,
An’ work as’d drive a sailor dead.
Blow, boys, bully boys... .B’lay !
AT SEA.
JACK HAMILTON
THE LAUNDRY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The laundry is a clumsy knave —
It wears my linen out, not I.
God, who hates waste, will never save
The laundress when she comes to die;
He will not save from hell or hurt
The woman who destroys my shirt.
•
For if I buy of collars six,
The laundress takes her will of three r
They that are left me by her tricks
Are frayed and ragged shockingly;
This miscreant who wrecks my duds
I’d like to drown in her own suds.
The tailors’ and the mercers’ friend,
The jackal of the drapers she,
Who, ere their time, does make an end
Of singlets and of hosiery.
And so I curse that private she,
My new pyjamas’ enemy.
QUEENSLAND.
MARTIN LUTHER.

POLITICAL
POINTS
During the talk on the new Loan Bill in
the Reps, the other night the Phewsion was
found guilty of dharging the cost of a pre-
sentation gold watch to Cohen. Also the
wonderful Win-the-War Government ad-
mitted borrowing £2OO or £3OO as a first in-
stalment towards a polite and fitting history
of Australia’s part in the scrap. But the
Phewsion is so besotted with the lOU sys-
tem of finance that it sees nothing irregular
in those proceedings, and regards continuous
cadging as quite as natural as breath-
ing and as inevitable as the seasons.
It followed, therefore, that when John
Forrest asked for leave to borrow
£80,000,000, his supporters’ ennui was
monumental. All that evening the bells
tinkled for a quorum without any sign of
awakening life on the part of the National-
ists. In fact, Forrest might have been ask-
ing for tuppence to buy the cough-drops he
so badly needed for all the interest the
Fusees manifested. It shows the degenera-
tion of the Win-the-War crowd when it
couldn’t maintain a house to see the largest
Loan Bill in our history through the first
stages of the second reading.
MA —WHO MEANDERED.
RAY: “Your mother’s very strict with you,
isn’t she?”
MAY: “Yes, ma’s trying to bring me up the
way she should have gone.”
The Federal Parliament, against the
advice of Bigjohn’s two predecessors, has
decided, to exempt the next war loan, at
any rafe, from income tax. It will be
doing ap ill service to Australia if it exempts
any more. The debt is growing so large
and the annual interest charge is so great
that the withdrawal of this huge capital
sum from the taxable area will throw a
frightfully increased burden upon the ordi-
nary taxpayer. There never should have
been any exemption at all. The country
should have known precisely what it was
paying. With exemptions it doesn’t know,
because the longer the war lasts and the
heavier the debt and the interest bill, the
more is the exemption worth, because the
less all other wealth will produce net. And
f
it is unfair in another way: it is worth far
more to the big man than to the little one.
To the latter it may be worth only Is. in the
£; to the former anything up to 55., or
whatever the maximum rate of income tax
on investments may be.
“Shall Starvation Win?” asked the melo-
dramatic 11. E. Boote, in Sydney Worker
last week. Well, it might have won if the
sensible people of N. S. Wales hadn’t decided
to set to and feed themselves and Boote
and Boote’s deluded readers with “black”
food. And Labor allowed itself to be led co
the “black” dinner-tabte like a lamb. The
fact that Boote is still alive and writing
instead of lying an em&ciated wreck in his
own wastepaper-basket shows that starva-
tion need never be feared while there is a
crust of “black” bread in the State.
The value of a catch-cry, even for so small
a thing as a municipal election, was illus-
trated at Footscray on
August 23. The antag-
onists were a fellmonger,
advocating the establish-
ment of new industries,
and a retiring councillor
conspicuous for his oppo-
sition to offensive trades.
The situation was com-
plicated by the fact that
the fellmonger was the
selected candidate of the
local Labor body, and
the other a ’vert running
as an Independent. Then
some student of “Nation-
alist” methods evolved
the catch-phrase: “Vote
for and no smells!”
Of course, the fellmonger
was no more an advo-
cate of smells than the
other fellow, but the
phrase caught on and
spread like a pantomime
gag, with the unfortunate
worker in skins panting
behind and trying to ex-
plain away the damag-
ing implication that he
did want smells! Poli-
tical Labor brought up
its great Parliamentary
guns and talked “solid-
arity” in stentorian
tones from countless
street cofners, but with-
out avail. The League’s anointed went down
and out by a 2 to 1 majority. Rule 1 of
politics as of advertising: First catch your
catch-phrase.
Britain has its hands so full of winning
the war that it hasn’t done much towards
winning trade after the w r ar. A few projects
are, nevertheless, in hand, mostly based on
German pre-war models. The most ambi-
tious is the British Trade Corporation,
w T hich has the blessing of the Treasury and
the Board of Trade. Authorised capital is
£10,000,000; a million has already been put
up and another million and a half was being
asked for the other day. This is the idea: —
There exists to-day no large British financial in-
stitution possessing an industrial department or an
organisation for study and research into new ideas
or inventions, which is specially equipped to examine
and nurse new schemes or developments until suffi-
ciently proved and ripe for public investment. The
Corporation will aim at becoming a link between
British industry and the British investor. It will
take a lead in the formation of syndicates to deal
with business of promise and importance, and it will
associate with itself other banking and financial in-
stitutions which care to participate in its operations.
An industrial bank of this character is
badly wanted in Australia. It ought to be
the institution to which the man with com-
mand of raw material (including ideas)
would naturally turn for advice and assist-
ance ; and it ought to be of such a character
as to be able to practically command what-
ever tariff protection it might deem necessary.
The nearest approach this part of the world
has to anything of the kind is in the Bank
of New T Zealand. When that concern w T as
tottering 20-odd years ago the State went
to its rescue and became a large shareholder,
and, though it didn’t have “an organisation
for study and research into new ideas or
inventions,” what it has been able to do for
Maoriland is of incalculable value.
A note from the Front: —
I wish the Australian men who still sit tight at
home could watch the doctors sending back men,
wounded two and three times many of them, before
they are properly fit. I wish they could hear the
doctors telling these men, when they complain, that
they have no option, their instructions being “so
many per day.” I have seen men with one leg
shorter than the other, after fracture by machine-
gun bullets, being sent back for another go, wearing
special boots.
Meantime at home, in order to save bis own
skin, Hughes promised not to introduce con-
scription during the life of the Parliament
then being elected. And in the camps there
still are men who have never been at the
Front, though they have been drawing the
country’s pay for anything from a year up-
wards. For the most part these men are
professing to train recruits; and they are
not one-tenth part as qualified for the job
as some of these wounded men who have to
hobble back into the trenches on specially-
built boots. And that’s how we Win the
War.
Cabled that J. D. Connolly, the new
Agent-General for Westralia, while en route
for London spoke a little piece to the
Canadian Club at Vancouver: —
The Referendum was not the best way to decide
any bitterly contested issue, such as conscription, in
consequence of the liability to misrepresentation.
The Government had better cable Connolly
telling him to either hold his tongue or come
home. If a referendum is not the best way
to decide any bitterly-contested issue, then
we are all wrong in protesting that there
should be no strike until the Labor union
affected has taken a secret ballot of its
members.
ANOTHER HIT.
“Young man, what are you doing for your
country?”
“ Oh , I stay home and mind the kids while
the missus sells buttons.”
One of the silliest things Australia has
ever done with public money was to devote
some of it to the repatriation of certain
“Russian refugees,” who, as soon as the Czar
disappeared, were assisted back to their own
country. A suspicion, if not an absolute
certainty, of German intrigue attaches to
this demand for repatriation. The one un-
doubted fact concerning some of the heftiest
of the “poor refugees” was their bitter
hatred for Britain. The largest maggot in
their Muscovite brain was the “secret
treaty” maggot. They were bent on burst-
ing up England’s “secret treaty” with the
Czar’s Government. They regarded their
alleged country’s connection with England
as an unholy alliance of aristocrats, their
feelings towards aristocrats being unfit for
publication. And these smouldering fire-
brands were packed back to Russia at the
expense of the Government! Our Exchequer
provided them with the means to go and do
their best to lose the war for our side!
Overworked Premier Peacock proposes—
when he has time and other promised
measures are passed and the war is over and
the century is old —to bring in a bill to
allow of wife-deserters and such like expen-
sive State guests being employed on the
roads, and so made to earn their keep as
well as provide something for the missus
and kids. The idea has merit, but where is
the need for a william? All that is called
for is such commonsense administration
as will compel all able-bodied prisoners to
earn their own tucker and a good bit over.
Labor unions have lately decided that the
time has come to cut out parasites. As
they are quite right in that, they presum-
cibly won t offer any more silly objections
to keeping the prisoners in gaols hard at
productive work.
i P rac tice of declaring various things
black because they were produced or
handled by non-unionists comes rather late
in the day. More than half of all the goods
that have come to Australia and have done
Australian union workmen out of a job have
been produced by non-unionists. Every dav
of their lives the wharfies handle “black”
goods—goods produced not only by non
unionists, but by non-unionists employed on
sweating terms. But Labor never turns n
hair over those goods and doesn’t, as a rule
care two straws about Protection. There is
nothing more illogical in all the world. S
Willie Kelly is annoying the Phewsion
with a charge of being a dull ass. A N s
Wales syndicate is alleged to have discovered
a substitute for petrol and applied for •.
patent, which was refused on the ground
apparently, that the disclosure might help
to Lose the War. Baulked in that direction
the interested people then tried to reach I
Bull’s ear through the Defence Department-
but that bright institution declined to disturb
the busy Titan with an invention that was
“worthless.” This decision was followed
by the disappearance of the previous objec-
tions against granting a patent. Thereupon
the inventors secured the needed protection
and asked the Commonwealth Treasury for
permission to float a small co. A heavv
negative blew them out of the door. They
are still standing on the mat, and it is
evidently the intention of the Government
to keep them there. It is said that the
strongest opposition comes from the Defence
Department, which only increases this
paper’s suspicions that the Department is
a graveyard for ideas. There is even con-
siderable evidence that George Foster Pearce
is the Chief Gravedigger. Never having had
an idea in his life, how can that poor man
be expected to see the need for any in others?
“0.K.”: In the revelation of the real
Russia we can see the utter foolishness of
Britain’s foreign policy in the past. Bea-
consfield, Salisbury. Derby and their old
Tory colleagues—all lived on the tradition
of a “Russian menace” and Muscovite “de-
signs on India.” Now we know that huge,
sprawling, dreamy Russia hadn’t a menace
in her constitution, and that the depth of
her designs was a fiction made in Germany
by Bismarck, who had a true measure of the
ignorance of British diplomacy. Germany,
living next door to Russia, has always
known its neighbor’s weakness. At the out-
break of the present war, when Australia
lived in a fool’s paradise of expectation
from the results of “the Russian steam
roller” the local Hun didn’t attempt to con-
ceal his opinion of what the roller was
worth. He knew, but we didn’t. The by-
gone statesmen of Britain had fooled us
with their pretentious ignorance. The old
belief in Ivan’s militarism had been merely
shaken, not upset, by the Japanese war,
and we were persuaded that Russia had
profited by experience to reorganise her
forces and remedy her mistakes. Our mis-
information was mostly made in Germany,
of course. The Asquith Government knew
a good deal of the truth and was only nega-
tively accessory to the public’s delusion.
But what shall be said of Governments that
kept the theory of a Russian menace in the
forefront of their policy and knew abso-
lutely nothing of a German menace which
was being organised under cover of it? Our
ignorance was grounded on the crass stu-
pidity of an aristocratic British Foreign
Office, always selecting the personnel of its
embassies from the easy-going, gullible class
that gathered its notions from Court circles
and put no natural astuteness into the job.
THE RIGHT TIME TO COME HOME.
“ There's father. He always comes home
after ten.”
"So he ought to. Ten drinks are quite
cnough for any man.”
OUR COUNTRY!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An appeal for recruits at Tabulam (N.S.W.) races
resulted in the enlistment of two aborigines and an
Indian.
They sent the message far and wide, the call to arms
shrilled high.
The strikers, idle rank on rank, meandered slowly by.
And Spring had spread her dimpled clouds with
laughter through the sky.
The death was on the stricken field, the strike was
on the town;
But men were wanted at the Front to pull the
German down—
Strong men and true to toil and smite for manhood’s
high renown.
The white man's land was fair to see by plain and
rolling down.
The white man’s war had raised its voice above the
strike in town.
Three stalwarts heard —and two were black and one
was Asian brown.
x.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

WORMS—THE SYMPTOMS.
The symptoms of worms are manifold, embracing
not only facial indications and depression of the whole
system, but serious irritation and much distress.
IN VERY YOUNG CHILDREN much of the irri-
tability and fretfulness is often due to the presence
of worms. Restlessness, fitfulness, sudden starts, hard-
ness and fulness of the belly, irregular movement of
the bowels, and excessive secretion of saliva from the
mouth are invariably indications of the irritation
caused by worms.
IN OLDER CHILDREN the 6ame symptoms are
present, but more distinctly defined. Grinding of the
teeth, violent movements of the legs and arms, and
an undue flow of saliva, which causes frequent efforts
to swallow. When awake there is a constant desire
to pick the nose and scratch the irritated parts.
The bowels are irregular, and frequently the move-
ment of the worms causes diarrhoea, alternating with
costiveness. The breath is usually foul, and the
child is languid and peevish. The tongue preter-
naturally red, or alternately clean and covered with
a white, slimy mucus. A short dry cough, frequently
slimy stools (excretion from the bowels) ; emaciation ;
slow fever, irregular pulse; and sometimes convul-
sions. Loss of appetite frequently alternates with a
craving for food. Indeed, the latter symptom is most
pronounced in many cases, and many mothers have
experienced the insatiable appetites of their children
without realising the cause.

appetite became precarious—sometimes he could not
obtain sufficient, and at others nothing seemed appe-
tising. I saw Comstock’s ‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets
advertised, and decided upon giving them a trial
and following the directions given with the medi-
cine. Cedric soon passed the whole of these thread
worms and became thoroughly restored to health,
his sleep being peaceful and appetite normal. You
may use this testimony if desired. Cedric is seven

are a purely vegetable medicinal preparation, com-
