SPORTING
NOTIONS
The strike having held up racing in Syd-
ney for the best part of a month, the Spiing
meeting of Tattersall’s Club at Rapdwiek on Satur-
day was as grateful as a shower in days of drought.
No trams were running to the ground, but the
public got there all the same —by boot and motor
and cab and cart. And when at the finish it rushed
out to find its way home and leaped eu masse into
everything with a wheel on it, the scene along the
Randwiek-road was like the final phase of the sports
at Hambone Flat multiplied by 56,924. MacShori-
odds walked back on air. There had been seven
races, and in .five the welcome outsider arrived with-
out the slightest apology.
When the Chelmsford Stakes were mentioned the
public unshipped its wad and rushed the Maori-
lander Biplane like suburban citizens catching the
last train home in strike time. There were 18
starters, half of them being nags of high quality.
But that didn’t matter. Two to 1 was the best on
offer against the stranger. That didn’t matter either,
except to anyone who was looking for something
else. And the end of it all was that the good thing
went down with the old familiar thud and the crowd
rent the atmosphere with wails of anguish. Biplane
ran as rotten a race as this page had noticed for
many days. He certainly has a lot of pace, but
either he is an animal of sour temper or he. requires
riding in a fashion very different, from that Hollowed
on Saturday. He jumped quickly off and in a few
strides was in front, fighting with Deeley for his
head, and he continued doing the same thing for
nearly half a mile. At this stage one of the rough
’uns caught him, and though the pair were clear of
the rest the race was over for Biplane. Coming into
the straight half the field swept past while he seemed
undecided whether 'to come on or get off the course
altogether. He was kept going, galloped like a
mule and finished a long way back in the ruck. A
deplorable exhibition that once more gives point
to the warning against paying too much attention to
sensational track work. Biplane had been galloping
over seven and eight furlongs at Randwick in great
style—with feather weights. He may be a fine
sprinter, but before reckoning him a Derby colt
most people will want to see him tried again with
different riding.
Prince Viridis won the Chelmsford after a hard
struggle with Lingle from the distance. Opposite the
stand it looked as if the older horse would get in
front, but the little fellow responded to Bracken's
persuasion with great gameness and reached the'post
with his head just clear. It was a very fine per-
formance, for the colt was not quite at his best, and
makes it as certain as anything connected with horse
can be that the winner will have something to say
in the Derby. The time was 2sec. worse than Sasan-
off registered last year, but Prince Viridis carried
101 b. more weight. Lingle and Cetigne both did
well and should be heard of again a little later on.
The Toff, another Maorilander, was at no stage in
the argument. The imported Theovil, who came into
the Epsom betting a month or two ago, began last
and finishetj at the same place. A slow commence-
ment is no good in an Epsom. But the writer
expects to see this handsome nag one of these days
finishing faster than he did after being galloped on
on Saturday.
Prince Viridis was bred by his owner, “Baron”
Brown, who had a second winner on Saturday, when
the generally-calamitous Wallace Isinglass appro-
priated the Spring Handicap, in which the running
of First Lesson and Bursar was noticeable. Wallace
had been doing well with Prince Viridis on the
track lately. Wherefore he started a hot favorite.
As usual he didn’t seem to care for the business, and
if the agitation of his tail meant anything it was
bad luck for Israel the post happened to be so close.
Bursar started at a long price. He is apparently
getting back to the form of last year.
Of the Epsom horses seen at Tattersall's
meeting on Saturday there were some that behaved
very unobtrusively. There were others that did the
other thing. Of these latter the most emphatic
were Ardrossan, Auberic and Mehi King.. The first
of these, a Featherstich gelding, five years old,
trained bv Mark Thompson, ran clean over every-
thing in the Tramway Handicap, seven furlongs.
This horse won the High Weight Handicap, six fur-
longs, at the A.J.C. Autumn meeting, and in .Tune
ran Clydeside to a head over 1} mile on the same
course. He carried 7st. 21b. on Saturday. His
Epsom burden is 7st. 41b. He will see a mile out
all right. Auberic (Bst. 101 b.). second on Saturday
to Ardrossan, did nearly as well, and by the end of
the month should be a considerably better horse.
When at his best he is mighty hard to beat. Epsom
weight—Bst. 61b. Mehi King, Bst. 21b., was solidly
backed for the Tramway, but, though well placed,
at the top of the straight faded out of the argument.
He has been supported with the right material for
the Epsom, but will have to do a long way better
to land the bacon. Still, the same can be said of a
dozen others, and sudden improvement is not an
uncommon complaint at this time of year.
There were 44 horses ready for the Novice
Handicap at Randwick on Saturday, and for the
first time a race on that course was run in divisions.
Of the six place-getters one was imported and the
others were by imported sires. Incidentally they
included L. K. S. Mackinnon’s Salvini, which did
something for the first time since bringing 1900
guineas as a yearling. He ran third in the first
division. In the other races, including a steeple-
chase, the 15 place-getters were with three excep-
tions either imp. or had imp. on one or both sides
of the pedigree. Imported horses were third in
four events.
Probably Bursar, thoroughly wound up,
would be about the very best horse in the country
tor a two-mile race. He looks great for stamina
and strength. But last year he went amiss in the
winding-up process, and the bitten punters are shy.
By his victory in the Chelmsford Stakes
young Prince Viridis goes up 71b. in the Melbourne
Cup weights, and a Derby win will add another 31b.
This comes very rough on Prince V. He was handi-
capped up to Derby-winning form in the first place,
and every pound over 7st. 61b. will be more than
his dad, Prince Foote, had to carry when he won the
Melbourne Cup.
« * * *
Young Charlie Barden, who got smashed
up in a tinpot race at Canterbury Park (Sydney)
a few weeks ago, was a loser in more ways than
one. Until he pulls round again Bracken will be
aboard the “Baron’s” horses, and they started
off with a couple of wins at Randwick on Saturday.
* * *
“H.0’8.”: Tom Donoghue, of Mudgee
(NSW.), reputed the oldest riding jockey in Aus-
tralia, will have to hand the title on to C. F.
Clifford At Broken Hill last week Clifford booted
his own mare Gratiosa to victory in the handicap
Trial Stakes. Clifford is 65 year's of age. Donoghue
is a mere kid of 63.
Backers of Glad Eye for the two Cups
will be disturbed to find him an acceptor for the
Metropolitan and an entrant for various other events
at the A.J.C. meeting. As regards the Caulfield Cup
the alleged hope of Scobie’s stable could, perhaps,
afford a penalty, for he is let in at 7st. 41b., or 51b.
less than his handicap for the Met.
* * * *
With the petering out of the strike some
of the Victorian horses commenced to reach Randwick
last week, and as the favorites for the Epsom and
Metropolitan (Wedge and Court Jester) come from
over the border, and Thrice is stated to be a genuine
Derby animal, the appearance of this trio has been
received with loud applause. Up to the other day it
was a little doubtful whether Lord Windemere or
Prince Viridis would be the chosen of Barden’s stable
for the Derby, but Saturday’s running settles
the matter. The problem of the Derby with many
people is whether Thrice can stay the journey and
if so whether he can beat the Prince. If he has
developed according to promise he may do both, but
he will have to be as exceptionally good at three
years as he was at two to do it. Meanwhile there
are others in the Derby who have yet to be heard
from on the subject—Bronzetti, Pah King and
Broken Glass for instance.
During the last half-dozen years or so
any three-year-old to run well up in the Chelmsford
Stakes did as well or better in the A.J.C. Derby.
Prince Foote won both events. Beragoon and
Mountain Knight were both second in the Stakes
and each won the Derby of his year. A.nd Wallace
Isinglass, after being second to Carlin in the
Stakes, arrived behind Cetigne in the Derby. But
there was an exception to the general rule last
year. Kilboy ran out of place in the first event
but got away with the Derby afterwards.
Last year’s sensational nag Quinologist
had been consuming oats under the eye of the Law
in Sydney for a long while up till last week, when,
after listening to evidence of various kinds and pro-
longed argument, Justice Duffy made up his mind
and gave judgment. The case turned upon a claim
brought by Albert Cochram (W.A.) against Theodore
Trautwein and David Price (Sydney), alleging fraud
on the part of the defendants. Cochram had been
part owner with Trautwein of the moke, and what
he wanted was cither a declaration that lie was
still a partner or £5OOO damages. The claim was
based upon allegations against Trautwein of having
defrauded Cochram by making a bogus sale to Price
and afterwards buying the horse back as part of the
dummy transaction. The Court gave judgment against
Cochram, and Trautwein emerged a winner. But the
mind of the Court was filled with dark suspicion and
no costs were allowed. There was some fairly hard
lying over some phases of the business, and h’sonor,
after refusing a stay of proceedings, intimated that
if anvone wanted permission to prosecute someone
for perjury he was ready to give a hand. The
Court appeared to look with a cold eye on everybody
except the horse.

“0.K.”: The “Plunger Thompson” re-
ferred to by Billy Emerson in the song (“Could I
Onlv Back the Winner”) that “B.B.” heard him sing
in Maoriland Was probably Joe Thompson, the big-
gest bookmaker of the period—3o years ago. Joe
also owned nags occasionally, and when he backed
’em he had a plunge. He won a fortune when his
Don Juan took the Melbourne Cup. Lance Lenton,
who wrote the ditty, lived in Melbourne and knew
.Toe Thompson by repute, but didn’t know the differ-
ence between a “leviathan” booky and the other
sort of gambler that enjoys the fleeting notoriety
of a “plunger.”
Adelaide’s new P.M., S. .T. Mitchell, is
making a small sensation by a genuine effort to put
down bookmaking, which is illegal in S.A., by
smashing fines, instead of tickling the offender with
a sort of license-fee. “Betting is a business, and I
have to put it down,” he said last week, and in-
creased his previous record of a £3O fine to £4O,
with a hint that he is prepared to go after the
Maoriland record of £l5O.
* * * •
Of course it is poor sport, but pony-
racing leads to some exciting struggles for the cash.
There was one the other day at Victoria Park (Svd-
ney). At the distance in the Park Handicap Bal-
lanto appeared to have the race w T on ; but in a stride
or two the Trickster ran up into the argument, fol-
lowed by Rouvlet, and the three fought out a gruel-
ling finish. The Trickster was the middle of the
sandwich, and got squeezed out by a whisker: the
other two reached the post together. In the run-off
Rouvlet, the outsider, won easily, and backers of
Ballanto howled like dingoes.
The English thoroughbred Borrow’ (Ham-
burg—-Forget) is reckoned the fastest animal now-
racing in the United States. Before leaving Eng-
land Borrow was raced at two-, three- and four-
years, and recognised as a prad of exceptional endur-
ance. He is now nine years old, and in winning
the Brooklyn Handicap (July) ran the mile and a
quarter in lmin. 49 2-ssec —a world’s record. The
best time for this distance registered
is “521” by Woorak, and in England o 2 4-5 by
Polymelus and Hackler’s Pride. #

A couple of fly-weights collided at Sydney
Stadium on Saturday for the State champions p
George Mendies (Vst. 41b.) and Jack Creen ( .
121 b.). Jack is a sturdy little boxer with phmt)
of sand in him, but George smote him oftener than
he could retaliate and that settled the matter. In
contemplating these midgets hurling k.o s about
there is always a feeling that the business might
be more effectively managed by bringing larger
forces into the field. To liberate a dozen J. John-
sons and Jess Willards in a ring would be a fear-
some experiment. But if a score of flyweights
were turned loose on one another it would possibly
be a joyful occasion, with no one seriously damaged
and the conqueror standing at the finish like the
lone survivor on a battlefield whence all but he
had fled. „ ,
“Trot”: To Australians it will always
seem a pity that Darcy and Mike Gibbons were never
brought together. The other day Mike had an mtei-
view with George Chip over 12 rounds. The report
of the meeting shows that Mike gave Chip a boxing
lesson, making him “miss frequently.” Chip won
two or three rounds, and was shaded in the others.
Now, as between Darcy and Chip 12 rounds would
only have been possible on a stall. Chip was cer-
tainly not in the same class as Darcy, and when
the Maitlander knocked him cold in Sydney there
was no doubt of his ability to do it several times a
day all the year through. Taking a line through
anv of the men the two met, Darcy would have hurt
Mike quite a lot —and more* quickly, too, because
Michael is a boxer and not a puncher.
* * * *
“Mat”: Stan Wickham, quoted in a
recent Bulletin argument as the finest of Rugby
kicks, made a first mark at the game with the
Ormond XV at Parramatta (N.S.W.) in the ’9o’s.
He was in knickers then. Later, he pulled on the
N.S.W. blue jersey against Queensland and Maori-
land. When he gave up football he lifted a bat for
Central Cumberland in premiership cricket. In
1910 he was runner-up to H. A. Rumball in N.S.M .
amateur billiard championship. Nowadays a more
rotund Wickham keeps his appetite in repair at
bowls. Know anybody who has done so well in so
many branches of sport?
« * * \ *
Staff-sergeant Aubrey Lock, builder of
the second of Australia’s own aeroplane-engines, is
now in charge of an ambulance-repairing outfit near
the French Front. One of the occupations of the
little plant is the hardening of steel, in which process
crushed bones and chunks of old leather are burned
together to get the desired result, and at. first there
were many inquiries from passing soldiers as to
what the prefume meant. At length Lock rigged up
a notice and nailed it at the forge-door: “Corpse-
factory,” in French and English.
Bulldog-fancier Eric Serman, of Armadale
(Vic.), whose stock has pulled off many prizes on
the bench in years gone by, lately thrust his fingers
through his thinning hair and decided that, after
all, a man is as young as he feels. Now he is some-
where in France with the A.F.C.— not in the air,
being too tough in the wishbone for that, but doing
useful work as a motor-cyclist.
Billjim’s laconics. Three of him with the
“Diamond Division” somewhere in France received a
Melbourne paper containing a picture of two youths
at a seaside picnic, headed “Two Good Sports.”
Whereupon the Billjims Three cut out from the same
journal a photograph of two soldiers in uniform, and
returned it to the sender with the written comment,
“Two Better Sports.”
* * * *
A pair of lawyers are at the head of the
third unit of the Sportsmen’s Thousand, now being
slowly and somewhat painfully gathered together in
Melbourne. Forrest Davies is one of them. He
enlisted some time ago and has hitched his war-
waggon to a pair of stars in the meantime. The
other is Lieut. Dickson, who belongs to an old Mel-
bourne family which has for years been interested
in the pursuit of Costs.
* * * *
Maesmore Alfred Morris, who nearly 30
years ago was one of the most promising cricketers
in Victoria, is dead. He played for Vic. against
Ma at Sydney in Jan., ’B9, and made 11 and 0.
Soon afterwards one of his eyes was injured, and he
played the game no more. In that last match N. S.
Wales, set 75 to win, were mown down for 63 by
Hughie Trumble (6 for 33) and Jack Worrall (4
for 19). Trumble was then only a 22-year-old.
Percy McDonnell, Jack Ferris, Tommy Horan, Jack
Edwards and Jack Barrett, five Australian cham-
pions who played in the game, preceded Morris to
the Styx. Morris’s wife, a daughter of the late
Dr. J. J. E. Wilmott, was for a time well-known on
the stage.
The shoulders behind the third N.S.W.
sportsmen’s battalion belong to Lieut. Fred W.
Fifield and Joe Lecky. Fifield is an Australian, born
at Mt. McDonald, near Cowra, and before the war
gong sounded taught young Australia that two and
two don’t make five. In spare hours he played Union
Rugby, tennis and cricket. Lecky forsook the plough
and hoe at Camden to pull on khaki. He owns a
fondness for rowing, cricket and tennis, and an
abnormal appetite for golf.
* * * *
The Lindrum-Gray billiard match at
Sydney during the past fortnight was a labored
business. Owing to light restrictions in the first
week, the afternoon sessions struggled through in
semi-darkness, and the proceedings knocked the
nerves of both players to pieces. Then the glare
of the light at night unsettled them yet more, so
that to push the affair through in time three se»-
sions per day were necessary. At half-way Lindrum
led by 730—7230 to 6500. Gray livened up in the
second week, and was only 207 behind when the
marker called “16,000.” Gray's best cues were
245 and 244, mostly off the red. Lindrum, besides
running up a fine 267 at the ninth session, and 255
on the last day, scored more than double the cen-
turies of his opponent. The match for the rubber
ought to be more cheerful. The first game, played
in Melbourne, was taken by Gray.

* * * * *
Young Clarence Power, son of Tom Power
sporting editor of S. M. Herald, had Some queer
experiences in France recently. During a lull in
the fighting he went out with a stretcher party
picking up wounded, and the first he found was
his own brother, a signaller, who had stopped some
ironmongery with his knee. Later on in the day
Clarry did something else and was presented
with the Military Medal. Since then he has been
wounded, and the latest news received about him
is that he and the brother he found are having the
times of their young lives in smoky old London, on
furlough and crutches.
* * * »
Jim Cassidy, who drew stumps at Bath-
urst (N.S.W.) recently, did something more than
wield the willow and gamble in horseflesh. He
moved off just on the 50th anniversary of his feat
of saving four lives in one day. The Macquarie River
was in flood and the big bridge on the Sydney-
Bathurst-road was carried away. Boat traffic 1 was
substituted, and one of the craft overturned while
attempting the passage. Cassidy, who was riding
along the bank at the moment, jumped into the
river, struggled ashore with one of the submerged,
swam out again three times to where the boat was
pinned against a stump and saved the entire cargo.
Queenio Ball, a miss who still wears her
hair in plaits and attends the Petersham Public
School, may be giving some of the lady sprinters a
view of her back hair before long. Since 1912
she has won every skipping and running champion-
ship under the N. S. Wales P.S.A.A.A., and has
appropriated nearly a score of open events. Her
latest success was in a scratch race at the Empire
sports at Ashfield. Queenie’s fellow students tip her
as a sure winner at the next Olympic Games
girl- are given any sort of a chance.
* * * #
John Penningtou, a storekeeper from
Bealiba, who fills the Kara Kara seat in the Vic.
Assembly in succession to Peter Mcßride, is helping
Norman' Bayles with his Totalisator Bill. Of the
10 per cent, deduction, the clubs are to get three-
fourths for prize-money and tote-upkeep and the
balance will go to the State; and the bill specifies,
in Parliamentary language, that no bets are to be
made at “tote odds” —which only goes to show what
an ingenuous young sexagenarian Bayles still is.
The second reading is to happen on the 27th inst.,
the day before prorogation.

Though it made no promises to boost up
the War Funds, Vie. Hoofball League has thus far
collected for the two semi-finals £293 more than
last year—£324 for the ’Roy-Carlton game and
£37B' for Saturday’s ’Wood-South match, an attend-
ance of 16,463 yielding this latter sum. The game
was pleasing uo to half-time, especially through the
efforts of promising youngsters; but the latter part
of the episode degenerated into a Southern rout,
’Wood ultimately winning by 95 points to 35. By
downing Carlton on the previous Saturday, Roy.
which had only squeezed into the finals by being
half-a-game better than Geelong, will have the joy
of being walloped in the final by ’Wood, which to-
day boasts about the most effective combination
ever seen in the Cabbage Garden. What the blokes
who have spent 1000 days toying with a different
sort of ball think of the thing is another matter,
and can’t be discovered just now.

Standard Rubber Co. of Selangor, Ltd.
(Sydney).—This co., now about six years
old, has just got out of the purely develop-
ment stage, and for the year ended March 31
shows a net profit of £5078. Added to £147
previously available, there was £5225 for
appropriation, which was applied this way:
To write off accumulated Sydney adminis-
tration exes., £1326; to write down capital
expenditure (including plant) £1387; to
pay cumulative prefs. 12 p.c. for one year,
£1566 (assuming the dividend was payable
for a full year on the whole of the pref.
capital shown in the balance-sheet, which is
doubtful) ; balance forward, £946. The cum.
pref. capital is now £13,057, and about 50
p.c. accumulated dividend is still payable.
“Should the price of rubber remain equal to
that of last year,” the directors say, “the
profit of the current year should be sufficient
to pay off the whole amount.” There is no
strong reason to expect any serious fall in
the price of rubber, which seems to have
about reached its level.
The co.’s estate, which is leasehold, con-
sists of 1193 acres, handy to Kuala Lumpur,
the rubber centre of the Malay Peninsula.
The planted area is 802 acres, and the co.’s
plans provide for adding 50 acres and felling
the other 300 acres of jungle in the current
year, and having the whole estate planted
by the end of 1918, the object of the rush
being to secure from the Government, which
is apparently the lessor, certain statutory
rebates. The labor difficulty doesn’t afflict the
F.M.S. cos. to the same extent as it does the
Islands planters: most of the labor force are
Tamils, with Chinese for special work, and
there is a handy supply for rush
jobs. Last year’s rubber crop was
135,6581 b., which bettered the estimate
by over 44,0001 b.: and the estimate
for the current year is 223,0001 b. Last
year’s lot realised a fraction under 2s. net,
and cost Is. 6-id., which is quite an outside
figure; the management puts it at Is. Id. for
the current year, and that is about a fair
thing for a young plantation. A profit of
lid. on the current year’s estimate would
show a profit of a shade over £lO,OOO. And
there should be a steady increase for about
10 years. The ordinary capital is £28,403,
with £13,057 pref.; total, £41,460. The
prefs.’ 12 p.c. means a big bite; but given
fair luck —no smash in the market, reason-
able freedom from disease and no destruc-
tive storms- —the co. seems now to have got
on to a good wicket. The co. has no debts
to speak of (they are more than balanced by
liquid assets), and at £44,008 the estate
stands in the books at a very reasonable
figure, considering the improvements and the
five or six years’ accumulation of charges.

There you have the effect of six o’clock
closing: a smaller net profit than in any of
the previous 19 years. Restricted trading
hours have meant decreased brewings and
reduced rentals from hotel properties; and
meantime extra taxation and manufacturing
costs are also cutting into profits. Brew-
eries and Bung are having the worry of their
lives; so in the three eastern capitals they
have combined to do they don’t know quite
what. Says the Melbourne beer-union’s
president:—
It has been found by licensed victuallers that any
arrangement that they came to as to prices was not
observed —that- there was always a certain percentage
who broke away and destroyed any scheme. The
licensed victuallers approached the brewers and asked
them to adopt a firm scheme so that the great
majority would be protected from the “cutter” and
the exploiter in the trade. The only benefit which
the brewers got out of this was that they knew their
customers were thereby making a fair profit, and
that in consequence they were dealing with solvent
people.
Also “licensed victuallers recognise that the
price of beer has to be increased.” So each
brewery customer is now invoiced at a much
higher rate than he has been paying of late,
and he secures a rebate only by making a
statutory declaration each month that he has
charged full union prices and used the
standard-sized mugs, etc. All this indicates
the present condition of the trade.
As for Fitzgerald’s, it is in a better posi-
tion than most breweries to take a jolt or
two. During the boom times it kept to its
vats in Castlemaine, and did not rush in to
purchase city pub properties at inflated
prices. Still, there have been changes in
the past 10 years:—
1907. 1915. 1916. 1917.
Capital .. . . £60,000 £60,000 £60,000 £60,000
Liabilities.. .. 7,092 6,000 3,587
Properties.. .. 23,949 48,007 49,284 48,891
Other assets . . 44,624 32,116 29,585 27,971
Tradeaccountbalauce 9,149 8,793 9,077 7,339
Expenses . . . . 2,122 2,069 3,309 2,134
In 1907 the co. had £18,949 invested in
hotels and £12,343 in mortgages; but its
faith in the pint-pot has increased its hold-
ing of pub properties to £42.591, whilst mort-
gages now total only £lOOO. Whether it
was wise to put so much spare cash in beer
emporiums depends entirely on trading re-
strictions when normal times return. On
present appearances six o’clock will be a
permanent infliction. The present dividend
is really better than it looks. The original
capital of £40.000 has in 15 years been in-
creased by two transfers of £lO,OOO from
reserves, so to old holders at par the return
is actually 12 p.c.

No notice ( except of a hostile character ) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted ; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

Britain, being hard up for tanning sub-
stances owing to the shortage of French
chestnut, has just given itself permission to
import 3000 tons of wattlebark and wattle-
bark extract monthly. This means more
juicy pickings for S’Africa, to which Aus-
tralia presented this industry, being satis-
fied itself to live on loans. For a good while
S’Africa has been shipping the crude bark*to
America, India, Russia, Japan and other
places, not to mention an ounce or two to
Australia; now factories are pushing the
extract side of the biz., which should have
been ours; too —and would have been ours if
we hadn’t been afflicted with the borrowing
curse, which is at the root of nearly all our
woes.

SERmTION OF ENERGY AND THE TRANSMISSION OF POWER
Klingtite Beits Need No Dressing
The superior friction surface of a
Klingtite Belt is the best possible dressing
a belt can have.
Combating belt slippage with patented
dressings is like fighting a typhoid epidemic
with medicines only, instead of purifying the
water and milk of the community.
Belt slippage should be corrected at the
source —the source is the belt construction.
The superior friction of Klingtite Belts
grips the pulleys and insures efficient trans-
mission.
It does more.
It insures long, continuous life for the belt.
Its thickness, its toughness, its pliability
assure you of longer service per £ s. d. of cost
than you can hope to get from any other belt.
And long life is further assured bv the
special-weave duck used by Goodyear having
a tested tensile strength 25% greater than
the best generally used in transmission belts.
Into the very fibre of this duck the
friction is forced under regulated pressure.
In this way the plies in Klingtite belt-
ing are indissolubly welded and ply separation
practically made impossible, while maximum
pliability is secured.
And pliability means greater pulley
contact and consequent greater efficiency in
transmission.
So —it is another reason for not need-
ing dressing on Klingtite Belts.
Of course dressing is cheap in itself.
But it is not cheap when you consider
that even the best dressing, although it
temporarily increases efficiency, is bound to
decrease the hie of the belt.
But the friction of Klingtite Belts
increases efficiency and increases length ol
service as well. , ",
And the quality of Goodyear Klingtite
Belts remains the same, no matter what the
price of materials and labor.
I hey are the best transmission Kelts
you can use, and a trial will prove this fact.

IN BANANA LAND
This year’s fat-bullock gamble at Bris-
bane Show prostrated all previous efforts.
The dressed beast scaled 9601 b.. and 11
guessers sized up the correct weight. Four
of the 11 were womenfolk —another broad
hint that man’s domain has lost another
acre.
Hughes becomes more and more Gilbert-
ian. At the height of the railway strike he
threatened to put Premier Ryan in gaol.
Rut as the little man hasn't a gaol of his
own he would have had to put Ryan in
Ryan’s gaol, with the key in Ryan’s pocket.
“M.F.” : Billjim’s vote on the Fossils ref-
erendum has not yet arrived, and the return
of the writs has been extended for the
second time iu the hope that the package
will have dodged the U-boats. But even if
the papers don’t turn up it is not likely that
Ryan will risk another appeal to the electors
on the subject.
“Myall”: Pastoralists in the Mitchell dis-
trict are at present having a bad time with
caterpillars. On one station 80 head of
cattle have died as a result of dining on the
worms. The pest has a red head, green
body, with a spiked tail, and is about an
inch long. They appear on the leaves of the
ironbark trees, and stay up aloft until every
leaf has been eaten. Then they make for
the ground, and at the trunk of the tree the
cattle eat them. Stock live only a few days
after the banquet. L
lenry Willis, one-time Speaker of the
N. S. Wales Assembly and now proprietor
of a quantity of brigalow scrub and a net-
ting fence in the vicinity of Roma, is the
selected Labor candidate for the Maranoa.
The Nationalists and the Farmers’ Union
are both running a candidate, thus splitting
the anti-Labor vote and making it look a
good tiling for Willis. Tom Spencer, of
Roma, who was elected on the Liberal
ticket some years ago, is the Nationalist
candidate.
The Bulletin meets a new friend on the
Inky Way —The Link, whose object it is to
get hold of Bananalanders at the Front and
at home and bind the two bundles together.
And a very good job editor Billjim (returned)
is making of it —sending it out in the form of
a narrative letter to the chaps along the
battle lines, written smartly, mostly about
the girls and what the slackers are not doing
and the small things of the family circle.
Answered the last Roll Call at Brisbane
the other day, Sergt. Fred T. Denecke, who
had been 44 years in the local military
forces, having joined up when he was 18.
llis father was a foundation member of the
Brisbane volunteer forces. When Bill began
to move across Belgium Denecke rushed into
the recruiting office, but as he couldn’t chop
a sufficient number of years off his age he
was given home-service work. He held the
long-service medal and the Queensland record
for the longest continuous service in the
same regiment.
“Ashton.” about the good old days: —
The mode of payment of aboriginals as it operated
on parts of the Culgoa is, alas, beyond recall. 1
remember a crowd of Binghis that had been scalping
and shooting on the run, and barring meagre sup-
plies of rations and opium had received nothing.
One morning the whole tribe was waiting outside the
office, and when the boss opened the door Jimmy the
spokesman stepped up on the verandah and exploded:
“Look here, Misser Mac, scalpin’ eight week now —
no money. We know how many bean make five. We
want ’im settlement!” “Yes, yes,” said the boss.
“Always willing to meet you,” and swinging a chair
he hit the spokesman over the “cobra.” Then twirl-
ing his persuader with murder in his eye he said to
the deputation: “If no other gentleman wants a
settlement I’ll bring out the rum.” The rum was
carried unanimously.
THINGS THAT MIGHT HAVE BEEN PUT DIFFERENTLY.
VISITOR: “I notice you are never idle. You always pick up your knitting when I come
to see you.”
HOSTESS: “Oh, yes, I can't hear masting time.”
Passed away on August 80, Corporal
James Watt Macgregor, of Waroolaba,
Harrisville. After 19 months with the
A.I.F. in Egypt end on Headquarters staff
in London, he was invalided home in May
last; but privations in Egypt, followed by
intense cold in England, were too much for
him. In the old days of peace he managed
Mount Morris station, Charleville, and Ter-
rick Terrick, Blackall. There’s a wife and
one son to weep for him.
The parents of Sergt. S. J. Hill, of Bris-
bane, were proud people the other day when
news came through that he had got a star.
The boy himself was prouder of a card from
General Holmes congratulating him on his
courage and devotion to duty at Messines in
June last —just before the General was
killed. Young Hill left Brisbane as a pri-
vate in June. 1915. and all his promotions
came on the field. Twenty next month.
After three years’ strenuous work as
chairman of the Brisbane district of the
A.J.A. Fred Bryan has made way for
Norman Freeburg. Some solid thanks were
passed over to Bryan the other night be-
tween sodas.
Exit Frederick William Gray, known on
the Point as Denbigh Newton. Never
married, he came to Australia from London
in 1885, appearing with Genevieve AY aid in
“Antigone” at the Melbourne Town Hall. He
played comedy with Brough and Boucieault
at the Bijou, was afterwards with a Gilbert
and Sullivan opera co., and did much tour-
ing with Grattan Riggs. Tu 1889 he settled
in Queensland as a teacher of elocution, and
later was in Brisbane city engineer s office.
The dusk was spent at Vic. Old Colonists
Homes, which he entered in Aged 76.
“B.” : Re the dangers that lurk in the sea
carriage of copra (B. 28/8/10- The Suva
Chamber of Commerce has urged the Fijian
Government to take steps to make if a penal
offence to adulterate dry coeoanut meal by
adding sand, salt water or fresh water.
Safety to a carrier depends on the article
being shipped in a perfectly dry state, and
if it is being damped to add weight by the
untutored savage, then most of the copra
fires are explicable. Incidentally, it is only
a fair tiling that the Fijian Government
should be the first to take action, because
Fijian copra is notoriously badly cured.

Salv’army performance. I saw a lassie bring
down the street with a hymn, “The Saviour
is a Bosker.”
Reported that Dr. Michaelis, German
Chancellor, has been forced by the Reichstag
to appoint a Hun bai’on as sub-editor of the
Kaiser’s speeches. Is battered Germany at
last refusing to answer its Wilhelm?
“Trench”: My experience is that the
manual worker will kick against a humane
and wisely-arranged time-saving system
where he tamely knuckles under to the bully
who is out to sweat him for his private gain.
I once took a job shovelling with a gang.
We had as pacemaker a big buck navvy of
enormous strength. The ganger walked up
and down watching every man’s shovel.
“Up to the maker’s name or out!” he roared
whenever he caught some poor devil lagging.
There was no grading of shovels such as
Taylor recommends on that job. We all
used the same size as the pacemaking giant,
and if you couldn’t fill them “up to the
maker's name” out you went.

didn’t take him down by gettini him t'
swallow an outrageous snake-yarn
that seems to have changed, judging 'k v h,
way the unions have been collared and
being used by newchums for the ml*
gigantic leg-pull poor Australia has ovll
suffered. YY hat sort of humorless fool k
man who, while comfortably emploved 'hi
himself , drops his tools at the bidding 0 f
crazy stranger and describes himself in
dreary imported song as “a wage-slave”?

From the gallery :
Temporary dislikes are hardening into lusty
enmities in the House of Reps., and Speaker Johnson
will require more vigilance to check the flow of
opprobrium. Higgs’s charge of ratting, which
caused him to be temporarily extinguished the other
day, was only a modest posy rescued from the traffic
of bouquets across the Chamber. Most of the
epithets are beyond the aural range of the Policeman
in the Chair, and the worst escape censure because
they are delivered behind a barrage of respectable
interjections. But an attentive listener can usuallv
learn enough to start a History of Polite Conversa-
tion. From the rougher sort of politician references
to members’ personal appearance are frequent, while
the more urbane fire jeers with a Chesterfieldian
grace which doubles their malignity. But none, so
far, have eclipsed Hughes’s classic: “You have a
mind like a toad in a cesspool,” which was shot at
Higgs a few days ago. This pleasantry, which sur-
passes the daintiest Baudelairean mote of Tummy
Bent, gave the Speaker a chance to show his meticu-
lous care for the strict letter of the gibe. Johnson
■solemnly made Hughes withdraw his reflection on
the mind of Bill Higgs—seemingly the rest of
William didn’t matter.
“M.P.A.”: Beaumont Smith has struck a
winner in his “Hayseed” picture. During
the week that the story of wire-whiskers
was unfolded at Brisbane Pav. the movie
house in Queen-street put up a record. The
first series of the film was made up in Adel-
aide, the second was got together in Syd-
ney. and the third has kept Smith busy in
Brisbane. The Queensland series is full of
variety, as it touches on the farm, an agri-
cultural show and a bush sports-meeting,
besides supplying street views, with good
clean comedy and a neat little love-
story. The other Sunday the pictur"
actors- took possession of a little sub-
urban station on the Pinkenba line for
“the arrival of the Governor and suite."
The entire neighborhood turned up and let
the Sunday dinner spoil while they joined in
the fun. Several well-known comedians
donned hayseed clobber for the occasion, and
Seigenberg, of Sydney, cinoed the fooling.
Sunset from Elldon Hill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
God smoothed this hill with His hand
And left on it not one tree,
And then at the changing sunset hour
God lent the hill to me.
And over its rolling summit
On a color-seeking quest
I saw the gods of Olympus
Ride out of the blazing west.
If Jason had looked on the golden fleece
That was scattered across the sky
He would have banished his Argonauts
And straightway learned to tlx ;
And away on the Sandgate reaches
And over the ti-tree swamp
A vestal maid in a pale green gown
Had lighted an orange lamp.
Low down there were shreds of purple
And delicate lemon shades;
The Twilight friar in his hooded cloak
Was blessing those Pan-filled glades:
As leapt from the tiny holdings
An earth-light here and there.
The world was a burst of Pagan song
With an undertone of prayer!

From the nature of their construc-

It caused loss of powei, made engines
noisy, made valve grinding and cylin-

But instead of unseating the valves,
carbon seals the fit of the sliding sleeves

So instead of a loss there is a gain
in power, smoothness, quietness and
flexibility.

Don’t Have Grey Hair.
A simple, old-fashioned, home-made recipe will
make the greyness disappear.
Grey hair is often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often downright injurious. Few people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily-
made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a natural color in a perfectly harmless
manner. You have only to get two ounces
of tammalite concentrate from your chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum to
prove this. Apply this simple and harmless
lotion for a few nights to the hair with a
small sponge and the greyness will gradually
disappear. The lotion is neither sticky nor
greasy, and has been proved over and over
again for generations past bv those in posses-
sion of the formula. —Apyt.

Self-Poisoning
IT is easy to get constipated. It is easy to let
yourself stay constipated. It may seem a matter
of little consequence to you.
Yet any doctor will tell you that the poisoning ol
the system by the products of constipation is one of
the most dangerous conditions to which mankind
is subjected.
Dull headaches, listlessness, sallow complexion,
intestinal troubles—all of these are signs of self*
poisoning, which weakens the system and exposes
you to the inroads of disease.
You can prevent and relieve constipation by the
use of Nujol, the natural laxative which is now
recommended by leading physicians the world
over as the safe and sure treatment for constipation.
Nujol is not absorbed by the system but acts as
an internal lubricant.

Julius Knight has been visiting his people
in Scotland, and in a letter to a Melbourne
friend announces that he is going to do his
bit of Avar work by helping to gather the
harvest. Doubtless lie will design a becoming
costume for the part. Jules says that he lias
not given up his idea of staging “A Royal
Divorce” in N’Yark; he is merely waiting
until the time seems ripe.
“Copy Boy”: To anyone with a fairly
extensive experience of police-courts there
is nothing unbelievable in Dogberry's blun-
dering stupidity; good old British Justice is
often just such an ass. I have a vivid recollec-
tion of one Vic. Jay Pay pointing to the words
Ifoni soi, etc., on the coat of arms above the
bench. “D’ye know what those there words
mean?” he demanded of the prisoner; and
proceeded to translate: “’E ’oo does wrong
'as got to suffer for it!” The unconvincing
thing is that a type so essentially English
as Dogberry should be offered as an inhabi-
tant of old Messina. But it is doubtful if
Shakesbacon ever expected us to accept the
characters in “Much Ado” as real Sicilians,
any more than (filbert Avished us to regard
those in “The Mikado” as real Japs.
Oscar Asclie’s “Chu Chiu Chow” has
broken all records at London Majesty's: it
has passed its 400th performance and is still
going strong. The house record was till now
held by Beerbohm Tree's production of
“Henry VIII.,” Avhich was done 254 times.
“Nemo”: The Poverty par. about W.
Howard Smith and his old “Maritana” pro-
gramme set me turning over my own collec-
tion. The first of “Maritana” is a pro-
gramme of April 3, 1878, when Arrnes Beau-
mont was taking a benefit at Melbourne Opera
House, and had the part of Don Caesar de
Bazan. Fanny Simonsen was the Maritana.
The second part of the bill was “La Peri-
cliole,” in which Clara Thompson was the
Perichole and her husband. Henry Bracy,
the Paquillo. In June of 1885) I saw "Mari-
tana’’ again. Beaumont was still the Don
Caesar; and once more, in April, 1880, at
the Opera House. Here’s another old one:
“Maritana.” by Dunning’s Opera Co., and
the Montague-Turner Opera Co. at the Opera
House, September, 1883. I av.us there, and
heard the plain-faced Annis Montague sing
like an angel as Maritana, and her burly
husband, Charlie Turner, do the Don.
“Fax”: Brough and Boueieault’s "Much
Ado about Nothing,” put on 25 years ago,
was a beautiful production: but there was
not quite the Shakspearean bite in it that
Oscar Asche gave to his shows. Richly
garbed and intelligent comedians filled the
parts with a somewhat modern air in most
cases. Titheradge and Mrs. Brough hardly
suggested a Benedick and Beatrice of the
16th century ; and Emma Temple, as Hero,
Avas less convincing than her tAvo attendant
gentlewomen, Emma (1 wynne and Hilda
Spong. Cecil Ward as Claudio and Percy
Lyndal (Don Pedro) gave the impression of
modern young men in masquerade, whilst
Edward Sass, as gl< xmiy Don John, had tin-
wrong turn of the tongue for blank verse.
They were graceful actors, but their style
Avas not broad enough for ancient comedy.
Brough’s Leonato and the Friar of A. It.
LaAvrenee were about the best on the serious
side, and George Anson and Cannam (first
appearance in Australia) made a satisfac-
tory Dogberry and Verges. Anson was ex-
pected to be a supremely funiiy Dogberry,
but fell short of the ideal. Dion Boucicault
stayed out of the cast to give all possible
care to the stage-management. Boucicault
stuck at nothing in the way of expenditure
on the mounting of this costly show, or in
the treatment of the lines. lie was no
cranky stickler for the original text. If a
line was object'd to as being more or less
indecent, or if the meaning seemed obscure,
or the joke was puerile and'the line could be
dispensed Avith, Boucy’s recipe Avas always
the same —“Cut it out.” His idea Avas to
make the play go AA'itli a public that would
giA’e no thought to obsolete terms and
phrases, and he did make it go -for 1—
weeks.

and made another start. The same morning

nose or a dull,
complexion drives many
to cosmetics and con-
despair. And all the time
lies at hand in the home,

a woman

a simple remedy
If you have no
need only get
ibout an ounce from your chemist and add
just sufficient water to dissolve it. A little
of this simple lotion is Nature’s own beauti-

It lasts all day or evening, renders powder-

C COSMETICS can never really help a poor
J complexion ; often they are positively
harmful. The sensible, rational way is to
actually remove the thin veil of stifling,
half-dead scarf skin from the face, and give
the fresh, vigorous and beautiful young skin
underneath a chance to show itself and to
breathe.
This is best done in a very simple way,
by merely applying mercolised wax at night,
like cold cream, and washing it off in the
morning. It absorbs the disfiguring cuticle
gradually and harmlessly, leaving a brilliant
natural complexion. Of course, this also
takes with it all such facial blemishes as
red blotches, tan, moth patches, sallowness,
liver spots, etc. As a freckle remover and
general complexion beautifier this old-
fashioned remedy is unequalled.

When the effervescence has subsided the face
is bathed with the stymol-charged water and
then dried with a towel. The offending
blackheads, of their own accord, come right

BEAUTIFUL hair adds immensely to
the personal magnetism of both men
and women. Actresses and smart women
are ever on the look-out for any harmless
thing that will increase the natural beauty
of their hair. The latest method is to use
pure stallax as a shampoo on account of
the peculiarly glossy, fluffy and wavy effect
which it leaves. As stallax has never been
used much for this purpose it comes to the
chemist only in sealed original packages,
enough for twenty-five or thirty shampoos.
A teaspoonful of the fragrant stallax gran-
ules, dissolved in a cup of hot water, is more
than sufficient for each shampoo. It is very
beneficial and stimulating to the hair, apart
from its beautifying effect.
IT is astonishing the number of women
who suffer front the humiliating afflic-
tion of superfluous hair, and I am sorry to
say that through ignorance or wrong treat-
ment the majority of them have made matters
considerably worse by causing the hairs to
grow more quickly and stronger than before.
It only requires a little thought to realise
the folly of it all. Removing the surface
growth for a few days may give temporary
relief, but in order to obtain permanent
results the hair roots must be killed. Caustic
depilatories are worse than useless, and on
no account should the hairs be pulled out.
Apart from the electric needle, which is a
costly and painful process, any chemist will
tell you that the best home treatment is
pure powdered pheminol. which has a de-
vitalising effect upon the hair roots, in addi
tion to which it removes all trace of t. e
objectionable hair in a few seconds, and the
recommended treatment _ gradually, but
surely, dissolves out the hair follicles, which
is the only method of permanently ridding
oneself of this terrible disfigurement. Lb
The Inky Way.
“87”: Yes, the German 'is a thorough
brute. When Abdul evacuated El Arish he
left behind thousands of copies of a German
newspaper containing large-type headings
such as “20,000 British captured on Western
Front” ; “Russians Retiring” ; “Big Strike in
Australia,” all obviously prepared and
printed specially for the consumption of
Thomas and Billjim. An enemy who attends
to details like that commands respect.
* ’ * * *
“E.5.8.T.”: “Keep it out of the papers” is
the appeal of nine out of 10 Australian
soldiei’s who have done anything. In writing
home the request is made by practically all.
“It looks rotten when it’s read over here,” is
the common excuse. The least bit of skite
and a fellow gets nothing, I promise you.
A man looks such as ass when one of the
fellows props himself up in a crowd of us
and coldly reads an Australian paragraph
bragging about something he has pulled off.
The chances are nine out of 10 in the crowd
have done something better that hasn’t been
named. In fact, there’s a growing feeling
among the lads to edge decorations away on
the inside of a tunic. “I’m not going to do
anything to make anybody believe I’m better
than ten thousand better men who’ve got
nothing,” said one decorated boy the other
day. Great cobbers these Australians —and
I say it though I’m one of them myself.
* * * *
“Korimako” : Bill Lane, just dead, was the
eighth editor of Auckland (M.L.) Herald.
The first, David Burn, began to instruct the
democracy in November, 1863, wheix the
paper was founded by his partner, W. C.
Wilson. Burn’s health gave way, and F. J.
von Stunner succeeded him. Next was R. J.
Creighton, who later on took up press work
in ’Frisco. Then came James Bi-own, and
he was succeeded in 1875 by William Berry,
who carried on for 28 years. Associated
with Beri’y for about 18 months, after the
amalgamation of the Herald and Southern
Cross, was D. M. Luckie, who resigned to
join the M.L. Life Insurance Department.
When Berry died in 1903, W. S. Douglas,
sub-editor, became editor, and on his death
four years ago Lane took control. All these
men, I think, have passed West. Berry’s
exit was the most dramatic. One genial
October afternoon, having written an article,
he put his pen away saying “I’ve finished.”
An hour or so later he was dead.

“Constant Feeder”: I couldn’t see through
the Russian darkness until Sydney D.T.
fired this star shell in its August 31 leader:
By next Spring, as far as Russia is concerned,
things may be better or they may be worse. As far
as can be seen from this distance anything may
happen. And the most expectable thing is perhaps
the unexpected thing.
Now if one thing is clearer than any other
thing it is that everything is as clear as
anything.
“Quarter Single”: At Canterbury (Vic.)
there is an old Mrs. A. Wheaton, whose
father was James Cook —a collateral
descendant of Captain Cook, of the
Endeavor. In 1852 James and his two
brothers left Yorkshire for Australia, bring-
ing with them a small printing-press and
some knowledge of the trade. As the sailors
deserted as soon as the vessel reached Sand-
ridge, the Cooks —there were not too many
of them to spoil the broth —had to beach
their own goods, which they pulled in a dray
to the shed in Collins-street where they
were to sleep. James bought a Clydesdale
draught-horse at Keilor for £l5O, and the
trio set sail for Bendigo. Business boomed,
and when the father came out later he
brought a larger Howe press. William
Henry Cook took William Sherbon into
partnership, the other two brothers with-
drawing. The partners founded The Ben-
digo Advertiser and Sandhurst Commer-
cial Circular, the first copy of which bears
this imprint:—
Printed by Win. Hy. Cook and Wm. Sherbon, at
their Premises, Bendigo Flat, Bendigo Gold Field;
and Published bv R. R. Haverfield, at his Premises,
View Point, all being in the township of Sandhurst,
on Friday, Dec. 9, 1853.
William Henry Cook afterwards died while
rounding the Horn on his return to Eng-
land ; his widow married Sir W. Leng, of
the Sheffield Telegraph. Cook saw the
Bendigo Advertiser through the Crimean
War; Alfred May, as printer of the Argus,
saw that paper through the Franco-Prussian
War; his son married Mrs. Wheaton’s
daughter, and as printer of Melbourne
Herald is arranging that paper’s blasti-
ferous headings about the Kaiser. Old Bob
Haverfield was still connected with the Adver-
tiser as proof-reader up to 30 years ago;
and Tom lvelynaek, of the Melbourne
Herald, got his first job in a newspaper
office “holding copy” for the old man.
* * * *
Peake, who spoke against the Premier’s
Department when it was started by the
Vaughan Government in S. . Aus., has
promptly abolished it as an unnecessary ex-
pense. 'The secretary, Sincock, will go on
with his old duties as head of the Govern-
ment Reporting Department and secretary to
the Railways Standing Committee. bred
Johns remains leader of Hansard in the
Assembly, and Rodney Cockburn in the Coun-
cil ill three were lifted out of the Adel-
aide panel-- when an official Hansard staff
was organised.
THE PAPER FAMINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The tradesman at the poet’s door
Was red with righteous ire.
A thumping- sheaf of bills he bore.
Said he: “I’ve 1 served it oft before.
This sort of thing would tire
The patience of old Job. Are we
To l>ill you to eternity ?”
The poet on the tradesman smiled.
“Why should you mind,” said he,
“If 1 do not? I am not wild.
Indeed, I am quite reconciled.
Send in your bills to me.
I find them excellent anon
To rough-cast little poems on.”
VICTORIA.

health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.

keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes. crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

The Fairy Ring.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
> r piS a strange little smile you are wear-
| ing, my girl—
Oh, did ever a mouth know so weary
a droop !
Is it Old Mother Grief who’s unwinding a
curl,
And your bonny brave heart that is caught
in the loop?
So settle your bodice and wrinkle your lace,
And hold up a little white ear, for I bring
A whimsical tale of the wonderful place
Where the fairies danced in a faery ring.
For I stared the inscrutable face of the Dark
On the curve of the hill where the gold stars
shine,
And watched while each grey dog’s grufforous
bark
Droned to a growl and a wheening whine;
And I watched while the city lights ebbed
from sight—
How the frail little lights to the Dark will
cling,
Like sparkles of dew on the gown o’ the
Night:
And I watched for the dance o’ the faery
ring.
Then, toes to the stalk and heels to the grass
And little white hands on the clover-shoots,
I watched the fairies pass and pass
With a tappetty-tapping of tiny boots ;
Round and round, with a song and a jest.
Round and round till my slow mouth smiled,
And a quizzical fairy stole into my breast
And changed my heart to the heart of a
child.
And then in the faery ranks I saw
Dignified aunts in their stately silk,
The round, ruddy face of the boy next door,
The loud-lunged fellow who brought the
milk ;
The cook and the baker; the lean, sleek cat;
The pig-tailed maid with her spoon and
plate,
And the fairy king with the tall, tall hat
Who lifted me up at the garden gate ;
The queen of the fairies who sat apart
On a thin low chair by the slim quick fire,
Who gathered me close to her jocund heart
And kissed and kissed to her heart’s desire ;
The well-worn bag with its wealth of toys,
And a blithe little fellow who laughed with
glee,
A drawn blind shielding a room from noise,
And the men from the city who came to tea.
And drowsily dreaming upon the rug
By the fairy caves in the sputtering grate,
I felt on my shoulder the good-night hug
When the long day ended at half-past eight;
For the queen of the fairies, with nut-brown
hair,
Carried me up, with her dainty feet
Softly tapping upon the stair,
To a quaint little cot with its star-white
sheet.
And the grey rat knocked, the loose board
creaked,
The drawn blind rapped on the window-pane ;
A tap in a bathroom hissed and leaked
And dripped and dripped like warm, slow
rain ;
And wonderful ladies from fairy-lore
Hovered and smiled o’er the pillow-slip,
Till sleep came softly across the floor
And hid the whole world with her finger-tip.
Over the sea and over the hills
Clambered the sun to my daisied place;
Firing the dew on the window-sills,
Stroking its fingers across my face ;
And the wide sky wakened upon my sight,
And the town-mist lifted and swayed and
curled,
And I found the fairies had followed the
night
And left me alone ’mid a grown-up world.
They were fairykins all ; but the dim night
brings
The whimsical joys that the flesh outgrows,
And the well-tuned heart feels tenderer
things
As the fragrance wells from the soul of a
rose.
Little wan girl, on the curve of your heart
Let the fairies dance in a faery ring,
And a merry, bright song from your throat
will start
Of a Fairy Queen and a Fairy King!
MAORTT.A XT).
BOYCE BOWDEN.
A Matter of Duty.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GASSON —cunning, vindictive
Gasson —was at large once more.
The news of his discharge from
the Launceston Gaol flashed up and
down the coast with the almost in-
credible rapidity such ill news travels.
To the majority of the people the man
was no more than a name —an evil
name, culled from the columns of the news-
paper. But there were others, settlers
along the South Down plains, to whom the
news of “Big” Gasson’s unexpected release
came as a blow in the face. Such was the
sinister record of this man that the coast
folk became seized with a kind of mental
panic at the bare thought of his reappear-
ance amongst them.
Of those affected, police-trooper South,
stationed on the Constantin River, had most
reason to feel uncomfortable. It was he
who had finally run Gasson to earth in the
heart of the swamps, where he had taken
refuge after having almost battered to death
the old hutkeeper on the Fisli Bay coast-
run. It was South’s cleverly-given evidence
that had come more nearly than anything
else to hanging “Big” Gasson as high as
Tlaman. It had been touchy and go then.
But a flaw in the prosecution had been
seized on by the smart mainland lawyer,
retained, it was said, by some rich connec-
tions of the prisoner who were unwilling to
iisk the stigma of capital punishment, and
nve years for attempted murder had been the
p e Punishment exacted. The law pro-
essed to be satisfied, even if justice was
not. And now they had turned him loose
agiun with 12 months of his sentence re-
mitted.
1 rooper South was posting his mail in
tUly mail-box at the Cripple Creek Post
' . e when the news came through on the
wires. His hand, outstretched with the
letters, remained rigid for the length of time
that, it took the postmaster to call the tid-
ings ; then he quietly dropped his mail into
the box and felt unemotionally in the pocket
of his tunic for the blackened pipe that
shared his perplexed moments.
I he postmaster watched rather curiously.
He had been in the district many years, and
knew the ins and out of the Caisson busi-
ness almost as well as did the slim-built
policeman before him who had caught his
man single-handed. Mingled with the post-
master s curiosity was genuine concern. The
tone of his voice betrayed it.
Of course, if I hear anything of his
movements I’ll let you know at once, Jim.
Beats me what they let him out for, with
that much of his sentence still to run.
Maybe, he’s reformed; they do say a spell
of hard labor works wonders. I shouldn’t
worry about it, if 1 were you. lie’s more
than likely forgot his grouch by now.”
“Animals like him don’t forget.” The
trooper’s forehead was puckered in a frown.
‘You weren’t there to hear the snarl in his
voice when he threatened me—l was. It
was when they’d given his sentence out. I
was standing at the back of the court, and
he looks over at me with that cold-blooded
smile of his, and says he: ‘May God strike
me dead,’ he says, ‘if I don’t even up with
that —■— policeman of yours when I get
out again.’ You should have heard the wav
the Judge roared at him. No; he’ll make
this way right enough. Well, let him come ;
I’m not afraid. I got him once, and I
reckon I can do it again if he makes
trouble.”
“AVell, watch out,” advised the post-
master, turning into the office at the call
of the telephone. “If he comes at you at
all, it will be when you ain’t ready—you
can bank on that.”
“They’ll keep a good eye on him at head-
quarters,” called South. “All the same, if
you hear anything you can let me know.
Letting him go like this is one on me. I
ain’t scared, though.”
He unhitched his horse from the fence
and rode away in something of a hurry. In
spite of his previous disclaimer, it was evi-
dent that he was a trifle uneasy. Reaching
home he returned his wife’s greeting ab-
stractedly, eating his dinner in thoughtful
silence. Afterwards, he reached down his
revolver from where it hung on the wall of
the chimney and commenced to clean it.
Ilis wife, coming in to clear away the
dishes, found him spinning the barrel of the
weapon with his forefinger, with a little
satisfied smile on his face at the smooth
running of it.
“What’s the matter, .Tim? What d’you
want to get cleaning that up for? Anything
the matter?”
“Nothing out of the way. There’s a bit
of a character got his discharge from Laun-
ceston to-day, and maybe he’ll find his way
into these parts. It’s as well to be ready,
that’s all.”
She eyed him in quick suspicion.
“You’re not telling me all you know.
There’s something behind. Was—was he
an enemy of yours? Are you expecting him
to make trouble?”
South laughed reassuringly, tossing the
weapon to the table while he drew the girl
on to his knee.
“It’s quite all right, little woman; you
don’t need to worry.- It’s only some tough
I had trouble with a few years ago, before
you and I met. They gave him five years,
when they should have hanged him. He
blamed me for getting sent up. Now
they’ve let him out, he might try to stir up
trouble. But I don’t see what he can do. I
did my duty, and I don’t regret it. Some-
one had to get him.”
She shivered a little.
“I know; but he —that sort of a man
mightn’t understand. I think I’d sleep
easier if you were out of the police. You
can’t go straight without making enemies.
I don’t like it.”
“Some folk would get a set on you no
matter what you were at,” said the trooper,
gravely. “No ; I reckon you wouldn’t want
your husband to crawl down for fear of
getting himself hurt. Don’t worry. If
Gasson comes-this way, I’ll get plenty of
warning. Someone’s bound to see him.”
But nobody did. From the day of his
discharge the man seemed to vanish utterly.
For all the police knew of his whereabouts
he might not have existed. His disappear-
ance was complete.
As time passed and nothing was heard of
him the coast folk began to breathe more
freely. Even Trooper South was lulled into
a sense of security which his knowledge of
Gasson should never have permitted. He
began to relax, his vigilance, and his gun
filled its accustomed place on the chimney
wall.
“Gasson’s had sense enough to get out
of the country,” he told his friend the post-
master one day. “Ten to one he’s clean
forgot about me, and I doubt if we’ll hear
any more of him.”
“Well, maybe,” acceded the official,
doubtfully. “I’d keep my eye skinned awhile
yet though, if I were you. This is a queer 1
country, and there’s some queer folk in it.”
“But Gasson’s out of it,” said South ; and
went his way laughing.
Came a day in midsummer when the
police-sergeant in charge at Sandy Bay tele-
phoned his men to beat the coast-runs for
trappers. It was close season, but skins
were getting through to town. It was up
to each man to clean up bis district.
Trooper South made ready for an early
start. He had three hacks in the police
paddock, but he chose to ride a colt of his
own, a fiery little bit of horseflesh, just
rising three and hacked scarce a fortnight.
South reckoned a trip over the button-grass

and through the sandhills would give the
colt something better to think about than
running round the yard knocking the rails
down.
It was early dawn of a warm, still morn-
ing when lie manoeuvred himself into the
saddle and finally got the colt going along
the heathland. The brute bored unmerci-
fully, and the trooper’s wrists were quickly
aching. He had to watch himself. Twice
the colt suddenly propped and nearly un-
seated him. Then came catastrophe.
They were topping a knoll-ridge and the
colt jibbed. South, whose patience was
about exhausted, brought his spurs into
his mount’s ribs. The animal went at
it with a sudden rush, swerved past a
clump of tea-tree, caught his near forefoot
in a curly gum-root, and sent South flying-
out of the saddle to land on his back in a
sand-drift. When he could sit up and scrape
the rubbish out of his mouth and eyes the
colt was a quarter of a mile away.
The trooper got up swearing. Beyond a
good shaking he was unhurt, although his
mouth was bleeding where a bit of drift-
wood had jabbed it. It was the prospect of
a five-mile walk back home, with his day’s
work still to do, that worried him. He had
no fear for the colt. It would make straight
home, unless the trailing reins caught some-
how. He had known of a horse starved to
death that way.
He unbuttoned his tunic at the throat and
commenced to climb the side of the nearest
knoll. From the top of it the surrounding
country would be clear to see and, by keep-
ing to the ridges, he might cut the distance
a trifle.
The side of the knoll went almost straight
up into the air. By the time he was three-
quarters of the way up he was about winded.
The ground where he found himself now
ran level for a yard or two, forming a kind
of rough shelf across the face of the cliff.
On the far side, close to the next tier of
ground, was, a pile of rocks. By climbing
on this, South reckoned he might make a
jump for the crest.
It proved easier than he had thought. A
moment’s pause on the topmost boulder,
settling his body for the spring; then he
launched himself for the ridge five feet away,
caught at a tussock of silver-grass, hung a
moment, then drew himself up and rolled
into a hollow.
When he looked up it was to see the bar-
rel of a shot-gun within six inches of his
nose, and behind it the sullen face and evil,
squinting eyes of “Big” Gasson.
For the fraction of a minute the men
stared at each other I —South, dismayed and
apprehensive; the other, eager, triumphant,
vindictive.
Then Gasson withdrew the point of his
gun backwards till he stood erect a yard away
and spat out a single sentence.
“Got you—at last.”
The man was literally a scarecrow.
Weeks of burrowing through the bush in
order to reach his objective unseen and un-
hindered, lest his victim should be warned,
had reduced his clothing to rags. He looked
ill-nourished and ill-humored.
Trooper South met his scowl squarely.
Outwardly he was unconcerned; inwardly
he cursed the criminal folly that had sent
him out unarmed and unthinking of what
the day might bring forth. He moistened
his lips in an effort to speak, but Ins mouth
was swollen and still bleeding, and the words
came with difficulty.
“What’s the joke, ‘Big’? Better put that
gun of yours away, hadn’t you? It’s right
in a line with my stomach, and I don't fancy
it going off.” . , T ,
Gasson laughed without humor. tie
thought South’s halting speech was due to
fright, and the belief gave him added plea-
sure.
“Yes; it’ll go off all right—when I m
ready. When it does, you’ll forget to men-
tion* it. Man, I’ve lain awake at nights
praying for this. I knew I’d square with
you one day. That’s why I took my dose
lying down, and fooled them into thinking
the black sheep had turned color, till they
felt sorry for me and turned me loose. It
they’ve a sense of humor, they ought to
appreciate this when they hear of it. But
they won’t if I can help it.”
“Don’t be a fool,” said South painfully.
“Look here, Gasson, you’re an educated man
whatever else you are. You can shoot me
if you like, but if you do they’ll hang you—
as sure as God made little apples. And I
fancy you’re too smart to run the risk of
that. Cut the old trouble out of your life,
and start again. I'm willing to forget
this.”
“It’s a bit too late for you to learn.” said
Gasson, setting his finger to the trigger of
the gun, “but the knowledge may come use-
ful to you even in Hell. ‘Never forget and
never forgive.’ The Devil himself couldn’t
have a better motto than that, and it’s not
for me to turn my nose up at mv betters. If
you know a prayer you might as well say
it.”
“I only did my duty,” said South, with
paling face. “You speak as if the matter
were personal; as God hears me, it was not.
I should have done what I did if you had
been my dearest friend. It was a matter
of duty. You’re not thinking to murder me
for that?”
Gasson burst out into a sudden rage.
“I know what you police are ! When it’s
a matter of mauling a man and hunting him
down like a wild beast, you brag about duty.
Duty be damned ! It’s a catchword to ease
your conscience. Did you never stop to
think it’s your duty to be decent sometimes?
What are‘you staring at? You can’t trick
me that way. There’s nothing behind me,
and you know it”
But he was wrong. South, whose despair-
ing glance had momentarily gone past the
threatening gun, saw a black, shining coil
raise itself from a crevice of the rocks, with
flattened head drawn back to strike. From
between his swollen lips issued a hoarse croak
of warning, fcut he was too late. There
came a swift streak of color across the grey
dulness of the rocks, a sharp cry of fear as
the snake struck, the sudden frenzied kick
that dislodged it from Gasson’s ankle, and
the thunderous report of the shot that blew
the reptile into a spatter of blood and slime
against the side of the cliff.
The next moment the trooper had sprung
on Gasson and felled him with a blow be-
hind the ear. The man went down like a
pole-axed bullock. South caught up the
gun and heaved it into the gully below.
He turned to find Gasson sitting up clutch-
bit!" The bewildered
wretch could only repeat over and over
again, “I’m bit, 1 tell you—my God, Im
hit.” In the sudden turning of the tables
he was as helpless as a child.
South went down on one knee and turned
back the ragged trouser-leg. Th e surface ot
the wound was smeared with a little blood,
lie wiped it away, unhindered by Gasson.
who looked on in a kind of stupor, i he
trooper raised shining eyes tr< in his exam
ination of the bite.
“Rouse yourself up, man, and help get a
ligature on your leg. Hurry now:; every
minute counts.”
Big Gasson snarled hack at him in a kind
of frenzy. “Ligature? What good’s a liga-
ture? We’re twenty miles from a doctor,
ain’t we? I know when I’m done. Take
your damned face away, and let me die in
peace.” . . „
“Nonsense; you re not going to die.
South was busy while he spoke. “Sit steady
now while I tighten this twitch. It 11 hurt
you, but that can't be helped. Now I’m
going to suck the bite.'
“You? Man. you can’t; your mouth’s all
bleeding and you’d get the poison yourself.’
Gasson stared inereduously. “Besides, what
do you care? It’s an easy way out for you,
if you only knew it. I was going to kill
you a minute ago, but it looks like it was
your turn.”
“Quiet now till I cut round the bite....
so.” South raised his eyes and looked the
other man full in the face. “If you never
knew it before, you’re going to find that a
policeman’s duty cuts both ways. I know
my job and I’m going to do it. I’ll take the
risk of poisoning my mouth. That’s one of
the risks I’m paid to take. Still now.”
Gasson’s face was a study in conflicting
emotions. Gradually, as he watched the
bent head of this enemy ministering to his
need, his savage expression relaxed and gave
place to one of bewildered admiration.
At the end of five minutes South suddenly
desisted.
“My mouth’s that sore I can’t grip with it
any longer,” he explained. “I reckon you’re
all right now, anyway. How do you feel?”
“I’m a bit giddy, and my leg’s in Hell —
but maybe that’s the bandage,” said Gas-
son. “If —if I come through all right, we’ll
cry quits. You're a man’s man, South.”
“Don’t let it worry you,” said the trooper
confusedly. “It’s —it’s all in the day’s work.
I’ve been glad to do it. See how it feels
like to try to walk, will you? If I loosen
the ligature it’ll ease the pain.”
Came mid day and Gasson found him-
self well on his way to the doctor at Sandy
Bay. He said never a word at parting, but
South found complete satisfaction in the way
his hand was gripped. Unless he was much
mistaken t there would be no more trouble
South pushed his way through the little
knot of wide-eyed folk that stood watchim,
the departure of the subdued law-breaker*
and ran into his friend the postmaster

like that? Man, you’d be a kind of hero if
the re wasn’t a doubt of your sanity!”
“The kind of hero you read about in the
‘Bluebell Blossom’ series on the bookstalls”
gaid South . “Listen while.l tell you some-
thing; only, for the Lord’s sake, keen it to
yourself. That snake that bit Gasson wasn’t
poisonous. How do I know? I didn’t
exactly know, I took a chance at it. There
were four punctures on Gasson’s leg; and
more’n two don’t count, the doctors say. I
might have been wrong, but I was willing to
take the chance. I had to make friends
w ith Gasson. or he’d have come for me again
x’m a bit ashamed of the trick, but I reckon
Posty, it’s betteY to be ashamed than dead
any day. So long.”
BERNARD CRONIN.
Fortune.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dame Fortune’s a jade with a fanciful horn
Of silver ambitions she warms into flame;
With pearls for the princes and tears night
and morn
For poor little poets who fluttered for fame.
Who smile when she sings as she dances
along :
“Come woo me with courage and delicate
song.'’
I followed her once, hut she wearied me
soon.
All careless was I of her roseate quest.
I built a dream house, while the stars were
in tune,
And slipped into silence and exquisite rest;
But she, like her sex, when my passion
seemed cold,
Ran hither and offered me all of her gold.
I went to the door, and I looked at her ware
Of agate and amber and cool crysolite;
I shook my wise head with a holiday air.
And bade her good-day in a daring delight.
For I am a fool, and my fortune is made;
I care not a fig for a crown or a spade;
I dwell with the elves, ’neath the odorous
sky ;
The dews of the dawn brush my gables with
glee;
And moonlights and sunlights and lovers
pass by
All humming this song as they peep upon
me:
“Heigh-ho! for the fool, who can pity all
pelf,
And finds in his bliss that his fortune’s
himself.”
